<b>Preface</b><br>
<b>This book, Physical Chemistry for Advanced Level Secondary Schools, is written</b><br>
specifically for Form Five and Six  students in the United Republic of Tanzania.<br>
<b>The book is prepared according to the 2009 Chemistry Syllabus for Advanced</b><br>
<b>Secondary Education, Form V – VI, issued by the Ministry of Education and</b><br>
Vocational Training.<br>
<b>The book consists of nine chapters, which are: Gases, Relative molecular masses</b><br>
in solutions, Two component liquid systems, Energetics, Chemical equilibrium,<br>
<b>Chemical kinetics, Electrochemistry, Acids, bases and salts, and Solubility and</b><br>
solubility products. Each chapter comprises illustrations, exercises, revision<br>
questions, and some of practical work. Answers to numerical questions are<br>
provided at the end of the book. Learners are encouraged to do all the practical<br>
work and answer all the questions. This will enhance their understanding, and<br>
promote the acquisition of the intended skills and competencies for this level.<br>
<b>Tanzania Institute of Education</b><br>

1<br>
<b>Gases</b><br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
<b>Many reactions performed in the laboratory or industries involve gases as</b><br>
reactants or products. This means that a chemist must be well equipped with the<br>
knowledge of gases so as to successfully handle such reactions. The knowledge<br>
of gases is also important considering its wide application in manufacturing<br>
industries, transport, medicine, agriculture, and in home appliances.  In this<br>
chapter, you will learn about the parameters of a gas, gas laws, the kinetic theory<br>
of gases, and the relative molecular masses of gases.<br>
1.1<br>
<b>Gas parameters</b><br>
Despite their wide differences in chemical properties, all gases more or less obey<br>
the gas laws. These laws show the relationship between macroscopic physical<br>
properties of gases such as volume, pressure, temperature, and number of moles<br>
of a gas.<br>
(a)<br>
The volume, V – Gases have no definite volume as they expand to fill<br>
the volume of a container. Therefore, the volume of a gas is equal to the<br>
volume of a container occupied by the gas. The SI unit for the volume is<br>
cubic metre (m3) or cubic decimetre (dm3) or cubic centimetre (cm3).<br>
<b>The volume of a gas can also be expressed in units of litres (L) or millilitres</b><br>
(mL).<br>
1dm3 = 1L<br>
1L = 1000 mL<br>
1L = 1000 cm3<br>
1m3 = 1000 dm3<br>
(b)<br>
Pressure, P – The pressure of a gas is the force exerted by the impacts of its<br>
molecules per unit area of the wall of the container.<br>
<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>One</b><br>
<b>Gases</b><br>

2<br>
<b>The pressure of a gas in a container is measured by using an instrument</b><br>
called mercury manometer. The pressure for gases in the atmosphere is<br>
measured using mercury barometer. The mercury manometer and the<br>
mercury barometer for measuring gas pressure and atmospheric pressure<br>
are shown in Figures 1.1 and 1.2, respectively.<br>
<b>In mercury manometer, the gas</b><br>
container is connected to a U-tube<br>
containing mercury with vacuum at<br>
the top end. When the cock is opened,<br>
the gas flows from the container and<br>
pushes the mercury upward. The gas<br>
pressure is given by the difference in<br>
the heights of the mercury columns in<br>
the two arms of the manometer.<br>
<b>In mercury barometer, the long tube (80 cm) filled with mercury is inverted</b><br>
into a dish containing mercury. The atmospheric pressure is given by the<br>
height of mercury in the column, which is equal to 760 mm at sea level. The<br>
unit of pressure in this method is the<br>
millimetre of mercury (mmHg) or torr,<br>
that is, 1 atm = 760 mmHg = 760 torr.<br>
<b>However, because pressure is defined</b><br>
as force per unit area, the basic units<br>
of pressure are derived from the units<br>
of force (Newton, N) divided by the<br>
units of area (m2). Thus, the SI unit of<br>
pressure is Newton per square metre<br>
(N/m2) which is also called Pascal<br>
(Pa). The relationship among the four<br>
units of pressure is:<br>
5<br>
1 atm<br>
760 mmHg<br>
760 torr<br>
<b>1.013  10 Pa.</b><br>
=<br>

=<br>
´<br>
Note: Based on the health issues associated with the use of mercury,<br>
mercury containing instruments are being phased out.<br>
(c)<br>
Temperature, T – The temperature of a gas is measured in Celsius degree<br>
or centigrade degree (°C). The SI unit of temperature is the Kelvin (K).<br>

3<br>
<b>The Kelvin temperature (or absolute temperature) of a gas can be obtained</b><br>
using the following expression:<br>
<b>T (K) = T (°C) + 273</b><br>
Note that the degree (°) sign is not used with the Kelvin unit (K).<br>
(d)<br>
The number of moles of a gas, n – The number of moles of a gas in a container<br>
is equal to the mass of the gas (m) divided by its molecular mass (M).<br>
mass of a gas<br>

1.2<br>
<b>The gas laws</b><br>
<b>The parameters which are needed to characterise the gas are inter-related. Any</b><br>
change in one parameter causes the change in one of the other parameters. In<br>
this section, you will learn about the fundamental gas laws which describe<br>
the relationships among pressure, temperature, volume, and amounts of gas<br>
molecules (moles). These laws include the Boyle’s Law, the Charles’ Law, the<br>
<b>Avogadro’s Law and the ideal gas law. Other gas laws such as Dalton’s Law</b><br>
of partial pressure, Graham’s Law of diffusion and effusion, are also included.<br>
You will also learn to use the general gas equation derived from the gas laws to<br>
calculate the volume, pressure, and amount of gases as well as to determine the<br>
density and molecular masses of gases.<br>
1.2.1	 Boyle’s Law<br>
<b>The Boyle’s Law was named after an Anglo-Irish philosopher and scientist Robert</b><br>
Boyle (1627 – 1691) who published the law in 1662. The law states that at constant<br>
temperature, the volume of a fixed mass of a gas is inversely proportional to its<br>
pressure. That means, if pressure increases, the volume decreases. In this law, two<br>
parameters are kept constant. These are temperature and the  number of moles.<br>
The change in the volume of a gas with pressure is illustrated in Figure 1.3.<br>
Figure 1.3 The change in volume of a gas with pressure at constant temperature and<br>
number of moles<br>
<b>Pressure, P1</b><br>
<b>Volume, V1</b><br>
(T and n constant)<br>
<b>Volume, 0.5 V1</b><br>
(T and n constant)<br>
<b>Volume, 0.25 V1</b><br>
(T and n constant)<br>

1.2.2	 Charles’ Law<br>
<b>The Charles' Law was named after a French scientist Jacques Alexander Cesar</b><br>
<b>Charles (1746 –1823).  The law was published in 1802 by Joseph Louis Lussac</b><br>
who credited the unpublished work to Jacques Charles, and named the law in<br>
his honour. Charles’ Law states that at constant pressure, the volume of a fixed<br>
mass of a gas is directly<br>
proportional<br>
to<br>
the<br>
absolute temperature. That<br>
means,<br>
if<br>
temperature<br>
increases<br>
at<br>
constant<br>
pressure the volume also<br>
increases.<br>
<b>In this law, two parameters</b><br>
are kept constant: pressure<br>
(P) and the number of<br>
moles (n). The change<br>
in volume of a gas with<br>
temperature is shown in<br>
Figure 1.6.<br>
<b>Figure 1.6 The change in volume of a gas with</b><br>
temperature at constant pressure and number of moles<br>

<b>The important feature of the graph in Figure 1.7 is that when the graph is</b><br>
extrapolated, the temperature at the point where the volume is zero is obtained.<br>
<b>This is true for all gases which obey the Charles' Law. The temperature where</b><br>
all the extrapolated lines meet (–273 ºC or 0 K on the Kelvin temperature scale)<br>
is the absolute zero temperature and is given by the equation; K = θ ºC + 273,<br>
where θ ºC is the temperature in degree centigrade. Below absolute zero, the<br>
extrapolated volume of a gas would be negative which is never attained as there<br>
is no gas with negative volume.<br>

1.2.3	 Combined gas law<br>
According to the Boyle’s Law, pressure and volume of a gas are inversely related;<br>
and according to the Charles’ Law, the absolute temperature and volume of a<br>
gas are directly related. These two relationships can be combined into a single<br>
equation known as the combined gas law.<br>
Boyle’s Law;<br>
(T, n constant)<br>

<b>P</b><br>
<b>The combined gas law can be stated as for a fixed mass of a gas, the volume is</b><br>
directly proportional to the absolute temperature and inversely proportional to<br>
the pressure.<br>

1.2.4	 Avogadro’s Law<br>
<b>Avogadro’s Law was stated in 1811 by an Italian Chemist Amedeo Avogadro</b><br>
(1776–1856). The law states that equal volumes of gases at the same temperature<br>
and pressure contain equal number of particles. That is, at constant temperature<br>

<b>The molar gas volume</b><br>
<b>The molar gas volume is the volume occupied by one mole of any gas at a given</b><br>
temperature (T) and pressure (P). This volume is also known as molar volume. To<br>
be able to compare the molar volume of gases, a fixed reference temperature and<br>
pressure called standard temperature and pressure (STP) is used. The standard<br>
temperature and pressure are 273 K (0 °C) and 1 atm (760 mmHg), respectively.<br>
At STP, one mole of any gas occupies a volume of 22.4 litres.<br>
<b>Relative molecular mass of a gas and relative vapour density</b><br>
The relative vapour density (RVD) of a gas is the ratio of the mass of a certain<br>
volume of the gas to the mass of the same volume of hydrogen gas at the same<br>
temperature and pressure.<br>
<b>Mass of volume ‛V’ of the gas</b><br>
<b>Mass of volume ‛V’ of hydrogen gas</b><br>
<b>Relative vapour density (RVD) =</b><br>
<b>If V (mL) of any gas contains n molecules (Avogadro’s Law), then</b><br>
massof n moleculesof gaseoussubstance<br>
<b>RVD</b><br>
massof n moleculesof hydrogen<br>
=<br>
massof 1moleculeof gaseoussubstance<br>
<b>RVD</b><br>
massof 1moleculeof hydrogen<br>
=<br>

2 massof 1atomof hydrogen<br>
=<br>
mass of 1 molecule of the gas<br>
mass of 1 atom of hydrogen gas<br>

<b>By definition, mass of 1 atom of hydrogen = 1</b><br>
12th the mass of 1 atom of carbon–12<br>
mass of 1 molecule of the gas<br>

1.2.5	 The ideal gas equation<br>
<b>The behaviour of any gas can be described by considering four variables</b><br>
(temperature, pressure, volume and number of moles) whose relationships are<br>
studied separately by three laws; Boyle’s, Charles’ and Avogadro’s Laws. A<br>
combination of these laws gives an equation known as ideal gas equation which<br>
indicates how the four variables are related to each other.<br>
<b>Consider the expression for each gas law:</b><br>
Boyle’s Law;<br>
(T, n constant)<br>
Charles’ Law;<br>
(P, n constant)<br>
Avogadro’s Law;<br>
(P and T constant)<br>
1<br>
<b>V</b><br>
<b>P</b><br>
<b>V</b><br>
<b>T</b><br>
<b>V</b><br>
n<br>
<b>Combining the three gas laws gives Equation 1.9</b><br>
nT<br>
<b>V</b><br>
p<br>
(1.9)<br>
which is the statement of the ideal gas law. The ideal gas law states that, the<br>
volume of a given amount of a gas is directly proportional to the number of<br>
moles of the gas and the absolute temperature, and inversely proportional to the<br>
pressure. It is the equation of state for a gas where the state of a gas is its condition<br>
at a given time.<br>
<b>Rearranging Equation 1.9 gives:</b><br>
nT<br>
nT<br>
<b>V</b><br>
<b>V = R</b><br>
<b>P</b><br>
<b>P</b><br>
⇒<br>
where R is a proportionality constant called a universal gas constant. The<br>
preceding equation can be further rearranged into a more general form of the<br>
equation called ideal gas equation (Equation 1.10).<br>
<b>PV = nRT</b><br>
(1.10)<br>
A gas that obeys this equation is called ideal gas (i.e., behaves ideally). The law<br>
is based on empirical evidence and is sometimes considered as the limiting law<br>
as it prescribes the behaviour by which real gases approach at low pressures and<br>
high temperatures. Thus, an ideal gas is an imaginary substance.<br>

is 0.0821 atm L mol−1 K−1, the units of pressure, volume, and temperature must be<br>
atm, litre, and Kelvin, respectively. If R is 8.314 J mol−1 K−1, the units of pressure<br>
must be N/m2, cubic meter (m3), and  Kelvin (K), respectively.<br>

m = 40.4868 g  −  40.2959 g = 0.1909 g<br>

<b>The densities of gases differ from those of solids and liquids in that they:</b><br>
(a)<br>
are strongly affected by temperature and pressure, while those of solids and<br>
liquids depend on temperature but to a lesser extent on pressure as they are<br>
incompressible.<br>
(b)<br>
have a direct relationship with their molar masses, and they increase as<br>
the molar masses increase. Such simple relations do not exist in solids and<br>
liquids.<br>

1.2.6	 Dalton’s Law of partial pressure<br>
<b>The concept of partial pressure was invented by an English chemist called John</b><br>
<b>Dalton (1766–1844). Dalton proposed that in a mixture of gases which do not</b><br>
react chemically, each component exerts a pressure as if it was the only component<br>
occupying the container. This pressure is called a partial pressure. The partial<br>
pressure is a pressure exerted by an individual gas component as part of the total<br>
pressure of the mixture. The Dalton’s Law of partial pressure states that the total<br>
pressure of a mixture of gases is equal to the sum of the partial pressures of all<br>
the gases in the mixture.<br>
If the mixture consists of three gases A, B, and C, the total pressure (PT) of all the<br>
gases in a container is expressed as:<br>
<b>PT = PA + PB + PC       (T, V constant)</b><br>
(1.13)<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   18</b><br>
09/11/2019   08:55<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
19<br>
where PA, PB, and PC are the partial pressures of gases A, B, and C, respectively.<br>
The Dalton’s Law of partial pressure is illustrated in Figure 1.10.<br>
<b>Figure 1.10 Description of Dalton’s Law of partial pressure</b><br>
If the gas mixture consists of ideal gases, the ideal gas equation can be deduced<br>
from Dalton’s Law of partial pressure. From ideal gas equation:<br>

<b>Mole fraction of a gas</b><br>

1.2.7 Graham’s Laws of diffusion and effusion<br>
<b>Diffusion is a net movement of atoms</b><br>
or molecules from the region of high<br>
concentration to the region of low<br>
concentration (Figure 1.11).<br>
<b>Effusion is the process by which gas</b><br>
molecules escape from their container<br>
through a tiny hole into an evacuated<br>
space (Figure 1.12).<br>
<b>Figure 1.12: Effusion of gas molecules through a tiny hole</b><br>
<b>Both diffusion and effusion involve the movement of gas molecules from the</b><br>
region of high concentration to a region of low concentration. The only difference<br>
is that in effusion, the gas molecules pass through a tiny hole while in diffusion,<br>
gas molecules move through an open space.<br>
<b>The Graham’s Law of diffusion and effusion was named after the Scottish Chemist</b><br>
<b>Thomas Graham (1805 – 1869), who in 1846, studied the effusion process and</b><br>
concluded that the rate of effusion was inversely proportional to the square root<br>
of gas density.<br>

(1.24)<br>
<b>Graham’s Law of effusion or diffusion can be used to determine the molar mass</b><br>
of an unknown gas by comparing its diffusion or effusion rate with that of a<br>
known gas. The law applies mainly to effusion on a condition that it occurs at a<br>
very low pressure for gases to escape individually through the hole; and that, the<br>
hole is so tiny that the gases do not collide when they pass through it.<br>

mass of the unknown gas.<br>

1.3<br>
<b>The kinetic molecular theory of gases</b><br>
<b>The gas laws are useful for predicting the behaviour of gases, but they do not</b><br>
tell what happens at the molecular level to cause the observed changes such as<br>
the expansion of a gas upon heating. The kinetic molecular theory (KMT) was<br>
developed in the 19th century by James Clerk Maxwell (1831 – 1879 and Ludwig<br>
<b>Boltzmann (1844 –1906) who found that the physical properties of gases can be</b><br>
explained at the molecular level in terms of the motion of individual molecules.<br>
In this section, you will learn about the characteristics of gases as influenced by<br>
pressure, volume and temperature changes. You will also learn the assumptions<br>
used to derive the fundamental kinetic equation, and use the equation to deduce<br>
the gas laws.<br>
1.3.1	 Relationships among volume, pressure, and temperature<br>
The KMT explains how individual gas particles create a force on the surface,<br>
which is measured as gas pressure. According to Boyle’s Law, when gas pressure<br>
increases, the volume occupied by the gas decreases.  The theory also explains<br>
what happens to the gas particles leading to the change in gas volume when<br>
pressure changes. It also clarifies the contribution of each individual gas to<br>
the total pressure as stated by Dalton’s Law. The increase in volume due to an<br>
increase in temperature as stated in Charles’ Law is also part of this theory.  The<br>
dependence of gas volume or pressure on the number of moles present, and not<br>
on the nature or size of the gas molecules is also explained by the KMT of gases.<br>
1.3.2	 Assumptions of the kinetic molecular theory<br>
<b>The following are the assumptions (postulates) of the kinetic molecular theory</b><br>
of gases:<br>
1.<br>
<b>A gas consists of very small particles called atoms or molecules, scattered</b><br>
throughout the container. Due to their small size, the volume of individual<br>
<b>Gases</b><br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   27</b><br>
09/11/2019   08:55<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five and Six</b><br>
<b>Physical Chemistry</b><br>
for Advanced Level Secondary Schools<br>
28<br>
gas molecules is negligible compared to the volume of the container.<br>
2.<br>
<b>Gas molecules are in constant random motion and travel with uniform</b><br>
velocity whose direction changes on collision with other gas molecules or<br>
with the walls of the container (Figure 1.13).<br>
3.<br>
<b>All collisions between gas molecules are perfectly elastic, hence no loss of</b><br>
kinetic energy during collision.<br>
4.<br>
<b>The distance between gas molecules is very large such that the van der</b><br>
<b>Waals forces of attraction are negligible. As a result, the gas molecules can</b><br>
move freely, independent of each other.<br>
5.<br>
<b>The pressure of a gas is caused by the collisions of gas molecules with the</b><br>
walls of the container.<br>
6.<br>
<b>The average kinetic energy</b><br>
2<br>
2<br>
1 mv<br>
of the gas molecules is directly<br>
proportional to the absolute temperature. This means that the average<br>
kinetic energy of gas molecules is the same at a certain temperature.<br>
<b>Figure 1.13 The random motion of gas molecules in a container</b><br>

1.4<br>
<b>Relative molecular masses of gases</b><br>
<b>The molecular mass is the sum of the atomic masses (in a.m.u, i.e., atomic mass</b><br>
unit) in the molecule.  It is numerically equal to the molar mass (in grams) of a<br>
compound. The most convenient method used nowadays to determine molecular<br>
mass is mass spectrometry. However, in the 19th century (before the invention<br>
of mass spectrometer) several methods were developed based on the gas laws<br>
to determine molecular masses. In this section, you will learn about different<br>
methods used to find molecular masses of gases such as Duma’s and Victor<br>
Meyer’s Methods.<br>
1.4.1	 Determination of molecular mass by Dumas’ Method<br>
<b>Dumas' Method of determining the molecular mass was named after a French</b><br>
<b>Chemist, John Dumas (1800 – 1884). This method is used for the determination</b><br>
of molecular mass of gases. Since liquids are much easier to handle than gases,<br>
volatile liquids are used as sources of gases.  In order for this method to work<br>
<b>Gases</b><br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   31</b><br>
09/11/2019   08:56<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five and Six</b><br>
<b>Physical Chemistry</b><br>
for Advanced Level Secondary Schools<br>
32<br>
well, the volatile liquid must have a boiling point above the room temperature and<br>
below the boiling point of water. Examples of volatile liquids with this criterion<br>
are acetone, ethanol, and diethyl ether.<br>
<b>The experimental set-up for the determination of molar mass of unknown volatile</b><br>
liquid by Dumas’ Method is shown in Figure 1.14. A small amount of volatile<br>
liquid is introduced in a pre-weighed flask of a known volume. The flask is closed<br>
with a stopper containing a narrow tubing and heated in a boiling water bath to<br>
vaporise the liquid. As the liquid vaporises, the vapour first pushes all of the air<br>
out of the flask and then begins to flow out through the narrow tubing until the<br>
pressure inside the bulb equals the atmospheric pressure.<br>
<b>When all the liquid is vaporised and no more vapour is observed to escape the</b><br>
flask, the vapour pressure inside the flask is equal to the atmospheric pressure.<br>
<b>The volume of vapour inside the flask is equal to the volume of the flask. The</b><br>
flask is quickly removed from the boiling water bath to cool and condense the gas<br>
to liquid. The flask is re-weighed to obtain the mass of the liquid which is equal to<br>
the mass of the gas remained in the flask. During the experiment, the temperature<br>
(T) of the water bath and the atmospheric pressure (P) are measured directly. This<br>
information is then used to calculate the molecular mass of the gas by using the<br>
ideal gas equation (Equation 1.11).<br>

Determination of molecular mass of volatile liquid by Dumas' Method<br>
Requirements:	 Volatile liquid, thermometer, heat source, flat bottomed flask,<br>
capillary tube, stopper, paper towel, water trough, barometer,<br>
stopwatch, and weighing balance.<br>
<b>Procedure</b><br>
1.<br>
<b>Measure about 5 cm3 of unknown volatile liquid and put into a pre-weighed</b><br>
flat bottomed flask of known volume.<br>
2.<br>
Close the flat bottomed flask with stopper containing a narrow tubing.<br>
3.<br>
Set the apparatus as shown in Figure 1.14.<br>
4.<br>
<b>Clamp the flat bottomed flask so that it is as deep in the water trough as</b><br>
possible, but the bottom of the flat bottomed flask should not touch the<br>
trough.<br>
5.<br>
Heat the water in the trough to the boiling and record its temperature.<br>
6.<br>
<b>Vaporise the liquid until all the liquid has vaporised and continue heating</b><br>
for another 4 min.<br>
7.<br>
Remove the flask quickly from boiling water and cool it.<br>
8.<br>
Dry the outside of the flask thoroughly using a paper towel.<br>
9.<br>
<b>Reweigh the flask (with the condensed unknown liquid inside) and record</b><br>
this mass.<br>
10.<br>
Measure the atmospheric pressure using a barometer.<br>

1.4.2	 Determination of molecular mass by Victor-Meyer’s Method<br>
<b>Victor-Meyer’s Method of determining the molecular mass was named after a</b><br>
<b>German Chemist Victor Meyer (1848–1897). This method is used for determining</b><br>
the molecular weights of volatile liquids. Figure 1.15 shows the experimental<br>
setup of the Victor Meyer’s Method.<br>
Figure 1.15 Experimental set up for determination of  molecular mass by Victor-<br>
<b>Meyer’s Method</b><br>
The setup consists of an inner Victor-Meyer's tube surrounded by an outer jacket,<br>
with the lower end of the tube in the shape of a bulb. The upper end of the tube<br>
is connected to a side tube leading to a water trough. The outer jacket consists<br>
of a liquid with the boiling temperature of at least 30 °C higher than that of the<br>
<b>Victor Meyer Tube</b><br>
<b>Copper</b><br>
jacket<br>
<b>Hot vapours</b><br>

<b>Liquid</b><br>

<b>Hoffmann</b><br>

volatile substance being studied. At the bottom of the Victor-Meyer’s tube, a<br>
small quantity of a glass wool or asbestos fibre is placed in order to prevent<br>
breakage when the bottle containing the liquid substance is dropped in.<br>
<b>This method involves vaporisation of a known mass of volatile liquid by heating</b><br>
in a Victor Meyer’s tube. The vapour generated displaces the same volume of air,<br>
which is then measured at the recorded experimental temperature and pressure.<br>
The liquid in the jacket is boiled to allow the trapped air inside the tube to escape.<br>
<b>When no more air escapes from the side tube, a graduated tube filled with water</b><br>
is inverted on top of the side tube dipped in a water trough. A small quantity<br>
of volatile substance to be studied is weighed in a small stoppered bottle, and<br>
quickly dropped in the heated Victor-Meyer's tube. Then, the opening at the top<br>
of the tube is immediately corked. When the bottle falls on the asbestos fibre,<br>
its content changes into vapour and blows out the stopper, displacing an equal<br>
volume of air which is then collected in the inverted graduated tube.  To measure<br>
the volume of air in the graduated tube, the latter is taken out by closing its<br>
opening with the thumb and dipping it in a jar full of water. The volume of air<br>
displaced is noted when the water levels outside and inside the tube are the same.<br>

<b>Example 1.12</b><br>
<b>In a Victor-Meyer’s Method for determining molar mass, a 0.156 g of volatile</b><br>
liquid displaced 47.0 cm3 of air measured over water at 14 ºC and 762 mmHg<br>

<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Two</b><br>
<b>Relative molecular masses in</b><br>
solution<br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
A solution consists of a solvent and solute. For solutions to form, the intermolecular<br>
forces of attraction between solutes and solvents must be stronger than those<br>
between solute-solute and those between solvent-solvent. Generally, physical<br>
properties of a solution differ significantly from those of pure solutes and<br>
solvents. For example, aqueous solutions have both lower freezing and higher<br>
boiling points than pure water. The direct relationship of colligative properties<br>
with the number of solute particles dissolved in a solvent makes the colligative<br>
properties very useful for characterising the nature of a solute and for determining<br>
molar masses of substances. In this chapter, you will learn about the colligative<br>
properties, and the abnormality in molecular masses.<br>
2.1<br>
<b>Colligative properties</b><br>
Colligative properties are the properties of solutions that depend on the number of<br>
solute particles dissolved and not on the nature of solute particles. Properties such<br>
as vapour pressure lowering, boiling point elevation, freezing point depression<br>
and osmotic pressure are the four types of colligative properties. In this section,<br>
you will learn about the meaning of each type of colligative properties and how<br>
these properties are used to determine the molar masses of solutes.<br>
In order for a solution to exhibit colligative properties, it must fulfil the following<br>
conditions:<br>
(a)<br>
It should be dilute with a concentration less than or equal to 0.2 M.<br>
<b>Colligative properties require that solute particles do not interact with each</b><br>
other, or at least that their interactions do not change with concentrations. A<br>
high concentration of solution means that there is a large number of solute<br>
particles, and therefore there will be large solute-solute interactions.<br>
(b)<br>
A solute should be non-electrolyte.<br>
<b>Electrolyte solutes tend to form ions in a solution. Because colligative</b><br>
properties depend on the number of solute particles and not on the nature<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   43</b><br>
09/11/2019   08:56<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five and Six</b><br>
<b>Physical Chemistry</b><br>
for Advanced Level Secondary Schools<br>
44<br>
of particles, the formation of ions by a solute which is an electrolyte would<br>
increase the number of particles and hence the interaction between particles<br>
of the solute.<br>
(c)<br>
A solute should be non-volatile.<br>
<b>Consider that boiling point is the temperature at which vapour pressure is</b><br>
equal to atmospheric pressure. Large tendency of particles to escape from<br>
solution into vapour phase means that the vapour pressure which is equal<br>
to atmospheric pressure will be reached at a relatively lower temperature<br>
hence, lower boiling point.<br>
2.1.1	 The vapour pressure lowering<br>
A vapour is the gaseous state of a substance. For molecules of liquids to escape into<br>
the vapour phase, they have to overcome the intermolecular forces of attraction<br>
in liquids. If a container is closed, the molecules of the liquid escaping from the<br>
surface remain in the container above the surface of the liquid. The molecules<br>
in the vapour phase collide with each other, with the walls of the container and<br>
with the surface of the liquid and return to the liquid state leading to a continuous<br>
evaporation-condensation process. After sometime, an equilibrium is established<br>
between a liquid phase and a vapour phase, where the rate of evaporation is<br>
equal to the rate of condensation. The pressure exerted by vapour on the liquid<br>
surface is called the vapour pressure. The vapour pressure of liquid increases<br>
with an increase in temperature and does not depend on the volume of the liquid<br>
or the amount of vapour present. At room temperature, volatile liquids have more<br>
vapour pressures while non-volatile liquids have lower vapour pressures.<br>
<b>The concept of lowering of vapour pressure of a solvent</b><br>
When a non-volatile solute is dissolved in a liquid solvent, the vapour pressure<br>
of the solvent is lowered. Unlike in pure solvents, the surface area in the solution<br>
is not completely available for a volatile solvent because it is partly occupied by<br>
a non-volatile solute. As a result, the rate of evaporation of the solution is lower<br>
than that of pure solvent. Lowering of vapour pressure is the difference between<br>
the vapour pressure of the pure solvent and the vapour pressure of the solution.<br>
<b>Mathematically, the lowering of vapour pressure, ΔP is expressed as:</b><br>
<b>P</b><br>
<b>P</b><br>
<b>P</b><br>
<b>D</b><br>
=<br>
°-<br>
(2.1)<br>
where P° is the vapour pressure of pure solvent and P is the vapour pressure of<br>
the solution.<br>

<b>Vapour pressure lowering and the Raoult’s Law</b><br>
<b>Raoult’s Law was named after a French Chemist called François–Marie Raoult</b><br>
(1830-1901). In the 1880s, Raoult discovered that when a substance is dissolved<br>
in a solution the vapour pressure of the solution decreases. He further observed<br>
that, the decrease in vapour pressure of the solvent in a solution was dependent<br>
on the amount of the solute dissolved and hence the mole fraction of solute and<br>
the vapour pressure of pure solvent. These observations were summarised into<br>
what is called the Raoult’s Law which states that the vapour pressure of a solvent<br>
above a solution (Psolvent) is equal to the vapour pressure of pure solvent<br>

2.1.2	 Boiling point elevation<br>
The boiling point of a liquid is the temperature at which the vapour pressure of<br>
the liquid is equal to the atmospheric pressure. The vapour pressure of a solvent<br>
in a solution of non-volatile solute is always less than the vapour pressure of pure<br>
solvent<br>
o<br>
solvent<br>
solvent<br>
(<br>
)<br>
<b>P</b><br>
<b>P</b><br>
&lt;<br>
. This means that the solution has less tendency of forming<br>
vapour than the pure solvent. Therefore, the solution will boil at a temperature<br>
higher than the boiling point of<br>
pure solvent. For the solution<br>
to boil, its vapour pressure<br>
needs to be increased up to the<br>
external atmospheric pressure.<br>
<b>The increase in temperature is</b><br>
denoted by ΔTb (the subscript<br>
b stands for boiling) which is<br>
equal to T–T°, where T is the<br>
boiling point of solution and<br>
<b>T° is the boiling point of pure</b><br>
solvent. Since T is greater than<br>
<b>T°, therefore ΔTb is the elevation</b><br>
of boiling point.<br>
<b>The elevation of boiling point is defined as the difference between the boiling</b><br>
point of a solution and that of pure solvent. The elevation of boiling point of a<br>
solution is illustrated in Figure 2.2.<br>
<b>The vapour pressure–temperature curve of a solution (curve C – D) is always</b><br>
below that of pure solvent (curve A – B).  Point E is the boiling point of pure<br>
solvent and its corresponding temperature is Tº. At this point the vapour pressure<br>
of the solvent is equal to the atmospheric pressure which is 760 mmHg. At this<br>
temperature, the vapour pressure of a solution is below the atmospheric pressure<br>
as shown by point G. In order for the solution to reach its boiling point (point F),<br>
the temperature is raised from T° to T  This increase in temperature from T° to T<br>
is what is referred to as the elevation of boiling point (ΔTb)<br>
<b>Figure 2.2  The graph showing the elevation of</b><br>
boiling point of the solution<br>
<b>Vapour pressure</b><br>
<b>Temparature</b><br>
<b>Solvent</b><br>
<b>Solution</b><br>
<b>T°</b><br>
<b>T</b><br>
<b>P</b><br>
<b>A</b><br>
<b>C</b><br>
<b>B</b><br>
<b>E</b><br>
<b>F</b><br>

<b>D</b><br>
<b>Patm</b><br>
<b>ΔTb</b><br>

51<br>
<b>The magnitude of the boiling point elevation is proportional to the molal</b><br>
concentration (m) of solute particles.<br>
Mathematically,<br>
<b>ΔTb ∝ m ⇒ ΔTb = Kb m</b><br>
(2.10)<br>
where<br>
b<br>
K is the molal boiling point elevation constant or ebullioscopic constant.<br>
Molality or molal concentration (m) is the number of moles of a solute dissolved<br>
in 1 kg of a solvent. Ebullioscopic constant is defined as the ratio of the elevation<br>
of the boiling point of a solvent to the molality of the solution; or is the amount<br>
of temperature elevated when one mole of a solute is dissolved in one kilogram<br>
of a solvent.<br>
<b>The units of</b><br>
b<br>
<b>K  are degree Celsius per molal unit, (°C/m) or degree Celsius</b><br>
kilogram per mole (°C kg mol−1) or Kelvin kilogram per mole (K kg mol−1).<br>

2.1.3	 Freezing point depression<br>
Freezing point of a liquid is the temperature at which the vapour pressure of solid<br>
is equal to the vapour pressure of liquid. The vapour pressure of pure solvent is<br>
higher than the vapour pressure of solution. Therefore, a solution freezes at lower<br>
temperatures than pure solvent. This difference in the freezing point between<br>
pure solvent and a solution containing non-volatile solute is called depression<br>
of freezing point, denoted by ∆Tf or T°  – T,  where the subscript f stands for<br>
freezing, T  is the freezing point of a solution and T° is the freezing point of pure<br>
solvent.<br>
<b>The depression of freezing point of solution is illustrated in Figure 2.3 where</b><br>
<b>AB</b><br>
is<br>
the<br>
solid-vapour<br>
sublimation<br>
curve<br>
of<br>
the<br>
solid-solvent, and BC is the<br>
liquid–vapour pressure curve<br>
of pure liquid solvent. AD is<br>
the liquid–vapour pressure<br>
curve of solution containing<br>
non-volatile solute. At point<br>
<b>B, pure liquid solvent and</b><br>
solid solvent have the same<br>
vapour pressure. Therefore,<br>
the temperature corresponding<br>
to point B is the freezing point<br>
of pure solvent(<br>
)<br>
<b>T° . When a</b><br>
non-volatile solute is added<br>
into the pure liquid solvent and<br>

freezing point of the solution<br>

forms a solution, the vapour pressure of the solvent is lowered and the solvent<br>
can no longer freeze at the temperature T°. At point A, the vapour pressure of<br>
a solvent from the solution of non-volatile solute and the solid solvent are the<br>
same.  Therefore, the temperature corresponding to point A is the freezing point<br>
of the solution (T).<br>
<b>The vapour pressure curve of pure solvent (BC) is always found above the vapour</b><br>
pressure curve of the solution (AD). Therefore, the point at which the vapour<br>
pressure curve of a solution and that of a solid solvent meet is always lower than<br>
T°. As a result, the freezing point of a solution, T is always lower than that of<br>
pure solvent, T°. This difference between the freezing point of pure solvent and<br>
that of a solution is what is called the freezing point depression. The magnitude<br>
of the depression of the freezing point is proportional to the molal concertation<br>
(m) of solute particles.<br>
Mathematically,<br>

Cryoscopic constant is the ratio of the depression of the freezing point of a solvent<br>
to the molality of the solution or is the quantity of the freezing point depressed<br>
when one mole of a solute is dissolved in one kilogram of the solvent.<br>

<b>The relationship between freezing points and melting points</b><br>
<b>Melting point is the temperature at which the solid substance melts. At this</b><br>
temperature the solid phase and liquid phase are at equilibrium. For small samples<br>
of pure substance whose melting points can be measured to 1 °C, the transition<br>
between the solid phase and liquid phase is very sharp.The substance can exist<br>
as either a solid or liquid at a particular temperature. A substance exists as a solid<br>
at the temperature below melting point or freezing point and as a liquid at the<br>
temperature above melting point or freezing point. Since, the transition between<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   56</b><br>
09/11/2019   08:56<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
57<br>
liquid and solid is very sharp, the melting point of a solid substance is considered<br>
the same as the freezing point of the corresponding liquid.<br>

2.1.4	 Osmotic pressure<br>
A cook can tell why salt is not added in the pot at the start of cooking beans. The<br>
obvious reason is that the beans will take a long time to be cooked. What happens<br>
when a peeled potato with a depression at the top containing salt is immersed in<br>
<b>Relative molecular masses in</b><br>
solution<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   57</b><br>
09/11/2019   08:56<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five and Six</b><br>
<b>Physical Chemistry</b><br>
for Advanced Level Secondary Schools<br>
58<br>
a solution containing pure water? The salt will dissolve and increase the water<br>
level in the depression, suggesting that there is a net movement of water through<br>
the potato to the salt solution through the process called osmosis.<br>
<b>Osmosis is the movement of solvent molecules from a region of dilute solution</b><br>
to a region of concentrated solution through a semi–permeable membrane. The<br>
concept of osmosis can be understood by considering Figure 2.4. In this figure,<br>
two sides are separated by a semi–permeable membrane whereby one side<br>
consists of a dilute solution and the other contains concentrated solution. With<br>
time, the volume of the concentrated solution would increase while that of dilute<br>
solution would decrease. The small pores of the semi-permeable membrane allow<br>
the solvent molecules to pass in either side of the membrane, but not the larger<br>
solute molecules. Because of the presence of solute and fewer solvent molecules<br>
in the concentrated solution, the chance of the solvent molecules to touch the<br>
semi–permeable membrane is low. Therefore, fewer solvent molecules leave the<br>
concentrated solution side to enter the dilute solution side at a given time than<br>
the solvent molecules entering the concentrated solution. As a result, there is a<br>
net flow of solvent molecules into the concentrated solution which increases the<br>
volume of the concentrated solution thereby diluting it.<br>
<b>Figure 2.4 Osmosis process</b><br>
<b>Suppose a pressure greater than that leading to osmosis is applied on the</b><br>
concentrated solution side, the flow of solvent into the concentrated solution will<br>
<b>Dilute solution</b><br>
<b>Concentrated solution</b><br>
<b>Solute</b><br>
<b>Solvent</b><br>
<b>Semi-permeable membrane</b><br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   58</b><br>
09/11/2019   08:56<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
59<br>
stop. Such pressure, which is applied on the side of a concentrated solution to stop<br>
the net flow of the solvent into the concentrated solution through semi-permeable<br>
membrane is called the osmotic pressure. Recall that because of osmosis, there is<br>
a difference between the level of liquid in dilute solution and in the concentrated<br>
solution separated by a semi–permeable membrane. The difference in liquid level<br>
results into a pressure difference which pushes the solvent molecules from the<br>
concentrated solution back to the dilute solution through the semi-permeable<br>
membrane. The equilibrium is reached when the flow rate of the solvent molecules<br>
from dilute solution to the concentrated solution is the same as that from the<br>
concentrated solution back to the dilute solution as indicated by two arrows<br>
pointing at each direction (Figure 2.5). Therefore, such a pressure difference is<br>
the osmotic pressure.<br>
<b>Figure 2.5 The equilibrium between solvent and solution</b><br>
According to Figure 2.5, the pressure created by the difference in heights (h) of<br>
two liquids is the osmotic pressure. The value of osmotic pressure is equivalent<br>
to the external pressure which is applied to restore the liquid into the same level<br>
while maintaining the equilibrium as shown in Figure 2.6.<br>

<b>The van’t Hoff’s Law of osmotic pressure</b><br>
Quantification of the osmotic pressure on solutions can be seen from the work of<br>
the Dutch Chemist Jacobus Henricus van’t Hoff (1852–1911) in the law called<br>
the van’t Hoff’s Law of osmotic pressure. The law states that, the magnitude of<br>
osmotic pressure is proportional to the number of solute particles present in a<br>
given volume of solution.<br>

2.2<br>
<b>Abnormal molecular masses</b><br>
<b>You have seen in the previous sections that colligative properties such as</b><br>
lowering of vapour pressure, elevation of boiling point, depression of freezing<br>
point and osmotic pressure exist in dilute solutions of non-electrolytes.  In these<br>
solutions, the solutes remain in normal molecular conditions and neither undergo<br>
dissociation nor association. A good example is the solution of urea and glucose<br>
in water. Recall that colligative properties of solutions of non-volatile electrolyte<br>
solutes depend on the actual number of solute particles present in the solution,<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   62</b><br>
09/11/2019   08:56<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
63<br>
and hence, the molecular mass of solute. The mathematical relations show that<br>
the colligative property is inversely proportional to the molecular mass of a<br>
solute.  This value of the molecular mass of the solute is theoretical.<br>
For solutions of electrolytes, the values of the observed colligative properties<br>
can either be higher or lower than the theoretically expected value because the<br>
electrolyte solutes such as acid, base or salt can undergo dissociation or association<br>
when dissolved in a solvent.<br>
When a solute undergoes dissociation, the number of particles in the form of ions<br>
is more than the ones actually dissolved.  This increase in the number of particles<br>
increases the value of colligative properties. If the dissociation produces 2, 3,<br>
4 and so on ions, the observed molecular mass becomes<br>
4<br>
1<br>
,<br>
3<br>
1<br>
,<br>
2<br>
1<br>
and so on of<br>
the theoretical molecular mass of electrolyte solute. When the solutes associate<br>
together to form large aggregate molecules, the effective number of particles of<br>
the solute in the solution decreases.  As a result, the observed molecular mass is<br>
double, triple and so on the theoretical molecular mass. In general, the dissociation<br>
of solutes lowers the observed molecular mass and the association increases the<br>
observed molecular mass of the solutes, and these observed molecular masses<br>
together are called abnormal molecular masses.<br>

2.2.2 	 Freezing point depression and Blagden’s Law<br>
In 1788, Sir Charles Brian Blagden (1948 – 1820), a British Physician studied the<br>
effects of dissolved inorganic solutes in the freezing point depression of water. He<br>
revealed that, the dissolved inorganic solutes like salts affect the freezing point<br>
of water.  This discovery, led to the formulation of Blagden’s Law. According to<br>
Blagden’s Law, the depression of freezing point of a dilute solution is directly<br>
proportional to the concentration of the dissolved solutes.<br>
Mathematically,<br>
<b>ΔTf = Kf mi</b><br>
where,<br>
= freezing point depression.<br>
= freezing point constant called cryoscopic constant.<br>

i =   van’t Hoff’s factor (the number of ions particles formed)<br>

<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Three</b><br>
<b>Two component liquid</b><br>
systems<br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
<b>The preceding chapter focused on the properties of solutions that do not depend</b><br>
on the identity of particles but only on their relative concentrations. This chapter<br>
focuses on the properties of the liquid mixtures that depend on the identity of the<br>
individual components. The two component liquid systems are categorised into<br>
miscible, partially miscible, and immiscible liquid systems. The knowledge of<br>
two component liquid systems is important in understanding steam distillation,<br>
solvent extraction, and chromatography. In this chapter, you will learn about<br>
completely miscible liquids, immiscible liquids, and the distribution of solutes<br>
in immiscible solvents.<br>
3.1<br>
<b>Completely miscible liquids</b><br>
<b>Miscible liquids are liquids which dissolve completely in one another in all</b><br>
proportions to form a homogeneous solution. Depending on the strength of<br>
intermolecular forces between the solution and the components involved in<br>
forming the solution, the resulting solution can be ideal or non-ideal. This section<br>
will help you to be able to: differentiate ideal from non-ideal solutions, describe<br>
the Raoult’s Law of ideal solutions and the deviations from Raoult’s Law by<br>
non-ideal solution, describe the vapour pressure diagrams and demonstrate the<br>
process of fractional distillation. The applications of fractional distillation are<br>
also covered in this section.<br>
3.1.1	 Formation of miscible liquid mixture<br>
<b>Miscible liquids are formed between compounds which have similar types of</b><br>
intermolecular forces. For example, ethanol and water are miscible because they<br>
can form hydrogen bond with each other. Likewise, ethane (C2H6) and propane<br>
(C3H8) are miscible because they both exhibit dispersion forces. An illustration of<br>
the formation of miscible liquid mixture is shown in Figure 3.1.<br>
<b>Figure 3.1 The formation of miscible liquid mixture</b><br>
+<br>
<b>Liquid 1</b><br>
<b>Liquid 2</b><br>
<b>Miscible liquid</b><br>
=<br>

3.1.2	 Ideal solutions<br>
<b>An ideal solution is a solution that is made from completely miscible liquids</b><br>
whose intermolecular forces are the same as the intermolecular forces within the<br>
liquids involved in forming the solution. An ideal solution obeys Raoult’s Law<br>
which states that, the partial vapour pressure of a volatile component of an ideal<br>
solution is equal to the vapour pressure of the pure component multiplied by its<br>
mole fraction in the solution.<br>
For an ideal solution formed by liquids A and B,<br>
o<br>

and<br>
o<br>
=<br>
=<br>

<b>P</b><br>
<b>P</b><br>
<b>P</b><br>

and<br>
<b>AP</b><br>
<b>BP  are the partial vapour pressures of components A and B over</b><br>
the solution.<br>
and<br>
<b>A</b><br>
B  are the mole fractions of components A and B in the solution.<br>
and<br>
o<br>
<b>AP</b><br>
o<br>
BP  are the vapour pressures of pure A and B.<br>
<b>According to the Dalton’s Law of partial pressure, the total vapour pressure</b><br>
above the solution containing the volatile components A and B is the sum of the<br>
partial vapour pressures of the two components:<br>
<b>PT = PA + PB</b><br>
(3.2)<br>

<b>Pure component A has more vapour pressure than pure component B.  This</b><br>

of component A (<br>
)<br>
<b>A  increases from zero to one, the corresponding partial</b><br>

(3.3)<br>
<b>Properties of ideal solutions</b><br>
<b>The following are the properties of ideal solutions:</b><br>
(a)<br>
Ideal solutions obey Raoult’s Law at any concentration.<br>
(b)<br>
<b>During the mixing of the liquid components to form ideal solution, heat is</b><br>
neither evolved nor absorbed.  Therefore, the heat change is equal to zero.<br>
(c)<br>
<b>The total volume of the solution is equal to the sum of the volumes of the</b><br>
components of the liquid, in other words, the change in volume is equal to<br>
zero.<br>
(d)<br>
<b>The sum of the partial vapour pressures of the components, say A and B, is</b><br>
equal to the total vapour pressure of solution.<br>
(e)<br>
<b>The forces of interactions in individual components (A – A) and (B – B) are</b><br>
the same as the interactive forces in the solution (A – B); in other words, A<br>
and B are identical in shape, size and character.<br>
Examples of ideal solutions include the solutions of benzene and toluene,<br>
n-hexane and n-heptane, chlorobenzene and bromobenzene, and n-butylchloride<br>
and n-butylbromide.<br>
3.1.3	 Non-ideal solution<br>
A non-ideal solution is a solution that is made from completely miscible liquids<br>
whose intermolecular forces of attractions are different from the intermolecular<br>
forces of the liquid components that form the solution. In other words, the<br>
interactions between A – A or B – B are different from A – B interactions.  Non-<br>
ideal solutions do not obey the Raoult’s Law. Therefore, these solutions are said<br>
to exhibit deviations from the Raoult’s Law such that:<br>

<b>The deviations from Raoult’s Law can be positive or negative depending on</b><br>
the strength of the interactions in the resulting solution compared to those in<br>
individual components.<br>
<b>Positive deviation from Raoult’s Law</b><br>
<b>Positive deviation from Raoult’s Law occurs when the intermolecular forces of</b><br>
attraction between particles in the solution (A – B) are less than those in pure<br>
components (A – A or B – B). Due to weaker intermolecular forces of attraction<br>
in the solution than in pure liquids, the solution has a higher tendency of forming<br>
vapour than any of the individual components. Therefore, the vapour pressure<br>
above the solution is higher than the expected from Raoult’s Law.  This occurs<br>
when a polar molecule is mixed with a non-polar molecule. Two liquids that show<br>
positive deviation from Raoult’s Law in solution, form vapour pressures at low<br>
temperatures. Examples of non-ideal solutions which show positive deviations<br>
from Raoult’s Law include the solutions of acetone (C3H6O) and ethanol<br>
(C2H5OH), acetone and carbon disulphide (CS2), water (H2O) and methanol<br>
(CH3OH), carbon tetrachloride (CCl4) and toluene (C6H5CH3), and cyclohexane<br>
(C6H12) and ethanol.<br>
<b>Properties of non-ideal solutions with positive deviation from Raoult’s Law</b><br>
The following are the properties of non-ideal solutions which exhibit a positive<br>
deviation from Raoult’s Law:<br>
(a)<br>
The solutions do not obey Raoult’s Law at any concentration.<br>
(b)<br>
During mixing of the liquid components to form solution, heat is absorbed.<br>
Therefore, the heat change is greater than zero (endothermic).<br>
(c)<br>
<b>The total volume of the solution is increased. Therefore, the change in</b><br>
volume is greater than zero.<br>
(d)<br>
<b>The partial vapour pressures of the components say A and B, are greater</b><br>
than the products of their mole fractions and their vapour pressures when<br>
pure, i.e.,<br>

<b>In ethanol, the molecules are held together by hydrogen bond. When cyclohexane</b><br>

process is endothermic (ΔHmix&gt; 0). In this process, there is an increase in volume<br>
(ΔVmix&gt; 0) and a slight increase in vapour pressure.<br>

<b>Negative deviation from Raoult’s Law</b><br>
<b>Negative deviation from Raoult’s Law occurs when the intermolecular forces</b><br>
of attraction between molecules in the solution (A – B) are stronger than those<br>
between pure components (A – A or B – B). Stronger intermolecular forces in a<br>
solution rather than in pure liquids make the solution to have less tendency of<br>
forming vapour pressure than pure components. This means that the escaping<br>
tendency of A and B molecules from the solution becomes less than from pure<br>
liquids because much energy must be supplied to break the attractive forces and set<br>
the molecules free. Because of that, the solution has lower total vapour pressure<br>
than the expected from Raoult’s Law. Such solutions form vapour pressure at<br>
higher temperatures than that of pure components.<br>
<b>Examples of non-ideal solutions which show negative deviations from Raoult’s</b><br>
<b>Law include the solutions of acetone (</b><br>

<b>Another way in which the negative deviation from Raoult’s Law occurs is the</b><br>
formation of ions in solutions. A good example is a solution of nitric acid (HNO3)<br>
and water (H2O). Both water and nitric acid react to form ions when mixed<br>
together and these ions interact by ionic bond.<br>
The resultant ionic attractions are greater than the attraction between pure water<br>
or pure nitric acid molecules.  Hence, few molecules escape leading to low vapour<br>
pressure.<br>
<b>Large deviation from Raoult’s Law</b><br>
<b>Recall that, non-ideal solutions deviate positively or negatively from Raoult’s</b><br>
Law due to the difference in intermolecular forces in the solution relative to pure<br>
liquid components. The mixtures which exert vapour pressures that are slightly<br>
greater than predicted by Raoult’s Law are said to show a positive deviation and<br>
those which exert vapour pressures slightly less than that predicted by Raoult’s<br>
<b>Law are said to show a negative deviation.  However, under a slight deviation</b><br>
from Raoult’s Law, the highest vapour pressure at any composition is still the<br>
vapour pressure of the more volatile pure component.<br>
<b>Some liquid mixtures exhibit very large deviations from Raoult’s Law such that</b><br>
the curves become much distorted. For the case of very large positive deviation,<br>
vapour pressures of the solutions at some compositions are higher than the vapour<br>
pressure of even the more volatile pure component.  That is, the maximum vapour<br>
pressure is no longer that of one of the pure liquids (more volatile liquids).<br>

<b>Azeotropic mixtures</b><br>
An azeotropic mixture is a mixture of liquids that boil at constant temperature,<br>
behaves like a pure liquid and whose composition in the liquid phase is the same<br>
as in the vapour phase. The boiling point of azeotropic mixture is either higher<br>
(negative deviation) or lower (positive deviation) than the boiling point of the<br>
pure liquids that form the mixture.<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   80</b><br>

<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
81<br>
<b>Maximum boiling azeotropic mixture</b><br>
<b>The maximum boiling azeotropic mixture boils at a higher temperature than</b><br>
the boiling points of pure components giving lower vapour pressure (negative<br>
deviation). An example of a maximum boiling azeotropic mixture is a mixture of<br>
water<br>

<b>Figure 3.7 Maximum boiling azeotropic mixture of nitric acid and water</b><br>
<b>The boiling point of pure nitric acid (HNO3)  is 86 °C (359 K) and the boiling</b><br>
point of water (H2O)  is 100 °C (373 K). The liquid mixture with 32% H2O and<br>
68% HNO3 has a boiling point of 120.5 °C (393.5 K). This boiling point is higher<br>
than those of pure components and the composition in the liquid phase is the<br>
same as that in a vapour phase. The 32% H2O and 68% HNO3 form what is called<br>
maximum boiling azeotropic mixture.<br>
<b>Minimum boiling azeotropic mixture</b><br>
<b>The minimum boiling azeotropic mixture boils at a lower temperature than the</b><br>
boiling points of pure components, giving higher vapour pressure. A good example<br>
of a minimum boiling azeotropic mixture is a mixture of ethanol  (C2H5OH) and<br>
water (H2O) with a composition of 95.6% and 4.4%, respectively. An illustration<br>
of this azeotropic composition is shown in Figure 3.8.<br>

3.1.4	 Separation of miscible liquid mixture by distillation<br>
<b>Distillation is a method of separating and purifying liquid mixtures into their</b><br>
individual components based on their differences in boiling points. The distillation<br>
process involves heating of a liquid mixture to form vapour and then condensing<br>
vapour into liquid. The vapour composition will be richer in the more volatile<br>
component which has lower boiling point than the less volatile component. The<br>
composition of the condensed liquid will be the same as the composition of the<br>
vapour which contains more volatile component. If the difference in the boiling<br>
points of the liquids is large, a simple distillation can be used. Simple distillation<br>
involves heating the liquid mixture to form vapour followed by condensing vapour<br>
to form liquid. Illustrations of the simple distillation setup and the corresponding<br>
distillation curve are shown in Figures 3.9(a) and 3.9(b), respectively.<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   82</b><br>
09/11/2019   08:57<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
83<br>
<b>Figure 3.9(a) Simple distillation setup</b><br>
<b>Figure 3.9(b) Distillation curve</b><br>
The distillation curve (Figure 3.9b) shows that the liquid mixture consists of liquids<br>
<b>A and B with 50% each. When this mixture is heated, the vapour composition</b><br>
consists of 0.8 (80%) of A and 0.2 (20%) of B. This implies that it is difficult to<br>
get pure liquid components by using simple distillation. In order to get pure liquid<br>
components from the mixture with high purity, successive reflux operations are<br>
required. This can be done by a series of repeated simple distillation processes.<br>
However, in doing a series of repeated distillations, some materials are lost by<br>

evaporation into the air or getting stuck into the apparatus in each distillation run. At<br>
the end of the distillation,<br>
it may be found that much<br>
materials<br>
have<br>
been<br>
lost. There is a method<br>
for carrying out several<br>
simple distillations in<br>
one apparatus resulting<br>
in high yields of pure<br>
liquid<br>
components<br>

<b>This method is called</b><br>
fractional<br>
distillation.<br>
<b>An illustration of the</b><br>
fractional<br>
distillation<br>
setup is shown in Figure<br>
3.10.<br>
In this technique, vapours   of a liquid mixture are passed through a fractionating<br>
column before condensation. The vapours rising in the fractionating column<br>
become richer in the more volatile liquid component. After a series of successive<br>
distillations, the remaining liquid in the distillation flask gets enriched with the<br>
high boiling liquid component.<br>
<b>A fractionating column</b><br>
In fractional distillation, a series of successive distillations occur in a fractionating<br>
column. The fractionating column consists of a glass tube with the attached disks<br>
inside. When the liquid mixture boils, some vapour condenses at each disk since<br>
the temperature decreases with an increase in the height of the column. When<br>
ascending the column, the vapour condenses and re-evaporates repeatedly and<br>
hence becomes richer in the more volatile liquid. The condensed liquid on disks<br>
becomes richer in the less volatile liquid when descending from one disk to the<br>
next along the column. Once all the more volatile liquids have been distilled off,<br>
the temperature at the thermometer rises to the boiling point of the less volatile<br>
liquid. If the distillation is stopped at this point, the less volatile liquid remains<br>
in the distillation flask while the more volatile liquid is collected in the receiver.<br>
<b>The difference in boiling points between the liquid components in the mixture</b><br>
determines the length of the fractionating column and hence the number of plates<br>
required. The temperature versus composition phase diagram for the fractional<br>
distillation process is shown in Figure 3.11.<br>

If a liquid mixture with composition L1 is boiled, it forms vapour with composition<br>
<b>V1. When the vapour V1 is condensed, it gives a liquid composition L2 which has</b><br>
the same composition as the vapour. Boiling of liquid L2 gives the corresponding<br>
vapour V2 with different composition from liquid L2. Generally, at each progressive<br>
boiling and condensing stage, the vapour and the corresponding liquid gets richer<br>
in the more volatile liquid component. At L3 for example, both liquid components<br>
<b>A and B have a composition of 0.5. Boiling of this liquid mixture forms vapour</b><br>
<b>V3 whose composition is 0.2 (20%) liquid component B and 0.8 (80%) liquid</b><br>
component A. Condensing vapour V3 gives liquid mixture L4 with the same<br>
composition.  Then, when liquid mixture L4 is boiled and condensed, liquid L5 is<br>
obtained which is nearly pure liquid component A. This pure liquid component<br>
A is collected in the receiver leaving the less volatile liquid component B in the<br>
flask. Liquid component B can be boiled to its boiling point and then condensed<br>
and collected in a separate receiver.<br>

<b>Fractional distillation of miscible liquids which form azeotropic mixtures</b><br>
<b>Azeotropic mixtures boil at constant temperatures and behave as pure liquids</b><br>
with the same compositions in liquid and vapour phases. Consider the minimum<br>
boiling azeotrope which boils at lower temperature than the boiling point of pure<br>
components. An example of this azeotropic mixture is the ethanol–water mixture<br>
with a composition of 95.6% and 4.4%, respectively, as shown in Figure 3.8.<br>
<b>The boiling point of water is 100 °C (373 K) and that of ethanol is 78.3 °C</b><br>
(351.3 K). These boiling points are higher than the boiling point of the azeotropic<br>
mixture which is 78.1°C (351.1 K). If a mixture of the solution containing less<br>
than 95.6% ethanol which is a more volatile component is distilled by fractional<br>
distillation, the azeotropic mixture that is collected in the receiver will exhaust the<br>
volatile component (ethanol) leaving pure water in the flask. This is because the<br>
amount of ethanol present in the mixture is less than what is needed to form the<br>
azeotropic mixture with water. Therefore, the proportion of water that is needed<br>
to form azeotropic mixture with ethanol is also lowered.<br>
<b>If the composition with greater than 95.6% ethanol is distilled, the azeotropic</b><br>
mixture which is collected in the receiver will exhaust all the water contents in<br>
the solution, leaving pure ethanol in the flask. This is because the proportion<br>
of water available to form azeotropic mixture with ethanol is less than 4.4 %,<br>
therefore the amount of ethanol used is also less than 95.6%.<br>
<b>For the case of maximum boiling azeotrope, the boiling point of azeotropic</b><br>
mixture is higher than the boiling point of pure components. An example is the<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   86</b><br>
09/11/2019   08:57<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
87<br>
azeotropic mixture of water–nitric acid with a composition of 32% and 68%,<br>
respectively as shown in Figure 3.7. The boiling point of water is 100 °C (373  K)<br>
and that of nitric acid is 86 °C (359 K). These boiling points are lower than the<br>
boiling point of the azeotropic mixture, which is 120.5 °C (393.5 K). If a mixture<br>
of solution containing less than 68% nitric acid is distilled, pure water is distilled<br>
and collected in the receiver and azeotropic mixture remains in the flask. This is<br>
because the boiling point of pure water is less than the boiling point of azeotropic<br>
mixture and all the nitric acid is used in the formation of azeotropic mixture. If<br>
the composition with greater than 68% nitric acid is distilled, the acid distils off<br>
and its concentration in the distillation flask decreases. The temperature rises<br>
until the boiling point of the azeotropic mixture is reached. Pure nitric acid is<br>
collected in the receiver while the azeotropic mixture remains in the distillation<br>
flask.<br>
<b>The given examples show that, azeotropic mixtures can only be collected as</b><br>
pure components in the receiver or left in the distillation flask depending on<br>
their boiling points relative to pure components. Therefore, the composition of<br>
azeotropic mixtures cannot be separated by normal fractional distillation. The<br>
following are the methods that can be used to separate azeotropic mixtures:<br>
(a)<br>
<b>Distillation with a third component</b><br>
<b>Consider the azeotropic mixture of ethanol and water containing 95.6%</b><br>
ethanol at a normal atmospheric pressure. If benzene is added in the<br>
distillation flask, a new minimum boiling azeotrope with 23.8% ethanol,<br>
23.3% water and 52.9% benzene is formed. Having a low boiling point<br>
of 64.86 °C (337 K) makes the mixture to be collected in the receiver and<br>
leaves pure ethanol in the flask.<br>
(b)<br>
<b>By chemical methods</b><br>
<b>Quicklime (CaO) may be used to remove water from an azeotropic mixture</b><br>
of ethanol and water as shown in the following reaction:<br>
<b>The formed precipitate of calcium hydroxide can be separated from the</b><br>
ethanol by physical means.<br>
(c)<br>
Adsorption: Charcoal or silica gel may adsorb one of the components.<br>
(d)<br>
<b>Solvent extraction: One component can be separated from the solution by</b><br>
solvent extraction where solvents with similar attractive forces are used.<br>

3.1.5 	 Applications of fractional distillation<br>
There are several ways in which fractional distillation is applied in daily life.<br>
One of the important industrial uses of fractional distillation is in the separation<br>
of crude oil into its various components. Other industrial uses include chemical<br>
plants for large scale manufacturing of chemicals, natural gas processing,<br>
separation of pure gas from the mixture of gases (cryogenic separation) and<br>
purification of organic compounds. The fractional distillation is also used in the<br>
purification of water as water contains many dissolved impurities that can be<br>
removed by this method. Other common uses of fractional distillation are the<br>
production of alcoholic beverages such as whisky and the like and the separation<br>
of organic compounds from water such as a mixture of acetone and water.<br>
<b>Fractional distillation of crude oil</b><br>
Fractional distillation is used to separate various components of crude oil which<br>
include bitumen, fuel and lubricating oil, diesel, kerosene, naphtha, petrol and<br>
refinery gas. An illustration of the fractional distillation column or tower of<br>
crude oil is shown in Figure 3.12. The crude oil is heated and the mixture start<br>
to boil. The temperature decreases as ascending the fractionating column.  The<br>
crude oil evaporates as it boils and its vapours allowed to condense at different<br>
temperatures in the fractionating column. Each fraction contains hydrocarbon<br>
molecules with a similar number of carbon atoms. Fractions with lower boiling<br>
points rise up to the top of the column where they cool down and condense to<br>
form liquid. Gases condense at the top of the column, the liquid in the middle<br>
and solids stay at the bottom. Bitumen which remains as a solid at the bottom of<br>
the column is used as material for road construction and roofing. Heavy fuel oils<br>
are used in the operation of ships and factories due to their high energy content.<br>
Above the fuel oil, the lubricating oil is collected. The lubricating oil works to<br>
reduce wear and tear in various machine parts in contact.<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   88</b><br>
09/11/2019   08:57<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
89<br>
<b>Figure 3.12 Fractional distillation of crude oil</b><br>
Above the lubricating oil, the sequence of the fractions follows this trend: diesel,<br>
kerosene, naphtha, petrol and finally refinery gas. Diesel is a source of energy to<br>
drive heavy duty vehicles such as lorries, tractors and buses. Kerosene is used as<br>
jet fuel, for lighting and heating. Naphtha, which is collected above the kerosene<br>
in the fractionating column is used in the production of various chemicals.  Petrol<br>
oil which is above the naphtha in the fractionating column is used as fuel for<br>
light duty machines and vehicles. The refinery gases which are condensed at the<br>
top of the column are used as fuels for industrial and domestic purposes such as<br>
cooking.<br>

3.2<br>
<b>Immiscible liquids</b><br>
<b>Immiscible liquids are liquids which when mixed together in any proportions</b><br>
form two separate phases. The two liquids are mutually insoluble in each other. In<br>
this section, you will learn to: describe properties of immiscible liquids, explain<br>
the applications of immiscible liquids and determine the molar mass of a high<br>
boiling liquid by using steam distillation.<br>
3.2.1	 Formation of immiscible liquid mixture<br>
<b>Liquids are immiscible with each other when the intermolecular forces are</b><br>
not alike. For example, water and butane are immiscible because the types<br>
of intermolecular forces in the two compounds are different. Water exhibits<br>
hydrogen bonding while butane exhibits only the dispersion forces. In a mixture<br>
of immiscible liquids such as chloroform and ethyl acetate, the less dense liquid<br>
(ethyl acetate) forms the upper layer and the denser liquid (chloroform) forms<br>
the bottom layer. An illustration of the formation of immiscible liquid mixture is<br>
shown in Figure 3.13.<br>

(boils at constant temperature). Bromobenzene–water mixture for example, has<br>
a boiling point of 95 °C which is less than that of bromobenzene (156 °C) and<br>
water (100 °C). The total vapour pressure (<br>

3.2.3	 Mixture of partially miscible liquids<br>
Partially miscible liquids are liquids which dissolve in one another to a limited<br>
extent. Examples are mixtures of phenol and water, and ether and water. If<br>
few drops of phenol are shaken in water, a clear solution of phenol in water is<br>
obtained, the same as for few drops of water in phenol. Similarly, ether dissolves<br>
1.2% of water and water dissolves about 6.5% of ether. When equal volumes of<br>
ether and water are shaken together, two layers are formed: a saturated solution<br>
of ether in water and a saturated solution of water in ether. Also for equal<br>
volumes of phenol and water,<br>
a saturated solution of phenol<br>
in water (top layer) and a<br>
saturated solution of water<br>
in phenol (bottom layer) is<br>
formed. These two solutions,<br>
which are partially miscible,<br>
can be treated as immiscible<br>
liquids. The solubility and<br>
hence<br>
miscibility<br>
of<br>
the<br>
partially<br>
miscible<br>
liquids<br>
depend on temperature and<br>
fractional composition of the<br>
liquid components. Phenol<br>
water mixture will be used<br>
to discuss this phenomenon<br>
(Figure 3.14).<br>
<b>The solubility of both phenol in water and water in phenol increases with</b><br>
temperature until the critical solution temperature (CST) is reached. The critical<br>
solution temperature is the temperature at which the two liquid layers merge into<br>
one another to form one phase. At any temperature above the critical solution<br>
temperature, phenol and water are miscible in all proportions. The critical solution<br>
temperature of phenol water system is about 67 °C. Below this temperature, the<br>
percentage composition of the two components determine the number of layers.<br>
<b>For the phenol water mixture at the temperature below 50 °C, a mixture of 90%</b><br>
phenol and 10% water or 5% phenol and 95% water will be completely miscible.<br>
<b>The coloured part of the phenol water phase diagram indicates the temperatures</b><br>
and compositions at which the two liquids behave as completely immiscible<br>

phase diagram for phenol-water system<br>

3.2.4	 Applications of immiscible liquids<br>
<b>The knowledge of immiscible liquids is mainly applicable in steam distillation</b><br>
for separation of mixtures and determination of molar masses of compounds with<br>
high boiling points or compounds that decompose at their boiling temperatures.<br>
<b>Steam distillation</b><br>
<b>Steam distillation is the process whereby steam is passed through an impure</b><br>
liquid which is immiscible with water and in the presence of steam, the liquid<br>
compound is made volatile and distils with steam. It is a special type of distillation<br>
for temperature sensitive materials. Many high boiling point organic compounds<br>
cannot be distilled at their own boiling points since they tend to decompose near<br>
their boiling points, hence they can be distilled by steam distillation below 100 °C.<br>
Since the mixture boils at constant temperature, it yields a distillate of constant<br>
composition. Therefore, steam distillation is used for the purification of organic<br>
liquids which are steam volatile and immiscible with water. The experimental<br>
setup of steam distillation is shown in Figure 3.15.<br>
<b>Figure 3.15 Steam distillation setup</b><br>
<b>The impure organic liquid containing non-volatile impurities is mixed with</b><br>
water, heated and steam passed into it. The aim of mixing organic liquid with<br>
water is to avoid the decomposition of its molecules by lowering the boiling<br>
temperature. The boiling point of the given liquid is reached when the vapour<br>

pressure of the liquid is equal to the atmospheric pressure. For a mixture of<br>
two immiscible liquids, each component exerts its own vapour pressure. The<br>
total vapour pressure, (PT) over the mixture is equal to the sum of the individual<br>
vapour pressures (PA, PB) at that temperature. Hence, the total vapour pressure is<br>
given by the following equation:<br>
<b>PT = PA  +  PB</b><br>
<b>Since PT > PA  or  PB, the boiling point of the mixture of two liquids will be</b><br>
lower than either of the pure components. For example, in the mixture of aniline<br>
and water, the boiling point of water is 100 °C and that of aniline is 184 °C<br>
but, the steam distillation temperature of aniline is 98 °C. Following the steam<br>
distillation process, the vapour of the organic liquid and steam rising from the<br>
boiling mixture pass through the condenser. The distillate which is collected in<br>
the receiver consists of two layers, one of the pure organic liquid and the other of<br>
water. The pure liquid is then removed by means of a separating funnel. The steam<br>
distillation process can be used to extract natural products from plant materials.<br>
<b>Examples include the extraction of eucalyptus oil from eucalyptus plant, citrus</b><br>
oil from lemon or orange peel and the extraction of oils from various plants such<br>
as Rosa damascena (Rose plant) and Jasmine plant which are used for making<br>
perfumes. The steam distillation is also used in the purification of organic liquids<br>
with high boiling points and those which can easily decompose on heating at high<br>
temperatures.<br>
<b>Determination of molar mass of high boiling point liquids by steam distillation</b><br>
<b>It has been discussed previously that if a mixture of two immiscible liquids is</b><br>
heated, both components can contribute to the vapour pressure and express their<br>
full saturated vapour pressure because being immiscible they act independently.<br>
<b>So, at a given temperature, the total vapour pressure is the sum of the vapour</b><br>
pressures of the pure liquid components. That is, for immiscible liquid A and B,<br>

3.3<br>
<b>Distribution of solutes in immiscible solvents</b><br>
<b>In the previous section, you learnt that when two immiscible liquids are mixed</b><br>
in the same container they separate into two layers. The denser liquid forms the<br>
lower layer and the lighter liquid forms the upper layer. For example, water is<br>
denser than many organic solvents but less dense than some of the organic solvents<br>
such as chloroform. Therefore, if chloroform and water are mixed in the same<br>
container, two layers are formed with water being the top layer and chloroform<br>
the bottom layer. In this section, you will learn to account for the distribution of<br>
solutes between two immiscible solvents, demonstrate the distribution law, apply<br>
the distribution law in solvent extraction and explain the deviations from the<br>
distribution law.<br>
3.3.1	 The distribution law<br>
<b>When a solute X which is soluble in both solvents is added to the mixture of</b><br>
immiscible liquids and shaken, it distributes itself between the two solvents.<br>
<b>There is a continuous interchange of solute between the liquid layers until a</b><br>
dynamic equilibrium is established. The equilibrium is heterogeneous since the<br>
solute is distributed between two distinct layers. At equilibrium, the rate (R1)<br>
at which molecules of X pass from solvent 1 to solvent 2 is proportional to its<br>
concentration (C1)  in solvent 1.<br>
1<br>
1<br>
1<br>
1<br>

of X pass from solvent 2 to solvent 1 is proportional to its concentration  (C2) in<br>
solvent 2.<br>
