<b>The distribution law is governed by the following conditions:</b><br>
(a)<br>
<b>Non-miscibility of solvents</b><br>
<b>The two solvents in which the solute is distributed should be immiscible</b><br>
or partially miscible with each other and the addition of solute should not<br>
alter the immiscibility.<br>
(b)<br>
<b>Equilibrium concentrations</b><br>
<b>The true values of solutes concentrations are obtained after the distribution</b><br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   100</b><br>
09/11/2019   08:57<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
101<br>
of solute between the two immiscible liquids reach equilibrium. If the<br>
equilibrium is not reached, the value of KD will not be correct.<br>
(c)<br>
<b>Dilute solution</b><br>
<b>The law does not apply at high concentration of solute therefore, the</b><br>
concentration should be kept low.<br>
(d)<br>
<b>Constant temperature</b><br>
<b>The temperature is maintained constant during the experiment because any</b><br>
change in temperature will affect the solubility of the solute in the two<br>
liquids.<br>
(e)<br>
<b>Same molecular state</b><br>
The molecular state of the solute should be the same in all the solvents.<br>
<b>That is, there should be no dissociation or association of solutes in any of</b><br>
the solvents.<br>

<b>Example 3.4</b><br>

<b>The distribution law in terms of solubility</b><br>
The distribution coefficient, KD, can also be expressed in terms of solubility. This<br>
is because solubility also represents the concentration of solute in a solvent.<br>

<b>Activity 3.3</b><br>
Determination of the distribution coefficient of ethanedioic acid (oxalic acid)<br>
in diethyl ether-water solvent system<br>
<b>Requirements:	 Oxalic acid, diethyl ether, distilled water, 0.1 M sodium</b><br>
hydroxide, phenolpthalein indicator, retort stand, burette,<br>
conical flask, separating funnel, thermometer, beaker (100<br>
cm3), measuring cylinder, dropper and pipette (10 cm3).<br>
<b>Procedure</b><br>
1.<br>
<b>Measure 100 cm3 of diethyl ether and 100 cm3 of distilled water and transfer</b><br>
them into the 250 cm3 separating funnel.<br>
2.<br>
<b>Add 5 g of oxalic acid into the separating funnel and fix the stopper on the</b><br>
separating funnel.<br>
3.<br>
Shake the mixture vigorously until all solid particles dissolve completely.<br>
4.<br>
Allow the mixture to settle.<br>
5.<br>
Separate the organic layer from the aqueous layer.<br>
6.<br>
<b>Measure 10 cm3 of the aqueous layer using a measuring cylinder and</b><br>
transfer into a conical flask.<br>
7.<br>
<b>Titrate the aqueous layer with sodium hydroxide solution and record the</b><br>
results as shown in the following table. Use phenolphthalein indicator.<br>
<b>Titration</b><br>
<b>Pilot</b><br>
1<br>
2<br>
3<br>
<b>Final volume (cm3)</b><br>
<b>Initial Volume (cm3)</b><br>
<b>Volume used (cm3)</b><br>
8.<br>
<b>Titrate 10 mL of the organic layer with sodium hydroxide solution using</b><br>
phenolphthalein indicator and record the results as in step 7.<br>
<b>Questions</b><br>
1.<br>
<b>Calculate the distribution coefficient (KD) for oxalic acid between ether and</b><br>
water.<br>
2.<br>
If 2.5 g of the acid had been shaken with 50 cm3 of each solvent at 25 ºC,<br>
what value of KD would be expected if the layers were again analysed?<br>

<b>Deviations from the distribution law</b><br>
<b>It has been explained that one of the conditions governing the distribution law</b><br>
is that, the molecular state of the solute should be maintained throughout the<br>
experiment. This means that the solute should neither associate nor dissociate<br>
in any of the solvents. If the solute undergoes an association or a dissociation in<br>
any of the solvents, the ratio of the concentrations in the upper layer to the lower<br>
layer would change. As a result, the value of the distribution coefficient would<br>
also change. These two cases will be discussed separately, that is, KD when the<br>
solute undergoes association and when it undergoes a dissociation.<br>
<b>The distribution coefficient when solute undergoes association</b><br>
<b>The distribution of a solute between two immiscible</b><br>
solvents when the solute undergoes association in<br>
one of the solvents (lower layer) is shown in Figure<br>
3.16. Suppose the solute X distributes itself between<br>
solvent A and solvent B. In solvent A, the solute X<br>
exists in a non-associated state while in solvent B, n<br>
molecules of X associates to form Xn molecules. At<br>
equilibrium, few free molecules of X are also present<br>
in solvent B.<br>

3.3.2	 Applications of the distribution law in solvent extraction<br>
<b>Solvent extraction or liquid-liquid extraction is a method by which a compound</b><br>
is pulled out from one solvent to another where the two solvents are immiscible.<br>
<b>With this method, the compounds which are poorly miscible in organic solvents</b><br>
but highly miscible in water can be extracted into organic compounds by repetitive<br>
extraction with the separating funnel or liquid–liquid extractor. Assume that an<br>
organic substance is present in aqueous solution. If this solution is mixed with an<br>
organic solvent which is immiscible with water, for example, ether, and shaken,<br>
some or all of the organic substances will be extracted from the water into the<br>
<b>Figure 3.17 The distribution</b><br>
of solute when undergoes<br>
dissociation<br>
<b>Two component liquid systems</b><br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   107</b><br>
09/11/2019   08:58<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five and Six</b><br>
<b>Physical Chemistry</b><br>
for Advanced Level Secondary Schools<br>
108<br>
organic phase. On standing, the two liquids separate into layers with some<br>
amount of solute from aqueous layer dissolved in the organic layer. The aqueous<br>
layer (in this case, lower layer) is transfered out of the separating funnel, leaving<br>
ether layer behind.  The ether layer is transferred to the distillation flask where it<br>
is distilled to obtain the organic substance targeted. If desired, the process may<br>
be repeated by taking the collected aqueous layer back to the separating funnel<br>
and again shaken with ether. Solvents such as chloroform, carbon disulphide and<br>
hexane can also be used in the solvent extraction. The solvent extraction setup is<br>
shown in Figure 3.18.<br>
<b>Figure 3.18 Solvent extraction setup</b><br>
<b>Multiple extraction</b><br>
Multiple extraction is the process by which solvent extraction is done repeatedly<br>
by introducing the aqueous layer back into the separating funnel and shaking it<br>
again with organic solvent immiscible with water. This method is used to recover<br>
the possible maximum amount of organic substance from the aqueous phase.<br>
The aqueous solution is first extracted with a portion of organic solvent in the<br>
separating funnel. This aqueous layer is then transferred to another separating<br>
funnel and shaken with another portion of the same organic solvent. This process<br>
is repeated several times until the maximum amount of organic substance is<br>
recovered. The following is a demonstration that, multiple solvent extraction is<br>
more efficient than using the same volume of organic solvent once.<br>
<b>Suppose that you have 100 mL of an aqueous solution containing m grams of</b><br>
an organic substance to be extracted with 100 mL of ether. The value of the<br>
distribution coefficient between ether and water is 2.<br>
<b>Organic solvent is</b><br>
added to the aqueous<br>
solution A<br>
<b>When shaken, the solute A</b><br>
distributes between organic<br>
and aqueous layer<br>
<b>Aqueous</b><br>
layer<br>
<b>Organic</b><br>
layer<br>
<b>Organic</b><br>
layer<br>
<b>Aqueous</b><br>
layer<br>
<b>Aqueous</b><br>
solution<br>
of A<br>
<b>Organic solvent</b><br>

3.3.3	 Applications of distribution law in chromatography<br>
<b>Chromatography is a technique which is used to separate the components of a</b><br>
mixture due to their difference in the affinity between the two phases which are a<br>
mobile phase and a stationary phase. This technique is used in the identification,<br>
separation and purification of organic substances from a mixture. It is based on the<br>
difference in the extent of adsorption of various components of a mixture on an<br>
adsorbent.  The commonly used adsorbents include magnesium oxide, cellulose<br>
paper, silica gel and alumina. The adsorbents form a fixed or stationary phase and<br>
the liquid in which the substance dissolves is called a mobile phase. Some of the<br>
most commonly used chromatographic techniques depending on the nature of<br>
the two phases include column chromatography, paper chromatography and thin<br>
layer chromatography.<br>
<b>Column chromatography</b><br>
<b>Column chromatography uses a solid adsorbent as a stationary phase and a liquid</b><br>
solvent as a mobile phase. Figure 3.19 shows the separation of the components<br>
of a mixture by column chromatography. The column is packed with a suitable<br>
adsorbent such as silica gel up to about three quarters of the column containing<br>
glass wool at the bottom. A solution of the mixture to be separated is poured on<br>
<b>Two component liquid systems</b><br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   115</b><br>
09/11/2019   08:58<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five and Six</b><br>
<b>Physical Chemistry</b><br>
for Advanced Level Secondary Schools<br>
116<br>
<b>Packed column</b><br>
<b>Sample</b><br>
<b>Stationary phase</b><br>
(e.g., silica gel)<br>
<b>Solvent</b><br>
addition<br>
<b>Separated</b><br>
components<br>
<b>Cotton wool</b><br>
top of the silica gel followed by the addition of liquid solvent as a mobile phase<br>
for elution. The component which is less adsorbed to the stationary phase runs<br>
faster with the solvent than the more adsorbed components hence, the separation<br>
of components. These components of the mixture are collected separately at the<br>
bottom of the column.<br>
.<br>
<b>Figure 3.19 Column chromatography</b><br>
<b>Paper chromatography</b><br>
<b>Paper chromatography uses liquids or solids as stationary phase and liquid as</b><br>
mobile phase. It involves a flow of a<br>
solvent on a filter paper. The mechanism<br>
of paper chromatography is partly<br>
partition and partly adsorption. The<br>
partly partition comes from the fact<br>
that the constituents of the mixture are<br>
distributed between the water present<br>
in the filter paper (as a stationary<br>
phase) and an organic solvent as a<br>
mobile phase. The experimental setup<br>
for paper chromatography is shown in<br>
Figure 3.20.<br>
<b>Figure 3.20 Paper chromatography</b><br>
<b>Lid (Petri dish)</b><br>
<b>Paper holder</b><br>
<b>Beaker</b><br>
<b>Solvent front</b><br>
<b>Separated</b><br>
components<br>
<b>Paper</b><br>
<b>Sample</b><br>
<b>Solvent</b><br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   116</b><br>

<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
117<br>
A drop of the mixture of the solution of compounds to be separated is placed near<br>
one edge of the filter paper (about 2 cm from the edge) and dried. The paper is<br>
dipped into the organic solvent (about 1 cm), leaving the dry spot outside. The<br>
organic solvent is sucked up through the capillaries of the paper. As the solvent<br>
moves up and reaches the mixture, it removes its components at various speeds,<br>
depending on the extent of the adsorption to the stationary phase and polarity<br>
relative to the solvent mobile phase. The distance moved by a compound relative<br>
to the solvent distance is expressed as RF, called a retention factor.<br>
<b>Distance travelled by the solutefrom the original line</b><br>
<b>Distance travelled by the solvent from the original line</b><br>
<b>RF =</b><br>
(3.23)<br>
<b>Thin layer chromatography (TLC)</b><br>
<b>Thin layer chromatography works in the same way as paper chromatography</b><br>
except that in TLC a thin layer which is coated with a solid adsorbent material on<br>
one side is used.<br>
<b>Confirmatory test for bromide and iodide ions</b><br>
<b>In confirming the presence of bromide or iodide in the solution, a solution is</b><br>
treated with chlorine water. If iodide or bromide ions are present, they will be<br>
oxidised to iodine and bromine, respectively. When the solution is shaken with<br>
trichloromethane (chloroform) and left to stand, two layers are formed with the<br>
denser chloroform at the bottom. Iodine or bromine distributes itself between the<br>
aqueous and the chloroform layer. Being more soluble in chloroform, iodine or<br>
bromine concentrates at the bottom layer making it violet for iodine and red for<br>
bromine.<br>

<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Four</b><br>
<b>Energetics</b><br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
<b>In chemical reactions, energy changes are involved in forming and breaking</b><br>
bonds. The study of chemical processes leading to energy changes is called<br>
energetics. This chapter, focuses on thermochemistry which is a branch of<br>
chemistry that describes the energy changes that occur during chemical reactions.<br>
Thermochemical concepts are important in measuring the energy content of food,<br>
determining the specific enthalpies of fuels, refrigeration, and air conditioning.<br>
The form of energy change that is considered here is the heat energy. Therefore,<br>
in this chapter you will learn about the heat of reaction, the enthalpy of reaction,<br>
and the application of Hess’s Law in the determination of enthalpy changes for<br>
chemical reactions.<br>
4.1<br>
<b>Heat of reaction</b><br>
Heat is a form of energy that flows between two objects or two parts of an object<br>
because of the difference in temperature. Many chemical reactions involve the<br>
transfer of heat from the surroundings to the system or vice versa. The amount of<br>
heat absorbed or evolved when the reaction goes to completion is called the heat<br>
of reaction.<br>
4.1.1	 The concept of heat of reaction<br>
The direction of flow of energy is from a warmer object or part to a cooler one.<br>
For example, thermal energy is transferred from a hot object to your hand if you<br>
touch the object because the object is warmer than your hand. Similarly, thermal<br>
energy is transferred from your hand to an ice if you touch it because ice is cooler<br>
than your hand. Heat is a measure of total energy of an object.<br>
<b>A physical property that quantitatively expresses the degree of hotness or</b><br>
coldness of an object is called temperature. When substances become warmer,<br>
it means that the average kinetic energy of their particles has increased. This<br>
kinetic energy causes what you sense as warmth and is quantitatively measured<br>
as temperature. The unit of temperature is Kelvin (K) or degree Celsius (ºC).<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   122</b><br>
09/11/2019   08:59<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
123<br>
Temperature is independent of the amount of substance present. For example, both<br>
one bucket of water and one cup of water at 50 ºC have the same temperature, even<br>
though the heat content is different. Unlike temperature, the heat content depends<br>
on the amount of substance present. The bucket full of water has larger heat<br>
content than a cup full of water although they have the same temperature. This<br>
heat content is called enthalpy (H). Heat is expressed in joules (J) or kilojoules<br>
(kJ) named after James Prescott Joule (1818–1889), an English Physicist who<br>
studied the nature of heat and discovered its relationship to mechanical work.<br>
<b>A joule is a unit of energy which is equal to work done when a force of one</b><br>
Newton acts through a distance of one meter. That is;<br>
1 joule (J) = 1 N m = 1 (kg m s−2) m<br>
2<br>
2<br>
1 kg<br>
=<br>
s<br>
m<br>
1 kilojoule (kJ) = 1000 joules (J)<br>
<b>The joule is the most commonly used unit of heat (energy).  Heat can also be</b><br>
expressed in a unit of calories or kilocalories.<br>
1 calorie (cal) = 4.184 Joules (J)<br>
1000 calories (cal) = 1 kilocalorie (kcal)<br>
1 kilocalorie (kcal) = 4.184 kilojoules (kJ)<br>
Calorie (cal) is the amount of heat required to raise the temperature of 1 gram of<br>
water by 1 degree Celsius (1 °C).<br>
<b>As discussed previously, heat flows whenever there is difference in temperature</b><br>
between two parts. For the case of chemical reactions, a system is a collection of<br>
matter being studied and the surroundings refer to everything outside the container,<br>
that means system and surroundings are separated by the wall of the container<br>
such as beakers and test tubes. If there is heat transfer from the surroundings to<br>
the system, heat change is positive which means that the surrounding are losing<br>
energy and the system is gaining energy. Likewise, if there is a heat transfer from<br>
the system to the surroundings, the heat change is negative. In this case, the system<br>
is losing energy and the surroundings are gaining energy. The illustration of the<br>
energy change between the system and the surroundings is shown in Figure 4.1.<br>
<b>Energetics</b><br>

For exothermic reaction, the system is losing energy, and the temperature of the<br>
surroundings increases. For endothermic reaction, the system is gaining energy<br>
and therefore the temperature of the surroundings decreases.<br>
4.1.2 Heat capacity and specific heat capacity<br>
<b>When a substance is heated, its temperature increases depending on its heat</b><br>
capacity.  Heat capacity (C) is the amount of heat required to raise the temperature<br>
of a substance by one degree Celsius (1 °C) or one Kelvin (1 K).<br>
The amount of heat required to raise the temperature of a unit mass of a substance<br>
by one degree Celsius (1 °C) or one Kelvin (1 K) is called specific heat capacity<br>
(c). Specific heat capacity tells how much the temperature of an object will<br>
increase or decrease by the gain or loss of heat energy.<br>

<b>Example 4.1</b><br>
<b>Calculate the amount of heat energy required to increase the temperature of 20</b><br>
g of nickel from 50 °C to 70 °C given that the specific heat capacity of nickel is<br>
equal to 440 J Kg−1 °C−1.<br>
<b>Solution</b><br>
<b>Data given:</b><br>
<b>Mass of nickel = 20 g, Temperature, T1 = 50 °C, T2 = 70 °C</b><br>
specific heat capacity of nickel, c = 440 J kg−1 °C−1<br>
q<br>
m<br>
c<br>

=<br>
m = 20 g = 0.02 kg, c = 440 J kg−1 °C−1, ΔT = 70 °C– 50 °C = 20 °C<br>
q = 0.02 kg × 440 J kg−1 °C−1 × 20 °C = 176 J<br>
Therefore, the heat energy required is 176 J.<br>
<b>Example 4.2</b><br>
Calculate the heat capacity of 80.0 g of water given that the specific heat capacity<br>
of water is 4.48 J g–1 °C–1.<br>
<b>Solution</b><br>
<b>Data given:</b><br>
<b>Mass of water = 80.0 g</b><br>
<b>Specific heat capacity of water, c = 4.18 J/g °C</b><br>
<b>Heat capacity (C) = mass (m) × specific heat capacity (c)</b><br>
<b>Heat capacity (C) = 80 g × 4.18 J g−1 °C−1 = 334 .4 J °C−1</b><br>
<b>Therefore, the heat capacity of 80 g of water is 334.4 J °C−1</b><br>

4.1.3	 Endothermic and exothermic reactions<br>
<b>Many chemical reactions involve the transfer of heat from the surroundings to</b><br>
the systems or vice versa. The chemical reaction which involves the transfer of<br>
heat from the surroundings to the reaction system is called endothermic reaction.<br>
<b>This means that the reaction is accompanied with the absorption of energy. The</b><br>
chemical reaction which involves the transfer of heat from the reaction system<br>
to the surroundings is called exothermic reaction. This means that, the reaction<br>
is accompanied with the evolution of heat. The energy profile diagrams for both<br>
endothermic and exothermic reactions are shown in Figure 4.2.<br>
<b>Figure 4.2 Energy profiles for the endothermic and exothermic reactions</b><br>
<b>An endothermic reaction has the products at a higher energy state than the</b><br>
reactants; that is, the internal energy of products is greater than that of the<br>
reactants (heat is absorbed from the surroundings). The change in enthalpy for<br>
endothermic reaction is positive (ΔH &gt; 0). An example of endothermic reaction<br>
is the dissolution of table salt (sodium chloride) in water to form an aqueous<br>
solution of sodium chloride.<br>
<b>NaCl(s)  +  (aq)</b><br>
<b>Na+(aq)  +  Cl−(aq)</b><br>
<b>ΔH = +3.90 kJ mol–1</b><br>
<b>In the exothermic reaction, the products are at a lower energy state than the</b><br>
reactants; that is, the internal energy of the reactants is at greater energy than<br>
that of the products. The change in enthalpy for exothermic reaction is negative<br>
(<br>
0)<br>

<b>D</b><br>
&lt;<br>
. An example of exothermic reaction is the combustion of methane to<br>
form carbon dioxide and water.<br>
<b>CH4(g)  +  2O2(g)</b><br>
<b>CO2(g)  +  2H2O(l)</b><br>
<b>ΔH = −802 kJ mol−1</b><br>

4.2<br>
<b>Enthalpy of reaction</b><br>
<b>The heat of reaction is the amount of heat absorbed or evolved when the</b><br>
reaction goes to completion. The amount of heat absorbed or evolved at constant<br>
temperature and pressure is called enthalpy (H) and the corresponding amount of<br>
heat change during the reaction is called enthalpy change<br>
(<br>
).<br>
<b>H  Several factors</b><br>
affect the quantity of heat released or absorbed during physical or chemical<br>
changes. These include the amounts of reactants or products, the physical states<br>
of the reactants and the products, temperature and pressure. Therefore, in writing<br>
a thermochemical equation, the amount of heat change in the reaction or process<br>
must be indicated. A thermochemical equation is a balanced stoichiometric<br>
chemical equation that includes the enthalpy change, ΔH. The equation must<br>
indicate the physical states of the reactants and the products as solid (s), liquid (l),<br>
aqueous (aq) and a gas (g), and must be balanced. The following are examples of<br>
thermochemical equations:<br>
<b>HNO3(aq)  +  NaOH(aq)</b><br>
<b>NaNO3(aq)  +  H2O(l)	 ΔH = −57.2 kJ mol−1</b><br>
<b>NaCl(s)  +  (aq)</b><br>
<b>Na+(aq)  +  Cl−(aq)</b><br>
<b>ΔH = +3.90 kJ mol–1</b><br>
<b>The above thermochemical equations are complete as they are balanced, they</b><br>
show physical states of the reactants and products as well as the enthalpy changes<br>
for the reactions.<br>
<b>The heat of reaction can be measured either at a constant volume or a constant</b><br>
pressure. However, most of the chemical reactions are carried out at constant<br>
pressure (atmospheric pressure). The magnitude of changes at a constant<br>
volume and constant pressure are different.  At constant volume, the heat change<br>
accompanying a chemical reaction is called a change in the internal energy<br>
(ΔH) because there is no work done. At constant pressure, the heat change<br>
accompanying a chemical reaction is called the enthalpy change (ΔH). The<br>
enthalpy change accompanying a chemical reaction can be measured accurately<br>
and is expressed as:<br>
<b>H =</b><br>
(4.4)<br>
It is not possible to measure the enthalpy (H) directly, only the enthalpy change<br>
(ΔH) can be measured. Since the value of the enthalpy change depends on<br>
temperature, pressure, concentration and physical states of the reactants, then<br>
the enthalpy changes are stated under standard conditions (that is, gases at 1 atm,<br>
solutions at unit concentration and substances in their standard states).<br>

<b>Enthalpy of solution (ΔHsol)</b><br>
<b>The enthalpy of solution is the change in enthalpy when one mole of a substance</b><br>
is dissolved in a large excess of solvent (infinite dilution) such that no further heat<br>
change occurs when more solvent is added to the solution. Infinite dilution is a<br>
solution that contains so much solvent that when more solvent is added, there is<br>
no change in concentration. For example, the enthalpies of solution of NaCl(s)<br>
and MgSO4(s) when added in water are:<br>
<b>NaCl(s) + (aq)</b><br>
<b>Na+ (aq) + Cl(aq)</b><br>
<b>ΔH = +390 kJ mol−1</b><br>
<b>MgSO4(s) + (aq)</b><br>
<b>Mg2+(aq) + SO4</b><br>
−2(aq)<br>
<b>ΔH = –84.8 kJ mol−1</b><br>
The concept of infinite dilution is included in the enthalpy of solution because<br>
if there is still a room for dilution, a further heat change may occur. The energy<br>
change in the solution process involves the following steps:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>Breaking of solute-solute attractions (endothermic), for example lattice</b><br>
energy in salts.<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Breaking of solvent-solvent attractions (endothermic) for example</b><br>
hydrogen bonding in water.<br>
(iii)	 Forming solvent–solute attraction (exothermic) in solvation.<br>
The magnitude of the enthalpy of solution is the summation of the energy changes<br>
involved in the solute-solute, solvent–solvent and solute–solvent interactions.<br>
<b>The enthalpy changes when one mole of a substance is dissolved in a solvent</b><br>
to form one litre (1 dm3) of solution at standard conditions (1 atm and 25 ºC) is<br>

4.2.2	 Measurement of heat of reaction by calorimetry<br>
<b>Calorimetry is the technique of measuring the amount of heat that may be</b><br>
generated (exothermic) or consumed (endothermic) by a reaction. The device<br>
used to measure the amount of heat of reaction is called calorimeter. This device<br>
consists of insulated walls which prevent the exchange of heat between the<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   136</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:01<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
137<br>
system and the surroundings. The measurement of heat of reaction is done by<br>
enclosing the reactants in the calorimeter and measure the temperature before<br>
and after the reaction. Then, the heat which is released or absorbed is determined<br>
from the temperature difference. There are different types of calorimeters, but the<br>
commonly used ones are water calorimeter and bomb calorimeter.<br>
<b>Water calorimeter</b><br>
<b>The apparatus consists of thermally insulated water bath in which a reaction</b><br>
chamber is placed. The thermometer and the stirrer are suspended into water bath<br>
through two openings of the lid. A pre-weighed amount of water is pored into the<br>
water bath and the initial temperature is recorded. The calorimetre and reactants<br>
are  left to equilibrate temperature. Then the reactants are mixed. The maximum<br>
or minimum temperature reached is<br>
recorded as the final temperature of<br>
the reaction. This is because, the heat<br>
released by the reaction is absorbed<br>
by water hence its temperature<br>
rises, and if the reaction absorbs<br>
heat, the temperature of water<br>
will decrease. Water calorimeter<br>
which is used for measuring heat<br>
of a reaction is shown in Figure 4.3.<br>
<b>The heat produced or absorbed is</b><br>
calculated from Equation 4.3.<br>
<b>Figure 4.3 The water calorimeter</b><br>
<b>Insulating lid</b><br>
<b>Insulating disc</b><br>
<b>Outer vessel</b><br>
<b>Water</b><br>
<b>Air space</b><br>

<b>Temperature correction during calorimetric measurement</b><br>
<b>For reactions that occur very fast, accurate measurements of temperature can</b><br>
be easily obtained using a simple calorimeter. For slow reactions, less accurate<br>
measurements of temperature are obtained due to loss of temperature to the<br>
surroundings. The correction for this temperature loss can be done by plotting the<br>
graph of temperature versus time. Figures 4.4 and 4.5 show the temperature versus<br>
time curves for exothermic and endothermic reactions, respectively. One reagent<br>
is placed in the simple calorimeter and the temperature is recorded up to about 4<br>
minutes which is the time for the equilibration of temperature. Then, the second<br>
reagent is added and the temperature is recorded in intervals until the maximum<br>
or minimum temperature is<br>
reached depending on the<br>
type of the reaction. The<br>
recording of temperature is<br>
done for several more minutes<br>
while stirring the mixture.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
temperature<br>
versus<br>
time curve is plotted from<br>
the recorded data. The lines<br>
are extrapolated to obtain<br>
the maximum or minimum<br>
temperature possible (point<br>
<b>C) in the absence of heat</b><br>
exchange between the system<br>
and the surroundings.<br>

<b>Bomb calorimeter (constant-volume calorimeter)</b><br>
<b>Bomb calorimeter is a common type of constant-volume calorimeter which</b><br>
is designed to measure accurately the enthalpy of combustion reactions. The<br>
measurement of enthalpy of combustion is very crucial for the determination of<br>
standard enthalpies of formation of many new compounds. The bomb calorimeter<br>
is illustrated in Figure 4.6. The calorimeter consists of a steel bomb which contains<br>
the reactants, a water bath in which the bomb is submerged, a thermometer, a<br>
motorised stirrer and ignition wires. A pre-weighed combustible sample is<br>
transfered to the bomb and then filled with oxygen gas. The bomb is then immersed<br>
in an insulated water bath which is fitted with a motorised stirrer and a thermometer.<br>
<b>The calorimeter is left for sometime to</b><br>
allow every component to equilibriate<br>
temperature. The initial temperature<br>
of the calorimeter is recorded. The<br>
sample is ignited by the electrical<br>
source and the combustion reaction<br>
takes place. As the reaction proceeds,<br>
the heat evolved rises the temperature<br>
of the system. The final temperature<br>
is then recorded. Since the mass of<br>
the sample and corresponding heat<br>
capacity are known, the measured<br>
<b>T</b><br>
<b>D</b><br>
is used to calculate the quantity<br>
of the heat released.<br>
<b>Figure 4.6 Bomb calorimeter</b><br>

4.2.3	 Hess’s Law of constant heat summation<br>
It is practically difficult to measure the heat for every reaction that takes place in<br>
a system. For some reactions, the measurement of heat may not even be feasible.<br>
<b>Therefore, heat of a reaction may be estimated from suitable combinations</b><br>
of compiled standard thermal data, usually in the form of standard heats of<br>
formation and heats of combustion. The method of calculating heats of reactions<br>
from measured values of heats of formation and combustion is based on the<br>
principle known as Hess’s Law of constant heat summation or simply Hess’s<br>
Law. In this section, you will learn to: explain the Hess’s Law, apply Hess’s Law<br>
in the calculations of heat changes, describe the Born Haber cycle and relate the<br>
heat of formation to bond energies.<br>
<b>The basis for the Hess’s Law</b><br>
<b>The Hess’s Law was named after a Russian Chemist and Doctor, Germaine</b><br>
<b>Henri Hess (1802–1850) who helped to formulate the early principles of</b><br>
thermochemistry. The Hess’s Law states that, the total enthalpy change of a<br>
chemical reaction is constant independent of the reaction path, but depends on<br>
the initial and final states of the reactants and the products. It was discussed<br>
previously that heat changes in chemical reactions are equal to the change in<br>
the internal energy (ΔE) or enthalpy (ΔH), depending on whether the reaction<br>
occurred at constant volume or pressure. Since ΔE and ΔH are the functions of<br>
the state of the system, the heat evolved or absorbed in a reaction depends on the<br>
initial and final states of the system and not the way the change takes place.  This<br>
forms the basis for the Hess’s Law. An illustration of the Hess’s Law is shown in<br>
Figure 4.7.<br>
<b>Energetics</b><br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   143</b><br>

<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>

<b>Student’s Book Form Five and Six</b><br>

place directly by route 1 or indirectly by route 2, through intermediates B and C.<br>
According to the Hess’s Law, the enthalpy change in route 1, ΔH1, is the same as<br>
the enthalpy changes in route 2. That is:<br>

<b>Applications of the Hess’s Law</b><br>
<b>Hess’s Law is useful in determining the enthalpy changes such as enthalpy of</b><br>
formation, transition and enthalpy of reaction.<br>
<b>Determination of enthalpy of formation of substances</b><br>
<b>The heat of the formation of substances, which can otherwise not be obtained</b><br>
experimentally, can be determined using the Hess’s Law. For example, it is very<br>
difficult to set an experiment to prepare compounds such as methane or benzene<br>
from their constituent elements. Therefore, it is not possible to measure the heat<br>
of formation of these compounds directly, but indirectly by using Hess’s Law.<br>

<b>Determination of enthalpy of reaction (ΔHrxn)</b><br>
<b>The enthalpy change of reaction is the amount of energy (or heat) absorbed or</b><br>
released in a chemical reaction. Enthalpies or heat changes of many chemical<br>
reactions that are difficult to be measured directly are determined using Hess’s<br>
<b>Law. For example, the Hess’s Law can be used to determine enthalpy of</b><br>
dimerisation of nitrogen dioxide (NO2).<br>

4.2.4	 Bond energy<br>
<b>Chemical reactions involve bond breaking and bond forming processes. The</b><br>
formation of a bond between two atoms is accompanied with the release of energy.<br>
<b>The same amount of energy is absorbed when the bond between the two atoms</b><br>
is broken. The bond energy is the average amount of energy which is required<br>
to break one mole of bonds of a particular type in gaseous covalent molecules.<br>
<b>When a chemical reaction occurs, molecular bonds are broken and other bonds</b><br>
are formed to make different molecules. The bonds of two water molecules, for<br>
example, are broken to form hydrogen and oxygen.<br>
1<br>
481.0kJmol<br>
<b>H = +</b><br>
<b>This implies that, the heat of 481 kJ is absorbed when two moles of water</b><br>
molecules break into two hydrogen molecules and one molecule of oxygen.. The<br>
same amount of heat is released when two moles of water molecules are formed<br>
that is – 481kJ.<br>
<b>Bond energies can be used to determine the enthalpy of reaction using Equation:</b><br>
rxn<br>
(bonds broken)<br>
(bonds formed)<br>

4.2.5	 Born–Haber Cycle<br>
<b>Born–Haber Cycle is a thermochemical cycle named after two German Physical</b><br>
<b>Chemists Max Born (1882–1970) and Fritz Haber (1868–1932) who devised it in</b><br>
1919. This cycle helps to understand and determine the lattice energies of ionic<br>
solids. It is the application of Hess’s Law to the enthalpy of formation of ionic<br>
solids at 25 oC. Lattice energy is the energy released when 1 mole of the ionic<br>
compound is formed from its gaseous ions under a standard state.<br>
<b>The terms ionisation energy, electron affinity, enthalpy of  sublimation, and</b><br>
enthalpy of dissociation are important in discussing Born–Haber Cycle.<br>
Enthalpies of sublimation and dissociation have been discussed in Section 4.2.1.<br>
<b>Ionisation energy (IE) is the energy required to remove completely one mole of</b><br>
electrons from one mole of gaseous atoms or ions.<br>
<b>Electron affinity (EA) is the energy change when one mole of electrons is added</b><br>
to one mole of gaseous atoms or ions.<br>
<b>Sodium chloride, an ionic solid will be used as an example to discuss the Born–</b><br>
<b>Haber Cycle. The formation of one mole sodium chloride crystals from solid</b><br>
sodium and gaseous chlorine is represented by the reaction:<br>
<b>Na(s) + 1</b><br>
2Cl2(g)<br>
<b>NaCl(s)                                    ΔHrxn = – 411 kJ mol–1</b><br>
<b>An illustration of the Born-Haber Cycle for the formation of NaCl is shown in</b><br>
Figure 4.8.<br>

<b>One mole of solid sodium is converted into gaseous sodium atoms. The energy</b><br>
change is positive because heat must be supplied to convert solid into a gas.<br>
∆H°sub = + 107.3 kJ mol-1<br>
<b>Step2: Dissociation of Cl2</b><br>
<b>Gaseous chlorine molecules (Cl2) dissociate into one mole of gaseous chlorine</b><br>
atoms. During this processes energy must be absorbed in order to break the bonds<br>
between chlorine atoms. Therefore, the energy change is positive. Because half<br>
a mole of chlorine molecules are being broken, the bond energy is also halved.<br>

<b>Step 4: Gain of an electron</b><br>
<b>One mole of chlorine atoms gain electrons to form chloride ions. The energy</b><br>
released is the electron affinity (EA) of chlorine. The first EA is negative.<br>
<b>Cl(g)  +  e–</b><br>
<b>Cl–(g)</b><br>
<b>ΔH°eg   =  - 348.6 kJ mol–1</b><br>
<b>Step 5: Formation of NaCl(s) from Na+(g) and Cl–(g)</b><br>
One mole of Na+(g) combine with one mole of Cl−(g) to form one mole of NaCl(s).<br>
The energy is released in this process and is called the lattice energy (LE). The<br>
process is exothermic because a large amount of heat is released when sodium<br>
and chloride ions coalesce to form a crystalline lattice.<br>
s<br>
<b>ΔH°, LE, = – 786.2 kJ mol–1</b><br>
<b>Lattice energy is determined indirectly through the application of Hess's Law</b><br>
provided that ΔH°f of the product is known. Thus from the Hess's Law of heat<br>
summation,<br>
<b>ΔH°f(NaCl) = ΔH°sub + ΔH°dis + ΔH°ion + ΔH°eg + LE</b><br>
<b>LE = ΔH°f – (ΔH°sub + ΔH°dis + ΔH°ion + ΔH°eg)</b><br>

<b>Five</b><br>
<b>Chemical equilibrium</b><br>
<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
The chemistry of the equilibrium state is quite interesting. You may be interested<br>
in getting to know the equilibrium concentration, equilibrium constant, and how<br>
factors such as pressure, temperature and concentration affects the formation of<br>
products. Therefore, the focus of this chapter, is to let you gain an insight on<br>
reversible reactions, and equilibrium constants. In addition, you will learn about<br>
the factors that affect the position of chemical equilibrium, and the industrial<br>
application of Le Châtelier’s Principle.<br>
5.1<br>
<b>Reversible reactions</b><br>
<b>A reversible reaction is a chemical response in which the reactants interact to</b><br>
form products which in turn react together to give back the reactants. Reversible<br>
reactions in a closed vessel reach equilibrium point in which the concentrations of<br>
the reactants and products no longer change. A reversible reaction is denoted by<br>
a double half headed arrows (<br>
) pointing in opposite directions of a chemical<br>
equation.<br>
<b>An example of a hypothetical reversible reaction in which two gaseous reactants</b><br>
interact to give two gaseous products in a closed vessel can be represented by the<br>
equation:<br>
<b>The forward reaction starts with reactants A and B and proceeds to products C</b><br>
and D. The concentrations of A and B decrease while those of C and D increase<br>
gradually. After some time, C and D react in a backward direction to form A and<br>
B. Since the rate of chemical reaction is directly proportional to the concentration<br>
of the reactants, the initial rate of forward reaction will be higher than that of<br>
the backward reaction. Therefore, the rate of the forward reaction decreases and<br>
the rate of reverse reaction increases. This process will continue until a state is<br>
reached where the rates of forward and backward reactions are equal. At this<br>
point, the reaction is said to attain a state of equilibrium. A reversible reaction<br>
whereby the forward and backward reactions occur at the same rate and the<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   158</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:03<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
159<br>
concentrations of the reactants and products do not change with time is called an<br>
equilibrium reaction.<br>
<b>Consider a chemical reaction between H2 and I2 to form HI and the decomposition</b><br>
of HI to form H2 and I2:<br>
<b>H2(g) + I2(g)                 2HI(g)</b><br>
<b>The changes of rates of forward and backward reactions are presented in Figure</b><br>
5.1.<br>
Figure 5.1 A graph showing the change of rates of forward and backward reactions<br>
<b>As shown in Figure 5.1, the state of equilibrium is reached when the forward</b><br>
reaction proceeds at the same rate as the reverse reaction. Moreover, it should be<br>
noted that the rates of the forward and backward reactions are not zero but equal.<br>
<b>Such a state is known as dynamic equilibrium because the forward and reverse</b><br>
reactions do not stop but proceed simultaneously at equal rates. As a result,<br>
the concentration of the components remains unchanged. One of the examples<br>
of dynamic equilibrium is the reaction of sulphur dioxide with oxygen to give<br>
sulphur trioxide:<br>
2SO2(g) + O2(g)                 2SO3(g)<br>
In this reaction, molecules of SO2 and O2 react to yield SO3. Meanwhile, molecules<br>
of SO3 decompose to give SO2 and O2. At equilibrium, all the three types of<br>
molecules are present in the reaction mixture and the rate of formation of SO3<br>
from SO2 and O2 is equal to the rate of decomposition of SO3 to SO2 and O2.<br>

5.1.1 	 Characteristics of chemical equilibria<br>
<b>All chemical equilibria exhibit the following characteristics:</b><br>
(a)<br>
<b>They are characterised by constancy of concentrations. When the chemical</b><br>
equilibrium is established in a closed vessel at constant temperature, the<br>
concentrations of the various molecules in the reaction mixture become<br>
constant. The reaction mixture at equilibrium is called equilibrium mixture<br>
and the concentrations of chemical species at equilibrium are called<br>
equilibrium concentrations.<br>
(b)<br>
<b>They can be initiated from either side of the reaction. The state of</b><br>
equilibrium of a reversible reaction can be approached by starting with<br>
either the reactant(s) or the product(s).<br>
(c)<br>
<b>Chemical equilibrium cannot be attained in an open vessel. The equilibrium</b><br>
can be established only if the reaction vessel is closed and neither part of<br>
the reactants nor of the products enters or escapes the system.<br>
(d)<br>
A catalyst does not change the equilibrium point of the chemical reaction.<br>
<b>When added to a reaction system, the catalyst alters the rate of both forward</b><br>
and reverse reactions at equal rates.<br>
(e)<br>
<b>The value of the equilibrium constant does not depend on the initial</b><br>
concentrations of the reactants. It remains the same when the concentrations<br>
of the reacting species are varied over a wide range. However, the<br>
equilibrium constants vary with temperature.<br>
5.1.2 	 The law of mass action<br>
For any chemical reaction to occur, reacting particles must first collide in a proper<br>
orientation and with sufficient energy. Thus, not all particles which collide in a<br>
reaction vessel will react. Only a fraction of the total number of particles in a<br>
reaction vessel which fulfills the stated conditions will react to give products.<br>
This fraction is called the active mass of the reacting particles. The rate of a<br>
chemical reaction is found to be proportional to this mass. The statement that<br>
the rate of chemical reaction is proportional to the active mass of the reactants<br>
is called the law of mass action. In more precise words, the law states that at<br>
constant temperature and presure, the rate of a chemical reaction is directly<br>
proportional to the product of molar concentrations of the reactants each raised<br>
to the power equal to the corresponding stoichiometric coefficient which appear<br>
in the balanced chemical equation. Consider the general chemical reaction of the<br>
type:<br>

is R2∝[C]c[D]d as represented by Equation 5.1, where, [  ] represents molar<br>
concentrations of individual species, and R1 and R2 represent the rates for forward<br>
and reverse reactions, respectively.<br>

<b>In order to obtain an expression for the equilibrium constant in terms of</b><br>
concentrations, Kc and its numerical value, the following steps should be followed:<br>
(a)<br>
Write the balanced chemical equation for the equilibrium reaction.<br>
(b)<br>
<b>Place the concentration of products on top of the fraction (numerator)</b><br>
followed by placing the concentration of the reactants on the bottom of the<br>
fraction (denominator).<br>
(c)<br>
<b>Raise the concentration of the products and the reactants to the power of</b><br>
their respective stoichiometric coefficients.<br>
(d)<br>
Compute the value of Kc by substituting the given data.<br>
<b>Note: If any of the reactants or products are present as solid or liquid, their</b><br>
concentrations are equal to one because they are in their pure form and therefore,<br>
they are not included in the equilibrium constant expression.<br>

5.2.2 	 Equilibrium constant in terms of partial pressure, Kp<br>
<b>When all reactants and products are gases, the equilibrium constant can also be</b><br>
expressed in terms of partial pressure. Assuming that the gases in the system<br>
behave ideally, then the ideal gas equation, PV = nRT applies; where, P is the<br>
pressure of the gas, V is the volume of the gas, n is the number of moles of the<br>
gas, R is the universal gas constant and T is the temperature.<br>
<b>Dividing both sides of the equation by V, gives</b><br>
<b>RT</b><br>

is the<br>
molar concentration, C. Substituting<br>
<b>V</b><br>
n<br>
by C the equation becomes P = CRT.<br>
Thus, partial pressure of the gas in the equilibrium mixture is directly proportional<br>
to its molar concentration at a given temperature. Therefore, partial pressure can<br>
be used in the expression of equilibrium constant instead of concentration.<br>

5.2.3 	 Reaction quotient<br>
<b>In the previous sections the equilibrium constants, Kc and Kp in terms of</b><br>
equilibrium concentrations and partial pressures, respectively were discussed.<br>
<b>For the reaction which is not at equilibrium, the ratio of the product of the</b><br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   168</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:04<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
169<br>
concentrations or partial pressures of the products raised to their stoichiometric<br>
coefficients to that of the product of the concentrations or partial pressures of the<br>
reactants raised to their stoichiometric coefficients can be less or greater than Kc<br>
or Kp values at a given temperature. Therefore, it is difficult to tell the direction of<br>
equilibrium and the values of the products and reactants in the reaction that is not<br>
at equilibrium. The reaction quotient, Q, is used for this purpose and it is defined<br>
as the ratio of the product of product concentrations to the product of reactant<br>
concentrations raised to their respective stoichiometric coefficients when the<br>
reaction has not attained equilibrium. It measures the relative amount of products<br>
and reactants present in the course of reaction at any instant. Reaction quotients<br>
in terms of concentration and partial pressure, Qc and Qp are obtained in the same<br>
way as the equilibrium constants Kc and Kp, except that the conditions must be<br>
other than equilibrium. They aid in understanding the position of equilibrium by<br>
comparing with equilibrium constants Kc and Kp.<br>
f<br>
f<br>
<b>If</b><br>
c<br>
c<br>
<b>K</b><br>
<b>Q ></b><br>
, it means that more products are present than there would be at<br>
equilibrium. The equilibrium will shift to the left to produce more reactants<br>
from the excess products.<br>
f<br>
f<br>
<b>If</b><br>
c<br>
c<br>
<b>K</b><br>
<b>Q <</b><br>
, it means there are more reactants than would be at equilibrium.<br>
<b>The equilibrium shifts to the right to produce more products from the</b><br>
excess reactants.<br>
f<br>
f<br>
<b>If</b><br>
c<br>
c<br>
<b>K</b><br>
<b>Q =</b><br>
, it means the reaction has attained equilibrium. Therefore, there<br>
is no tendency to form either more products or more reactants.<br>

<b>Variations in the forms of the reaction quotient and the equilibrium constant</b><br>
<b>The values of the reaction quotient (Q) and the equilibrium constant (K) vary</b><br>

respectively.<br>

<b>Koverall = K1×K2×K3 - - -</b><br>
(b)<br>
<b>The Q and K for a forward reaction, is the reciprocal of the Q and K for the</b><br>
reverse reaction, respectively.<br>

<b>Kp = Kc × (RT)(c+d) – (a+b)</b><br>
(5.7)<br>
The term (c + d) – (a + b) in Equation 5.7 represents the difference between the<br>
total number of moles of the products and the reactants in a balanced chemical<br>
equation and can be symbolised as ∆n. Introducing ∆n into Equation 5.7 leads to<br>
Equation 5.8.<br>
<b>Kp = Kc × (RT)∆n</b><br>
(5.8)<br>
<b>When the total number of moles of the products is equal to the total number of</b><br>
moles of the reactants, that is when ∆n = 0, then Kc = Kp.<br>
<b>Example 5.6</b><br>
<b>The reaction between sulphur dioxide and nitrogen dioxide is represented by the</b><br>
following chemical equation:<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   172</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:05<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
173<br>
If the Kc for this reaction is 2.25, calculate the value of Kp.<br>
<b>Solution</b><br>
Applying Equation 5.8, Kp = Kc × (RT)∆n. From the balanced chemical equation,<br>
the total number of moles of products is equal to the total number of moles of the<br>
reactants, thus, ∆n = 0, implying that, Kp = Kc × (RT)0   = 2.25. Therefore, Kp for<br>
the reaction is 2.25.<br>
<b>Units of Kc and Kp</b><br>
As seen from the previous discussions, the equilibrium constant can be expressed<br>
in terms of concentrations and partial pressures. Whether expressed in terms of<br>
concentration or partial pressure its units will depend on the difference in the<br>
total number of moles of the products and reactants which give three possible<br>
cases as described hereunder.<br>
<b>Case 1: When the total number of moles of products is equal to the total number</b><br>
of moles of reactants, the values of Kc and Kp have no units. For example, the<br>
reaction between nitrogen and oxygen gases to give nitric oxide gas is:<br>
<b>The Kp and Kc for this reaction can be expressed as:</b><br>
2<br>

<b>Kc and Kp for systems involving different phases at equilibrium</b><br>
<b>The previous discussions on Kc  and Kp  focused only on homogeneous equilibria</b><br>
in which all the components of the reaction are in the same phase. When the<br>
components are in different phases, the system reaches heterogeneous equilibrium.<br>
<b>Experimental results show that the position of a heterogeneous equilibrium does</b><br>
not depend on the amount of pure solids or liquids present in the system. A pure<br>
solid or liquid always has the same concentration at a given temperature, that is,<br>
the same number of moles per its unit volume. Moreover, since a solid’s volume<br>
changes very little with temperature, its concentration also changes very little.<br>
<b>For these reasons, the concentration of pure solids is constant and the same</b><br>
argument applies to the concentration of pure liquids which is assumed to be<br>
one. Since the concentrations and partial pressures significantly change as the<br>
reactants approach equilibrium, the terms for pure liquids and solids from the<br>
reaction quotient are eliminated. This is done by incorporating their constant<br>
concentrations or partial pressures into a rearranged reaction quotient.<br>

5.3<br>
<b>Factors affecting chemical equilibrium</b><br>
One of the remarkable features of equilibrium systems is their ability to return to<br>
equilibrium after the change in conditions which were involved in its establishment.<br>
<b>For this reason, when a chemical system at equilibrium is subjected to a</b><br>
“disturbance”, it regains its equilibrium by undergoing a net reaction that reduces<br>
the effect of the “disturbance”. The changes in the concentration, pressure, and<br>
temperature commonly disturb the equilibrium system.<br>
The general rule that helps to predict the direction in which an equilibrium reaction<br>
will move is known as Le Châtelier’s Principle. The Le Châtelier’s Principle<br>
states that, if an external disturbance is applied to a system at equilibrium, the<br>
equilibrium shifts in the opposite direction to offset the change. This principle<br>
will be used to assess the effect of such changes.<br>
5.3.1  The effects of change in concentration<br>
<b>The effect of concentration on the position of a chemical equilibrium can be</b><br>
demonstrated by the dissolution of potassium dichromate in water as per the<br>
reaction:<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   176</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:05<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
177<br>
At the beginning of the reaction the solution is orange in colour due to the high<br>
concentration of Cr2O7<br>
2− ions. When sodium hydroxide solution is added to this<br>
system, the colour of the solution changes from orange to yellow. The disturbance<br>
applied to the equilibrium system is an increase in concentration of OH− (from<br>
sodium hydroxide solution). To offset this disturbance, some H+ ions will react<br>
with the added OH− ions and the equilibrium will shift from left to right causing<br>
an increase in the concentration of CrO4<br>
2− ions, hence yellow colour. Conversely,<br>
the addition of dilute HCl solution to the system at equilibrium will increase H+<br>
ions concentration. To offset this disturbance, some CrO4<br>
2− ions react with H+<br>
ions and hence the equilibrium shifts to the left making the colour of the solution<br>
orange.<br>
5.3.2  The effects of change in pressure<br>
There are three possible ways to change the pressure of a reaction system involving<br>
gaseous components at a given temperature namely: adding or removing gaseous<br>
reactant(s) or product(s) at constant volume, adding an inert gas at constant<br>
volume, and changing the volume of the container.<br>
<b>The effect of adding or removing gaseous reactant(s) or product(s) from the</b><br>
equilibrium mixture at constant volume can be explained by considering the ideal<br>
gas equation,<br>
or<br>
n<br>
<b>PV</b><br>
nRT<br>
<b>P</b><br>
<b>RT</b><br>
<b>V</b><br>
=<br>
=<br>
<b>Since pressure is directly proportional to the molar concentration, n/V, of the</b><br>
gas in the reaction mixture, an increase in pressure is similar to the increase<br>
in the concentration of the gas in the reaction mixture. Similarly, a decrease in<br>
pressure decreases the concentration of the gas in the reaction mixture. The effect<br>
of pressure on the position of equilibrium can be noticed only when there is<br>
a change in number of moles between the left hand side (LHS) and the right<br>
hand side (RHS) of the reaction. An increase in pressure shifts the position of<br>
equilibrium from the side with more number of moles to the side with less number<br>
of moles. The decrease in pressure has an opposite effect, i.e., shifts the position<br>
of the equilibrium from the side with less number of moles to the side with more<br>
of moles.<br>
<b>Chemical equilibrium</b><br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   177</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:05<br>

<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>

<b>Physical Chemistry</b><br>

178<br>
<b>Consider the formation of carbon monoxide in the steam reforming process:</b><br>
The LHS of this reaction has 2 moles while the RHS has 4 moles. The total number<br>
of moles of products is higher than the total number of moles of the reactants.<br>
<b>Therefore, the forward reaction is favoured by a decrease in pressure because</b><br>
it proceeds with an increase in volume. The increase in pressure will force the<br>
equilibrium to shift to the left to offset the disturbance by decreasing the volume<br>
of the reaction mixture. In addition, reducing the volume of the container due to<br>
the increase in pressure shifts the equilibrium to the side with fewer number of<br>
moles of the gas since fewer moles occupy the smallest volume. Adding an inert<br>
gas to the equilibrium mixture will have no effects on the equilibrium position.<br>
The inert gas increases the total pressure of the system but does not affect the<br>
partial pressure of the reactants or products.<br>
5.3.3  The effects of change in temperature<br>
The effect of temperature on the position of chemical equilibrium depends on the<br>
type of the reaction, that is whether the reaction is endothermic or exothermic. For<br>
endothermic reactions, an increase in temperature favours the forward reaction<br>
while a decrease in temperature favours the backward reaction. Conversely, for<br>
exothermic reactions the increase in temperature favours the backward reaction<br>
while the decrease in temperature favours the forward reaction. This is according<br>
to the van’t Hoff’s Law, i.e., the increase in temperature causes the increase in<br>
rate of endothermic reactions.<br>
Aqueous solution of cobalt(II) chloride acidified with concentrated hydrochloric<br>
acid can save as an example of the effect of temperature on the position of<br>
equilibrium. When concentrated hydrochloric acid is added to a pinkish aqueous<br>
solution of cobalt(II) chloride, the solution turns blue due to the displacement of<br>
the H2O molecules by the chloride ions:<br>
<b>The temperature of the solution is lowered when concentrated hydrochloric acid</b><br>
is added. This implies that the formation of complex [CoCl4]2− is endothermic. On<br>
heating, the equilibrium shifts to the right and therefore, the solution turns blue.<br>
Cooling, favours the exothermic reaction. That is, the reaction reverses towards<br>
formation of the [Co(H2O)6]2+ and the solution turns pinkish.<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   178</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:05<br>

<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
179<br>
5.3.4  The effects of catalyst<br>
A catalyst alters the rate of a reaction by providing an alternative mechanism of a<br>
relatively lower activation energy. The lowering of activation energy affects both<br>
forward and reverse reactions to the same rate. It can therefore be concluded that,<br>
the presence of a catalyst neither alters the equilibrium constant nor shifts the<br>
position of an equilibrium system.<br>
5.4<br>
<b>Application of Le Châtelier’s Principle in industrial processes</b><br>
In industrial processes, chemists and chemical engineers basically strive to choose<br>
favourable conditiohydrolyns that maximise yields of the desired products. The<br>
synthesis of ammonia by the Haber process and sulphuric acid by the Contact<br>
process are among industrial processes in which Le Châtelier’s Principle finds its<br>
application. Below are the chemical equations for the two processes.<br>
1<br>
mol<br>
kJ<br>
4.<br>
92<br>
=<br>
<b>H</b><br>
1<br>
mol<br>
kJ<br>
197<br>
=<br>
<b>H</b><br>
For the contact process, the sulphur trioxide gas formed undergoes hydrolysis to<br>
form sulphuric acid, H2SO4. But the reaction between sulphur trioxide and water<br>
is extremely exothermic such that it vapourises all the acid formed. Therefore,<br>
to safely obtain the yield, the sulphur trioxide gas is absorbed in 98% sulphuric<br>
acid to form oleum, H2S2O7, which undergoes hydrolysis to form concentrated<br>
sulphuric acid.<br>
<b>In both Haber and Contact processes, the reactions are reversible, exothermic</b><br>
and proceed with a decrease in volume. For maximum production of ammonia<br>
and sulphur trioxide gases, the reactants are made to react at higher pressure<br>
and lower temperature. These conditions favour forward reactions. However, the<br>
decrease in temperature lowers the rate of reaction which in turn causes a rise in<br>
the production cost. To offset this effect, catalysts are used in both cases to speed<br>
up the rate of reactions. At the industrial scale, the mass production of ammonia<br>
requires a temperature of 500 ºC, a pressure of 200 to 1000 atm and the use of<br>
iron with aluminium oxide catalyst. For the maximum conversion of sulphur<br>
dioxide to about 95% sulphuric acid, vanadium(V) oxide catalyst, a pressure of 2<br>
atm and a temperature of 450 ºC are employed.<br>

<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Six</b><br>
<b>Chemical kinetics</b><br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
<b>Chemical kinetics entail aspects that describe the dynamics and characteristics</b><br>
of a chemical reaction. It helps to control the reaction conditions to obtain<br>
products as quickly and economically as possible. For example, how quickly a<br>
medicine is able to work or how quickly the ozone is depleted can be explained<br>
by chemical kinetics. The factors that characterise a chemical reaction from its<br>
minute processes, energy changes, and the speed at which the chemical reaction<br>
progresses are important. In this chapter therefore, you will learn about the rate<br>
of chemical reactions, rate laws, experimental determination of  rate laws, orders<br>
of reactions, factors affecting rates of reactions, and reaction mechanisms.<br>
6.1<br>
<b>The rate of chemical reactions</b><br>
<b>Any chemical reaction can be presented as:</b><br>
<b>Reactants</b><br>
<b>Products</b><br>
It can be deduced from this equation that in the course of a reaction, reactants are<br>
consumed while products are formed. The progress of a reaction can therefore<br>
be monitored by either measuring the decrease in concentration of the reactants<br>
or the increase in concentration of the products. For a simple reaction in which<br>
<b>A molecules are converted to B molecules, the following general equation may</b><br>
be used:<br>
<b>A</b><br>
<b>B</b><br>
Initially, only A molecules are present. As time passes, the number of molecules<br>
of A decreases while the number of molecules of B increases. Graphically, this<br>
reaction can be represented by Figure 6.1.<br>

<b>Practically, the progress of a chemical reaction can be followed by measuring</b><br>
any property which is related to the concentration of the reactant(s) or product(s)<br>
at a given time interval, for example, measurement of pressure and volume<br>
and change in mass of the system for gaseous reactions. This can also be done<br>
by conductivity or pH measurements for reactions involving ions, absorbance<br>
measurements for coloured compounds and optical measurements for chiral<br>
compounds. In any chemical reaction, the observed change in the concentration<br>
of the reactants or products per unit time is referred to as the rate of the reaction.<br>

<b>B, the rate can be expressed as:</b><br>

6.2<br>
<b>Rate laws</b><br>
When chemical reactions are considered to be irreversible processes, it is always<br>
found that the rate at which they proceed to give products depends on the<br>
concentrations of the reactants. The increase in the concentration of reactants<br>
increases the rate of the reactions and vice versa. However, chemical reactions can<br>
be reversible and after a period of time, enough products accumulate so that the<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   188</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:07<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
189<br>
reverse reaction becomes important. Dealing with the rate of reversible reactions<br>
is always very challenging. To avoid the complication, this book focuses on the<br>
rate of chemical reactions under the conditions where the reverse reaction makes<br>
negligible contribution.<br>
Consider conditions where the reverse reaction makes no significant contribution.<br>
The relationship that exists between the rate of the reaction and the concentration<br>
of the reactants can be expressed mathematically. For example, the reaction<br>
represented by the equation;<br>
its rate of reaction is expressed as:<br>
m<br>
n<br>
k<br>
]<br>
<b>B</b><br>
[<br>
]<br>
<b>A</b><br>
[<br>
<b>Rate =</b><br>
(6.4)<br>
Equation 6.4 shows that the rate of a reaction is directly proportional to the initial<br>
concentration of the reactants raised to factors n and m, where n and m can be zero,<br>
small integers or simple fractions. The Equation 6.4 is called a rate law. Thus, the<br>
rate law is a mathematical expression which shows how the rate of a chemical<br>
reaction depends on the concentration of the reactants. The proportionality<br>
constant, k , is called the rate constant. The rate law can be used to calculate the<br>
rate of the reaction if the concentration of the reactants and values of n, m, and k<br>
are known. The rate law of a given reaction can be obtained experimentally but<br>
not from a balanced chemical equation. This is because the values of n and m are<br>
not obtained from a chemical equation although sometimes they can be equal to<br>
the values of the stoichiometric coefficients.<br>

rate law therefore, has the form,<br>

6.3<br>
<b>Order of reactions</b><br>
<b>From Section 6.2, the rate of chemical reaction depends on the concentration</b><br>
of reactants raised to a certain numerical value. The exponent to which the<br>
concentration of a reactant is raised to in the rate law is known as the order of a<br>
reaction. The order of a reaction can be zero, small integers or simple fractions.<br>
For a general reaction,<br>
aA  +  bB  +  eE              Products<br>
the rate law takes the form:<br>

overall order of the reaction.<br>

For the known values of rate, k and [A], the order of a reaction (n) can be determined<br>
and it can be 0, 1 or 2. When n = 0, the reaction is said to be a zero order, when<br>
n = 1 the reaction is first-order and for n = 2 the reaction is second-order. In<br>
this section, zero, first, and second order reactions are dealt with. Generally, the<br>
overall order of reaction is the sum of powers to which the concentration terms<br>
of reactants are raised in the rate law equation for a particular chemical reaction.<br>

<b>Half-life for zero-order reactions</b><br>
Figure 6.1 shows that as the chemical reaction proceeds, the concentration of the<br>
reactant A decreases. The time at which the concentration of A is half the initial<br>
value gives a new measure of a rate of reaction. The time required for the initial<br>
concentration of the reactant(s) to decrease to half of its initial concentration is<br>
called half-life and is often expressed as t . The half-life for zero-order reaction<br>
can be determined from its integrated rate law (Equation 6.7) where from the<br>
definition, half-life, t  is obtained when<br>

6.4<br>
<b>Experimental determination of rate laws</b><br>
<b>The rate law for a chemical reaction which involves only one reactant can be</b><br>
determined experimentally by changing the concentrations of the reactant and<br>
measuring the initial rates of the reaction. The obtained initial rates and the new<br>
concentrations are compared with the previous initial rates and concentrations.<br>
For example, if the reaction is first-order with respect to reactant A, then doubling<br>
the concentration of A will double the rate of the reaction. Moreover, if the<br>
reaction is second-order with respect to A, then the rate is proportional to [A]2<br>
and doubling the concentration of A will cause the rate to increase by the factor<br>
of 4.<br>
<b>Reactions involving more than one reactants require a different approach for</b><br>
the determination of the rate law. The commonly used approach is to keep the<br>
concentration of one reactant constant while altering the concentration of the<br>
second reactant.<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   206</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:09<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
207<br>
<b>Consider the reaction:</b><br>
<b>A  +  B</b><br>
<b>Products</b><br>
<b>When [A]o = 0.001 mol dm-3 and [B]o = 1 mol dm-3, as the reaction proceeds, the</b><br>
concentration of A decreases significantly whereas that of B remains approximately<br>
constant. Under this condition, where [B] is much larger than [A], it can be<br>
assumed that throughout the reaction, [B]t = [B]o. Therefore, considering k[B]m =<br>
k′, the rate law; Rate = k[A]n[B]m has the form, Rate = k′[A]n which implies that<br>
under this condition, the rate is determined by the concentration of A. From this<br>
rate law, the order of the reaction with respect to A can be determined. Similarly,<br>
when  [A]o = 1 mol dm-3 and [B]o = 0.001 mol dm-3, the rate of reaction will<br>
depend on the concentration of B as the concentration of A is considered to remain<br>
approximately constant. Considering k[A]n = k″, the rate law, Rate = k[A]n[B]m<br>
has the form, Rate = k″[B]m, which implies that under this condition, the rate of<br>
the reaction depends on the concentration of B and its order of reaction can be<br>
determined. An example for the determination of the rate law, order of reaction<br>
and rate constant, k is given in Example 6.7.<br>

<b>Activity 6.1</b><br>
6.5<br>
<b>Factors affecting the rate of chemical reactions</b><br>
The rate of chemical reactions is influenced by the nature of chemical substances<br>
and other factors such as concentration, pressure, temperature, surface area,<br>
catalyst and light. A discussion on the effect of these factors on the rate of<br>
chemical reaction is presented in the following sub-sections.<br>
6.5.1 	 Concentration of the reactants<br>
From the expression of the rate law, the rate of a reaction is proportional to the<br>
concentration of reactants. It is also qualitatively agreed that for a chemical<br>
reaction to occur, reacting particles (molecules) must first come into contact or<br>
collide. Based on these two arguments, it can be concluded that, there is a greater<br>
chance that molecules will collide more in a system where particles (molecules)<br>
are in higher concentration than in the system where they are less concentrated.<br>
The effect of the concentration on the rate of a reaction can be demonstrated by<br>
the reaction between sodium thiosulphate, Na2S2O3 and potassium iodide, KI in<br>
acidic solution (Activity 6.1).<br>
<b>Effects of reactant(s) concentration on the rate of reaction</b><br>
<b>Requirements: 	 Beakers, pipette, stop watch, 0.10 M Na2S2O3, 0.20 M KI</b><br>
and 1.0 M H2SO4 solutions, distilled H2O, starch solution and<br>
dropper.<br>
<b>Procedure</b><br>
1.<br>
Measure 10 cm3 of 0.1 mol dm-3 Na2S2O3 solution into a 100 cm3 beaker.<br>
<b>Add 10 cm3 of 1.0 M H2SO4 solution followed by two drops of starch</b><br>
solution in the same beaker.<br>
2.<br>
Measure 10 cm3 of 0.20 mol dm-3 KI solution.<br>
3.<br>
<b>Pour the KI solution into the beaker containing a mixture of Na2S2O3, starch</b><br>
solution and sulphuric acid and immediately start the stop watch. Record<br>
the time taken for the blue-black colour to appear.<br>
4.<br>
<b>Repeat steps 1–3 but this time with the decreasing concentration of Na2S2O3</b><br>
solution by measuring 5 cm3 of the 0.1 mol dm-3 Na2S2O3 solution, and add<br>
distilled water to make it 10 cm3.<br>
<b>Question</b><br>
Compare the two observations.<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   210</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:10<br>

6.5.2 	 Temperature<br>
Temperature is an important factor for many life processes such as combustion,<br>
cooking, rusting, and all biological processes. The effect of temperature can<br>
be explained in terms of the average kinetic energy of the reacting particles.<br>
<b>Increasing temperature of the system, increases the average kinetic energy of</b><br>
the constituent particles. As the average kinetic energy increases, the particles<br>
move faster and collide more frequently per unit time. It is worth noting that the<br>
temperature (in kelvin) is proportional to the kinetic energy of the particles in a<br>
substance, and therefore the reaction rates of all reactions increase with increasing<br>
temperature. Similarly, the reaction rates of virtually all reactions decrease with<br>
decrease in temperature. For example, refrigeration of food retards the rate of<br>
growth of bacteria in food by decreasing the reaction rate of biochemical reactions<br>
that enable bacteria to reproduce.<br>
<b>The collision model qualitatively explains the observation that an increase in</b><br>
temperature increases the rate of chemical reaction. However, it is found that the<br>
rate of a reaction is much smaller than the calculated collision frequency in a given<br>
reaction vessel. This implies that only a small fraction of the collision produces a<br>
reaction. Svante Arrhenius in 1880’s proposed the existence of a threshold energy<br>
which the reacting particles must acquire for a chemical reaction to take place.<br>
This energy barrier is called the activation energy (Ea) and it is defined as the<br>
minimum amount of energy required for reactants to start forming products. As<br>
stated previously, chemical reactions only take place when this energy barrier is<br>
attained because the energy is needed to first break the bonds for the product(s) to<br>
be formed. Within a molecule, energy is stored as potential energy and is released<br>
when molecules (reacting particles) rearrange into products. Particles with small<br>
kinetic energy (activation energy) will not have enough energy to break the<br>
bonds and therefore, no reaction will occur. This idea can easily be understood<br>
by considering the decomposition of NOBr molecules in the gas phase.<br>
For this reaction to occur, NOBr must acquire energy enough to form an activated<br>
complex at the transition state. At this point, the activated molecules possess high<br>
energy making them less stable and easily convertible into products. The NO–Br<br>
bonds break down followed by the formation of one Br–Br bond. Breaking N–Br<br>
bond in O=N–Br requires considerable amount of energy (243 kJ mol-1) which<br>
must be supplied to the system. The progress of the above reaction is summarised<br>
in Figure 6.5.<br>
<b>Chemical kinetics</b><br>

observation implies that, some collisions even though they have the required<br>
energy, still do not produce a reaction. To account for this observation, another<br>
factor known as proper orientation of reacting particles during collisions was<br>
proposed. Some collision orientations can lead to reactions while others cannot.<br>
Therefore, a factor to allow for collisions with proper molecular orientations was<br>
agreed to be included. Taking these two factors into account, Arrhenius obtained<br>
an expression shown in Equation 6.19.<br>

6.5.3 	 Surface area<br>
<b>In heterogeneous reactions, the rate of the chemical reaction depends on the</b><br>
surface area of solid reactants. The greater the surface area, the higher the rate of<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   216</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:10<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
217<br>
a chemical reaction. For instance, finely divided calcium carbonate reacts more<br>
quickly with hydrochloric acid than calcium carbonate chips. This is due to the<br>
fact that powdered calcium carbonate offers larger surface area to the reacting<br>
acid. In other words, by increasing the surface area of the reactant, the rate of<br>
reaction increases due to the increased contact surface area between individual<br>
particles and also due to the fact that surface molecules react more quickly.<br>
6.5.4 	 Pressure<br>
When pressure is applied to reactants in solid or liquid state no significant change<br>
in the rate of the reaction is observed. This is because solid and liquid reactants<br>
are incompressible. On the other hand, a change in pressure shows a significant<br>
change in the rate of reaction in which reactants are in gaseous form. This<br>
observation can be explained by using the collision model. When the pressure<br>
is increased, the volume of gaseous reaction mixture decreases resulting into<br>
increasing chances of the gases molecules to collide. Figure 6.6 shows the effect<br>
of increasing pressure from P1 to P2.<br>
<b>Figure 6.6 Effect of changing pressure on the volume of the gas mixture</b><br>
6.5.5 	 Catalysts<br>
Some reactions usually take a long time to go to completion and therefore,<br>
they are economically not viable in the industrial production of substances.<br>
<b>Such reactions may require monitoring of their rates by controlling either the</b><br>
concentration of the reactants or the temperature to which a chemical reaction<br>
is subjected. Dealing with concentration and temperature in controlling the rates<br>
of chemical reactions is not without limitations. These include the imprecise<br>
concentration of reactants or an introduction of unwanted reactions that may<br>
emanate from the temperature. In order to avoid these effects catalysts are found<br>
to be the best choice for controlling the rate of chemical reactions. A catalyst is<br>
defined as a chemical substance that alters the rate of a chemical reaction but<br>
remains chemically unchanged at the end of the reaction.<br>
<b>Chemical kinetics</b><br>
<b>P1</b><br>
<b>V1</b><br>
<b>V1 > V2</b><br>
<b>P1 < P2</b><br>
<b>P2</b><br>
<b>V2</b><br>

Examples of reactions in which catalysts have a role include the decomposition of<br>
H2O2 in the presence of KI or the decomposition of solid KClO3 in the presence of<br>
solid manganese(IV) oxide. In the first case the H2O2 decomposes at a relatively<br>
low rate in the absence of I- ions. The addition of KI to the H2O2 solution at pH<br>
7 increases the rate of the decomposition reaction to produce water and oxygen.<br>
2<br>
At the end of the reaction, the added KI remains qualitatively unchanged. Similarly,<br>
the rate of thermal decomposition of KClO3 is increased when manganese(IV)<br>
oxide powder is added.<br>
Like in the first case, at the end of the chemical reaction the quality of MnO2 also<br>
remains unchanged.<br>

towards the formation of products through an elementary reaction with the<br>
lowest activation energy. Figure 6.7 shows a reaction profile for a catalysed and<br>
uncatalysed pathways of the same reaction.<br>
