<b>Types of catalysis</b><br>
Catalysis is classified based on the phase of a catalyst in respect to the phase(s)<br>
of the reactants. In this criterion, catalysis can either be homogeneous or<br>
heterogeneous. In homogeneous catalysis, a reactant and a catalyst are of the<br>
same phase. That is to say if the reactant is in gaseous phase, the catalyst must<br>
also be in gaseous phase for a catalytic process to be classified as homogeneous.<br>
<b>An example of homogeneous catalysis is given by the reaction between ethyl</b><br>
acetate and water to form acetic acid and ethanol in the presence of hydrochloric<br>
acid as a catalyst. In the presence of HCl solution, the rate is faster than in its<br>
absence.<br>
<b>Unlike homogeneous catalysis, heterogeneous catalysis involves reactants</b><br>
and the catalyst in different phases. That is to say when a reactant is in the<br>
liquid phase, a catalyst can be in the solid phase or vice versa. An example of<br>
heterogeneous catalysis is in the Haber process for the production of ammonia.<br>
<b>The production of ammonia from hydrogen and nitrogen gases can be speeded</b><br>
up by decreasing the temperature. However, the reaction becomes too slow that<br>
makes it economically unfeasible. Therefore, the reaction is catalysed by a solid<br>
phase iron with aluminium oxide.<br>
<b>Enzyme catalysis</b><br>
<b>Chemical processes which occur in living systems are catalysed by biological</b><br>
catalysts which are called enzymes. Almost every biologically important reaction<br>
is assisted by a specific enzyme. As human body is designed to operate at almost<br>
constant temperature of 37 °C, many biochemical reactions would have been<br>
too slow at this temperature without these enzymes. An example of enzyme<br>
catalysis is the reaction between carbon dioxide and water catalysed by carbonic<br>
anhydrase.<br>
<b>Chemical kinetics</b><br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   219</b><br>

<b>This reaction is important as it removes the CO2 formed in the cell during</b><br>
metabolism.<br>
6.5.6 	 Light<br>
<b>Not all chemical reactions are affected by light except a few that usually form</b><br>
free radicals. Photo-chemical reactions are influenced by light. Photosynthesis<br>
process saves as an example of those reactions. Light activates some of the<br>
reactant molecules producing free radicals. Often, when a molecule absorbs a<br>
photon, the resulting excited state is much more chemically reactive than the<br>
molecule in its ground state. When photons of light strike the reactant molecules,<br>
it provides the necessary activation energy and enables the reaction to proceed at<br>
a faster rate.<br>

6.6<br>
<b>Reaction mechanisms</b><br>
Reaction equations are symbolic presentation of chemical reactions which specify<br>
only the origin and destination of the reaction process. They do not actually<br>
tell much about how the reaction takes place. Most chemical reactions occur<br>
by a series of steps known as elementary steps. Therefore, reaction equations<br>
represent the sum of several elementary steps. A series of steps through which a<br>
certain chemical reaction passes to form products is called reaction mechanism.<br>
<b>Consider the reaction between NO and Br2 to form NOBr. A proposed mechanism</b><br>
for this reaction is as follows:<br>
<b>Steps 1 and 2 are elementary steps. The net chemical equation which represents</b><br>
the overall reaction is given by the sum of the elementary steps.<br>
<b>The overall reaction is:</b><br>
<b>Chemical kinetics</b><br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   221</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:11<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five and Six</b><br>
<b>Physical Chemistry</b><br>
for Advanced Level Secondary Schools<br>
222<br>
<b>The sum of the elementary steps must give the accepted overall balanced</b><br>
equation for the reaction. Species such as N2O2 in the above example are called<br>
intermediates because they appear in the mechanism of the reaction but not in<br>
the overall balanced chemical equation. Intermediates are formed in an early<br>
elementary step and consumed in a later elementary step. They are neither<br>
reactants nor products but species which are formed and consumed in the course<br>
of the reaction to form products. By looking at the preceding example of the<br>
reaction mechanism, it can be deduced that in each of the two elementary steps,<br>
two molecules are involved in the reaction. That is to say in step 1, two NO<br>
molecules and in step 2, N2O2 and Br2 are involved. An elementary step in which<br>
two molecules are involved is referred to as a bimolecular reaction.<br>
<b>Elementary reactions in which one or three molecules (species) are involved are</b><br>
called unimoleculars and termoleculars, respectively. The number of molecules<br>
reacting in an elementary step is called molecularity of a reaction. When the rate<br>
laws for a unimolecular, bimolecular and termolecular reactions are observed,<br>
they correspond to their orders of reaction, respectively. For example, for a<br>
bimolecular reaction, the rate law is always second-order. This is either of the<br>
form<br>
2]<br>
<b>A</b><br>
[<br>
k<br>
for a step with a single reactant or k[A][B]  for a step involving two<br>
reactants. Generally, it can be concluded that a unimolecular elementary step is<br>
always first-order, a bimolecular elementary step is always second-order and a<br>
termolecular elementary step is always third-order.<br>
If chemical reactions occur by a series of steps and if each step has its own rate,<br>
then it can be concluded that the step which proceeds at the slowest rate is the<br>
one which controls the overall reaction rate. An example for this situation is the<br>
rapid pouring of water through a funnel into a container. The water collects in<br>
the container at a rate that is essentially determined by the size of the funnel<br>
opening and not by the rate of pouring water into the funnel. The slowest step in<br>
the sequence of steps leading to product formation is called the rate determining<br>
step. Thus, the rate law for the overall process is given by the rate determining<br>
step. The rate law expression that is obtained from the rate determining step must<br>
agree with the experimentally determined rate law.<br>
<b>NOTE: Overall order of reaction should not be confused with molecularity. A</b><br>
reaction which is first-order overall may as well involve a number of bimolecular<br>
and unimolecular steps.<br>

<b>Electrochem</b><br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
Electrochemistry offers a promising field of chemistry with a range of applications<br>
in various areas such as the manufacture of storage batteries, extraction and<br>
purification of metals in mining industry, and coating of metals for durability<br>
and appearance. In this chapter, you will learn about oxidation and reduction<br>
reactions, balancing of redox reactions, redox titrations, and electrolysis of<br>
electrolytes. In addition, you will learn about Faraday’s Laws of electrolysis,<br>
electrode potential, dependence of cell potential on concentration, and ionisation<br>
of electrolytes in solutions.<br>
7.1<br>
<b>Reduction and oxidation reactions</b><br>
<b>Electrochemical processes take place when there is a transfer of electrons from</b><br>
electron donors to electron recipients in a chemical reaction. In this context,<br>
electrochemical processes involve oxidation and reduction reactions which occur<br>
simultaneously. This means that for one chemical reaction to generate electric<br>
current, there should be another chemical reaction occurring to consume the<br>
electric current, and thus, producing a chemical change. In other words, oxidation<br>
of one reactant can not occur without reduction of the other reactant. Therefore,<br>
each of the two reactions is a half-reaction.<br>
7.1.1 	 Oxidation reactions<br>
<b>Any chemical reaction in which oxygen or any other electronegative radical is</b><br>
added to a substance, or hydrogen or any other electropositive radical is removed<br>
from the substance, or increase in oxidation state of a substance is called oxidation.<br>
<b>The concept can best be understood by considering the following examples:</b><br>
(a)<br>
<b>Addition of oxygen</b><br>
2Ca  +  O2<br>
2CaO<br>
<b>2H2</b><br>
<b>+  O2</b><br>
<b>2H2O</b><br>
<b>In the above examples, calcium and hydrogen are oxidised to their respective</b><br>
oxides.<br>

(b)<br>

<b>In terms of electron transfer, oxidation can be defined as a process of</b><br>

reducing agent loses electron(s) to an oxidising agent. For example, in the<br>

increase in oxidation state of a substance.<br>
(c)<br>

2HBr  +  S<br>
4HCl  +  MnO2 heat  MnCl2  +  Cl2  +  2H2O<br>
hydrogen sulphide has been oxidised by bromine to sulphur through loss<br>
of hydrogen; similary, hydrochloric acid has been oxidised to chlorine by<br>
manganese(IV) oxide on heating by loss of hydrogen.<br>
(d)<br>
<b>Removal of an electropositive radical</b><br>
<b>When potassium iodide is mixed with hydrogen peroxide, it loses potassium</b><br>
ion (an electropositive radical) to form iodine.<br>
<b>2KI  +  H2O2</b><br>
<b>2KOH  +  I2</b><br>
<b>Similarly, when potassium ferrocyanide is treated with chlorine, it is</b><br>
oxidised to potassium ferricyanide<br>
7.1.2 	 Reduction reactions<br>
The reaction between sodium and chlorine involves a simultaneous loss of electron<br>
from sodium and gain of electron by chlorine. The process in which electrons are<br>
gained by a substance is known as reduction and the substance gaining electron is<br>
reduced. Reduction can be defined in terms of electron gain, decrease in oxidation<br>

In this case, chlorine gains one electron from sodium to form sodium chloride.<br>
The oxidation state of chlorine changes from 0 to –1. Reduction can therefore be<br>
defined as the decrease in oxidation number of the substance.<br>
An oxidising agent or an oxidant causes oxidation of another substance and itself<br>
is reduced, whereas a reducing agent or a reductant causes reduction of another<br>
substance and itself is oxidised. The two reactions depend on each other, that<br>
oxidation of one species cannot occur without reduction of the counterpart and<br>
vice versa.<br>
<b>Reactions which involve simultaneous oxidation and reduction of chemical</b><br>
species are called redox reactions. The key proccess in redox reactions is the<br>
transfer of electrons from one substance to another as exemplified in the reaction<br>
below.<br>
1<br>
<b>In the above example, chloride in HCl has lost one electron to become oxidised</b><br>
to chlorine atom and manganese(IV) ion in MnO2 has gained two electrons to<br>
become manganese(II) ion in MnCl2.<br>
<b>Redox reactions can be classified as direct or indirect depending on whether</b><br>
the reducing and oxidising agents are present in the same solution. When the<br>
oxidising and reducing agents are present in the same solution, then the reaction<br>
is called direct redox reaction. Unlike the direct redox reactions, indirect redox<br>
reactions are kept separately and the transfer of electrons from one species to<br>
another does not take place directly but through an external conducting media<br>
such as a wire. Both types of redox reactions involve transfer of electrons which<br>
goes in hand with a change in the charge of atoms involved in the reaction. In<br>
order to identify the oxidising and reducing agent of a redox reaction, the change<br>
on the number of charge between reactants and products must be checked. To do<br>
that, chemists have developed a system of assigning numbers (called oxidation<br>
numbers or oxidation states) to the reacting species. The concept of oxidation<br>
states can be understood by considering the following examples:<br>
In the first reaction, the charge of Cu2+ ion has changed from +2 to 0 in Cu and<br>
that of hydrogen from 0 in H2 to +1 in H+. Similarly, the charge of sulphur in H2S<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   232</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:12<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
233<br>
has changed from –2 to 0 in S and that of bromine from 0 in Br2 to –1 in HBr.<br>
The apparent charge on an atom in a compound or ion is called oxidation number.<br>
<b>Oxidation number represents the number of electrons gained or lost by an atom</b><br>
during combination with other atoms, assuming that compounds consist of only<br>
ions.<br>
<b>Rules for assigning oxidation numbers</b><br>
<b>The assignment of oxidation numbers to atoms is governed by a set of the</b><br>
following rules:<br>
1.<br>
The oxidation number of an atom or molecule in its free state is zero. Thus,<br>
the atoms in Al, Na, K, Fe or O2, O3, P4 and S8 have zero oxidation number.<br>
2.<br>
The oxidation number of monoatomic ions is equal to the charge on the ion.<br>
<b>For example, the oxidation number of sodium in the Na+ ion is +1 and the</b><br>
oxidation number of chlorine in the Cl– ion is –1.<br>
3.<br>
<b>The oxidation number of hydrogen is +1, when it is combined with a non-</b><br>
metal. Hydrogen is therefore in the +1 oxidation state in CH4, NH3, H2O<br>
and HCl. The exceptions are in the metal hydrides such as NaH, LiH, CaH2<br>
and LiAlH4 where the oxidation number of hydrogen is –1.<br>
4.<br>
<b>Oxygen has usually an oxidation number of –2. Exceptions include</b><br>
molecules and poly–atomic ions (peroxides and superoxides) that contain<br>
<b>O–O bonds such as O2, O3, H2O2 and O2</b><br>
2− ion.<br>
5.<br>
<b>The sum of oxidation numbers of all atoms in a molecule or ion is equal</b><br>
to the charge on the molecule or zero if the molecule is uncharged. For<br>
example, the sum of oxidation numbers of the atoms in H3PO4 and HCO3<br>
–<br>
are 0 and –1, respectively.<br>
6.<br>
<b>The most electronegative element in a compound has a negative oxidation</b><br>
number equal to its charge in its ionic compound.<br>
<b>Example 7.1</b><br>
Calculate the oxidation number of sulphur in K2SO4.<br>
<b>Solution</b><br>
<b>K2SO4 contains elements of K, S and O. From the rules, the oxidation number of</b><br>
K is +1, O is –2 and that of K2SO4 is 0.<br>

<b>Importance of assigning oxidation numbers</b><br>
<b>The system of assigning oxidation numbers to atoms is important in some areas</b><br>
of chemistry such as:<br>
(a)<br>
<b>Naming of inorganic compounds containing elements with variable</b><br>
oxidation states. For example; K2Cr2O7 is named as potassium dichromate<br>
(VI) and CoCl2 is named as cobalt(II) chloride. The Roman numbers (VI)<br>
and (II) in the names of the compounds stand for oxidation number of Cr<br>
and Co, respectively.<br>
(b)<br>
<b>Enabling identification of elements that are oxidised and reduced in a redox</b><br>
reaction at a glance.<br>
(c)<br>
In balancing redox reactions.<br>

<b>Identifying redox reactions</b><br>
<b>Redox reactions are accompanied by interchange of electrons between the</b><br>
oxidising and the reducing agents which leads to changes in their oxidation<br>
states. The change in oxidation state can be used to identify whether a species<br>
has undergone oxidation or reduction. The oxidation state of an element tells<br>
which species is undergoing reduction or oxidation in the course of a chemical<br>
reaction. The reaction between hydrogen and sulphur is an example which shows<br>
how oxidation numbers can be used to identify redox reactions.<br>
In this reaction, the oxidation state of hydrogen increases from 0 to +1 while that<br>
of sulphur decreases from 0 to –2. Therefore, hydrogen is the reducing agent<br>
while sulphur is the oxidising agent. In summary, in redox reactions the oxidation<br>
state of a reducing agent increases while that of an oxidising agent decreases.<br>

7.2<br>
<b>Balancing of redox reactions</b><br>
<b>Non-redox chemical reactions are balanced by simple inspections of the</b><br>
stoichiometry of the reactants and products. Unlike non-redox reactions,<br>
balancing of redox reactions cannot be done by simple inspections. The process<br>
involves balancing the atoms as well as the charges on each participating atom.<br>
<b>Simultaneous balancing of both the atoms and charges on the same reaction</b><br>
complicates the exercise. Therefore, methods applicable in balancing redox<br>
reactions are discussed in subsequent sections.<br>
7.2.1 	 The half reaction method for balancing redox reactions<br>
<b>Redox reactions involve oxidation and reduction half-reactions which take place</b><br>
simultaneously.<br>
<b>An example, of the redox reaction between tin(II) ion and cerium(IV) ion is:</b><br>
<b>As shown by arrows indicating the changes in oxidation states, the reaction can</b><br>
be split into two half-reactions, the oxidation half equation where tin(II) loses<br>
two electrons and becomes oxidised to tin(IV) and the reduction half equation<br>
in which cerium(IV) gains one electron and becomes reduced to cerium(III).<br>
Splitting a redox reaction into its half-reactions is a special technique used to<br>
balance redox reactions. It provides a simple route for balancing both atoms and<br>
charges. In balancing redox reactions by half-reaction method, the following<br>
steps are followed:<br>
(a)<br>
Identify the reducing and oxidising agents in the unbalanced redox reaction.<br>
(b)<br>
Split the whole equation into oxidation and reduction half-reactions.<br>
2Ce4+ (aq)       +      Sn2+ (aq)<br>
2Ce3+ (aq)<br>
+      Sn4+ (aq)<br>
(+4)<br>
(+2)<br>
(+3)<br>
(+4)<br>

<b>Oxidation</b><br>

<b>Balance both half-reactions separately as follows:</b><br>
(i)<br>
Balance all atoms other than H and O.<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>Balance oxygen by adding H2O to the side without or with fewer O</b><br>
atoms.<br>
(iii)	 Balance hydrogen by adding H+ to the side without or with few H<br>
atoms.<br>
(iv)	 Balance the charge by using electrons.<br>
(v)<br>
<b>Multiply one or both balanced half-equations with a suitable integer</b><br>
to balance electrons.<br>
(d)<br>
Combine the two balanced half-equations to get the overall reaction.<br>
Remember to cancel equal terms that appear on both sides of the equation.<br>
(e)<br>
<b>Make sure that the equation is balanced with respect to both materials and</b><br>
charges.<br>
<b>Depending on the media in which the reaction is occurring, the half-reaction</b><br>
method can be used to balance redox reactions in acidic, basic and neutral media.<br>

<b>Balancing redox reactions in neutral medium</b><br>
<b>This happens in a situation where the reaction is done neither in acidic nor in</b><br>
basic media. For example, when a piece of zinc metal is placed in CuSO4 solution<br>
<b>Zn is oxidised to Zn2+ ions and Cu2+ ions are reduced to metallic copper:</b><br>
In this reaction, electrons are transferred directly from the reducing agent, Zn to<br>
the oxidising agent, Cu2+ ion in solution. Since there is no added acid or base in<br>
this reaction, it means the reaction occurs in neutral solution.<br>
When balancing redox reactions in neutral solutions, the same steps as those used<br>
in acidic and basic media are followed except that some steps are skipped. For<br>
example, balance the following reaction in neutral medium.<br>

7.2.2 	Balancing redox reactions by oxidation number method<br>
<b>The oxidation number method is based on the conservation of the electrical</b><br>
charges in the course of the reaction, without violating the law of conservation<br>
of mass. Thus, at the end of the reaction, a total gain in the oxidation numbers<br>
must be equal to the total loss in the oxidation numbers. In order to balance redox<br>
reactions by this method, the following steps must be followed:<br>
(a)<br>
Write skeletal equation for the redox reaction.<br>
(b)<br>
Assign oxidation numbers of all the atoms in the skeletal equation.<br>
(c)<br>
<b>Identify the elements which have undergone changes in the oxidation</b><br>
numbers. (Usually only two elements undergo changes in their oxidation<br>
numbers).<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   242</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:14<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
243<br>
(d)<br>
<b>Calculate the increase or decrease in the oxidation number per atom of</b><br>
the elements involved in the reaction. Multiply the obtained change in the<br>
oxidation number by the number of atom of the elements involved in the<br>
reaction.<br>
(e)<br>
<b>Balance the gain and loss in the oxidation numbers. This is done by placing</b><br>
the number representing the change in the oxidation number of the oxidising<br>
agent infront of the formula of the reducing agent and vice versa.<br>
(f)<br>
<b>Complete the balancing of the two elements (which have changed oxidation</b><br>
numbers) on the right-side of the chemical equation, keeping in mind that<br>
the ratio of the oxidising agent to the reducing agent, as determined from<br>
the change in their oxidation numbers must be maintained.<br>
(g)<br>
<b>Balance hydrogen and oxygen after all the other elements have been</b><br>
balanced.<br>
These steps are described in Example 7.4.<br>

7.3<br>
<b>Redox titrations</b><br>
The quantitative analysis of substances can be done by using a titration technique.<br>
This involves the use of two solutions, one with known concentration and another<br>
with unknown concentration such as acid-base or redox titration. In an acid-base<br>
titration, a known concentration of base is used to find an unknown concentration<br>
of an acid or vice versa. Similarly, in a redox titration, a known concentration of<br>
an oxidising agent is used to find an unknown concentration of a reducing agent<br>
or vice versa.<br>
Redox reactions are used to determine the amount of an analyte in the sample only<br>
if the analyte can react with the titrant. The determination of the concentration of<br>
analyte from the sample through redox reaction by measuring volumes of analyte<br>
and titrant is called redox titration. In this type of titration, either the reducing or<br>
oxidising agent is titrated against the other reagent, until the end point is attained.<br>
<b>At the end point which is marked either by colour change or voltage change</b><br>
in a potentiometer, the reducing agent is completely oxidised by the oxidising<br>
agent. When the end point is reached, the titration volume is recorded and used to<br>
calculate the unknown concentration by relating it to the stoichiometric quantities.<br>
In this section, redox titrations whose end points are marked by indicator colour<br>
change are considered. There are three types of indicators namely, self-indicators,<br>
specific indicators and true redox indicators.<br>
<b>Electrochemistry</b><br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   245</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:15<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>

<b>Physical Chemistry</b><br>
for Advanced Level Secondary Schools<br>
246<br>
7.3.1 	 Self-indicators<br>
In self-indicators, either oxidising agent or reducing agent acts as an indicator.<br>
<b>For example, the reduced and oxidised form of MnO4</b><br>
– and Cr2O7<br>
2– have different<br>
colours. A solution of MnO4<br>
– is intensely purple in acidic medium while that<br>
of Cr2O7<br>
2– is orange. However, when MnO4<br>
− undergoes reduction to Mn2+, the<br>
solution is nearly colourless. Similarly, when Cr2O7<br>
2− undergoes reduction to Cr3+<br>
in acidic medium, the solution is light green. When using KMnO4 as a titrant, the<br>
analyte’s solution remains colourless until equivalence point. Likewise, when<br>
using K2Cr2O7 as titrant, the analyte’s solution remains light green until when an<br>
equivalent point is obtained. The first drop of excess MnO4<br>
– or Cr2O7<br>
2– produces<br>
a permanent tinge of purple or orange, respectively, signalling the end point.<br>
<b>Hence, when KMnO4</b><br>
or K2Cr2O7 is used as one of the titrants, it acts as a self-<br>
indicator and thus, eliminates the need for other indicators.<br>
7.3.2  Specific indicators<br>
<b>Specific indicators are substances which form coloured compounds with specific</b><br>
oxidised or reduced form of titrant or analyte. Starch for example, forms a dark<br>
blue complex with I3<br>
–<br>
ion. This distinct colour can be used to signal the presence<br>
of excess I3<br>
– ion as titrant (a change in colour from colourless to dark blue) or<br>
completion of reaction consuming I3<br>
– as analyte (change in colour from dark<br>
blue to colourless). Another example of a specific indicator is thiocyanate (SCN–)<br>
which forms a soluble red-coloured complex, [Fe(SCN)]2+ with Fe3+<br>
ion. The<br>
specific indicators are used in specified reactions that show positive responses<br>
with the indicators.<br>
7.3.3 	 True redox indicators<br>
True redox indicators are substances that do not participate in the redox titration,<br>
but whose oxidised and reduced forms differ in colours. When these indicators<br>
are added to the analyte, the indicator imparts a colour that depends on the<br>
solution’s potential. As the solution potential changes with the addition of titrant,<br>
the indicator changes its oxidation state and colour, signalling the end point. An<br>
example of a true redox indicator is methylene blue solution. The solution of this<br>
substance is blue when it is in oxidising environment. On addition of a reducing<br>
agent, the solution potential changes. The change in the solution potential at the<br>
end point is equivalent to the potential difference between the two solutions and<br>
the blue colour of the solution changes to colourless.<br>
<b>The commonly used chemical substances in redox titrations exhibit only one</b><br>
property either oxidising or reducing property. However, some chemical<br>
substances are known which exhibit both oxidising and reducing properties<br>

7.4<br>
<b>Electrolysis of electrolytes</b><br>
<b>All solutes that dissolve in water fit into one of the two categories; either</b><br>
electrolytes or non-electrolytes. An electrolyte is a substance which in molten<br>
state or when dissolved in water conducts electricity. A non-electrolyte does not<br>
conduct electricity when in molten state or dissolved in water. Sodium chloride,<br>
copper(II) sulphate, potassium nitrate,  CH3COOH, NH3, and HNO2 are examples<br>
of electrolytes. On the other hand, sugar, alcohols and glycerol are typical non-<br>
electrolytes because they provide neutral molecules in solution.<br>
<b>An electrolytic solution contains positive and negative ions which can gain and</b><br>
lose electrons, respectively. This transfer of electrons between ions has the same<br>
effect as the flow of electrons in a conductor.<br>
The electrolytic process is carried out in an electrochemical cell called electrolytic<br>
cell. A typical electrolytic cell has three essential features namely: electrolytic<br>
solution which contains free moving ions, two electrodes at which electrolysis<br>
occurs and an external source of electrons such as battery or power source. The<br>
electrode through which electrons from the external power source enter the<br>
<b>Electrochemistry</b><br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   249</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:16<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five and Six</b><br>
<b>Physical Chemistry</b><br>
for Advanced Level Secondary Schools<br>
250<br>
solution is the negative electrode called cathode, and reduction reaction occurs<br>
at this electrode. The electrode through which electrons leave the electrolytic<br>
solution is a positive electrode called anode, and oxidation occurs at this<br>
electrode. Cations (positively charged ions) are attracted to the cathode while<br>
anions (negatively charged ions) are attracted to the anode.<br>
An electrolyte like molten sodium chloride contains positive and negative ions.<br>
<b>The positive ions move to the electrode connected to a negative terminal of the</b><br>
battery where these positive ions take electrons from. They then become pure<br>
metal atoms and they are deposited on the electrode. Similarly, negative ions move<br>
to the electrode that is connected to the positive terminal of the battery where<br>
these ions give up their extra electrons and become atoms. The phenomenon of<br>
decomposition of an electrolyte by passing electric current through its solution<br>
is termed as electrolysis. An example of an electrolytic cell is shown in Figure<br>
7.1. When the electric current is passed through molten sodium chloride (Figure<br>
7.1), sodium ions are attracted to the cathode where they are reduced to molten<br>
sodium.<br>
<b>The produced sodium is</b><br>
observed as shiny beads at<br>
the cathode. Furthermore,<br>
chloride ions are attracted<br>
to the anode where they<br>
undergo<br>
oxidation<br>
to<br>
chlorine gas.<br>
<b>In this case, chlorine gas</b><br>
is liberated at the anode.<br>
<b>It is important to note</b><br>
that in redox reactions<br>
the number of electrons<br>
released at the anode is the<br>
same as those consumed<br>
at the cathode. Therefore,<br>
the overall reaction is<br>
<b>Figure 7.1 An electrolytic cell for electrolysis of NaCl</b><br>
<b>Molten NaCl</b><br>
<b>Battery</b><br>
<b>Anode</b><br>
e-<br>
e-<br>
<b>Cathode</b><br>
2Cl-(l)            Cl2(g) + 2e-<br>
2Na+(l) + 2e-            2Na(l)<br>
<b>Oxidation</b><br>

7.5<br>
<b>The Faraday’s Laws of electrolysis</b><br>
<b>In every electrochemical process, whether spontaneous or not, a certain amount</b><br>
of electric charge is transferred during the oxidation and reduction. The half-<br>
reactions for redox processes include the electrons which carry that charge. It<br>
is possible to measure the rate at which the charge is transferred with a device<br>
called an ammeter. The ammeter measures the current flowing through a circuit<br>
in amperes (amps), denoted as A. The amount of electric charge which passess<br>
through the circuit can be calculated by the relationship:<br>
<b>Charge, Q (coulombs) = Current (ampere) × Time (seconds)</b><br>
<b>This enables the connection of the knowledge on reaction stoichiometry to</b><br>
electrical measurements. The principles underlying these relationships were<br>
worked out by the English scientist, Michael Faraday and they are summarised in<br>
two statements which are called Faraday’s First and Second Laws of electrolysis.<br>
These laws give the relationship between the amount of material liberated at the<br>
electrode and the amount of electric energy passed through the electrolyte.<br>
7.5.1	 Faraday’s First Law of electrolysis<br>
<b>The law states that the mass of any substance liberated or deposited at the</b><br>
electrode during electrolysis is directly proportional to the quantity of electricity<br>
passed through the electrolyte. That is:<br>
m<br>

7.5.2 	 The Faraday’s Second Law of electrolysis<br>
The Faraday’s First Law of electrolysis explains the direct relationship between<br>
the mass of a substance deposited at the electrode and the quantity of electricity<br>
passed through the electrolyte. However, the mass of the substance that is<br>
deposited or liberated is not only proportional to the quantity of electricity<br>
passing through the electrolyte, but also depends on other factors. It is known that<br>
every element has its own atomic weight. Based on this fact, the same number<br>
of atoms of different substances will have different masses. Again, the number of<br>
atoms of an element deposited on the electrodes also depends on the number of<br>
valence of that element. If the same quantity of electricity pass through different<br>
electrolytes, the mass of each substance deposited is directly proportional to its<br>
atomic weight and inversely proportional to its valence. When the two factors are<br>
combined, the mathematical expression is:<br>
atomic weight<br>
valence<br>
m µ<br>
<b>The ratio of atomic weight to valence is known as equivalent weight, thus, mass</b><br>

z =<br>

7.6<br>
<b>Applications of electrolysis</b><br>
<b>Electrolysis finds its application in the following areas:</b><br>
(a)<br>
<b>Electrolytic extraction of metals</b><br>
<b>Metals like lithium, potassium, magnesium, calcium, and aluminium have high</b><br>
negative reduction potentials. In this case, there are no specific reducing agents<br>
that can reduce them since they are powerful reducing agents. Therefore, these<br>
metals are extracted through electrolysis.<br>
(b)<br>
<b>The refining of metals</b><br>
Metals like copper and silver can be obtained in high purity through electrolytic<br>
process. The impure metal is made an anode during electrolysis in which the pure<br>
metal will be deposited on the cathode. The electrolyte is the salt solution of the<br>
respective metal.<br>
(c)<br>
<b>Electroplating</b><br>
The electrical deposition of metal on another metal is called electroplating. The<br>
purpose of electroplating is to protect the metal from corrosion or rusting and<br>
improve its appearance. It is used to make ornaments such as rings, earrings,<br>
bangles and wrist watches to appear as if they were made of gold or silver. Steel<br>
parts of cars, bicycles and some tools appear shiny because they are electroplated<br>
with chromium. During the electroplating process, the anode is usually made of<br>
the metal to be used for plating while the cathode is the object to be electroplated.<br>
The electrolyte used should be a salt solution of the electroplating metal. In the<br>
course of electrolysis, the plating metal dissolves in the electrolyte and transferred<br>
to the cathode where it is  deposited.<br>

7.7<br>
<b>Corrosion as a redox reaction</b><br>
The term corrosion refers to the wearing of metals by an electrochemical process.<br>
<b>Rust on iron, tarnish on silver and the green patina formed on copper and brass</b><br>
are few examples of corrosion. Corrosion is an undesirable process because it<br>
causes enormous damage to such things as buildings, bridges, pipelines, ships,<br>
and cars. Corrosion is a redox reaction that takes place between a metal, water, and<br>
the gases in the air. Some metals are readily attacked by corrosion while others<br>
show a remarkable resistance to corrosion. Metals of group one such as sodium,<br>
potassium, rubidium, and caesium are very reactive. When they are exposed to<br>
air, they tarnish rapidly and others like rubidium and caesium catch fire. The<br>
oxide layers formed on their surfaces are weak and can be easily attacked by<br>
gases and moisture of the atmosphere to allow further reaction (corrosion).<br>
<b>Unlike group one elements, metals like aluminium, lead, and zinc tarnish at a</b><br>
relatively low rate to form a layer of oxide on their surface, which is hard, non-<br>
porous, impermeable and difficult to crack. The oxide of iron is not strong enough<br>
to prevent a metal from being attacked by corrosion. In the presence of air and<br>
moisture, iron serves as the anode and therefore undergoes oxidation to iron(II).<br>
<b>The electrons that are released move through the iron and reduce atmospheric</b><br>
oxygen at the cathode, which is another region of the same metal surface.<br>
<b>The overall process is represented by the overall redox reaction:</b><br>
s<br>
<b>The Fe2+ ions which are formed at the anode are further oxidised by oxygen as</b><br>
follows:<br>
•<br>
<b>The hydrated form of iron(III) oxide is what is known as rust. Analysis of the</b><br>
reaction equations representing corrosion of iron leads to the conclusion that<br>
corrosion is a redox reaction which takes place on the surface of the metal<br>
whereby one region of the same metal acts as the cathode and the other as the<br>
anode (Figure 7.3).<br>

<b>Figure 7.3 The process of rust formation</b><br>
<b>Prevention of corrosion</b><br>
Metals are used in all areas of human economic activities. If not controlled,<br>
deterioration of metals due to corrosion may bring a negative impact to the<br>
economic development of nations. Because of this, prevention of metal corrosion<br>
becomes inevitable. Various techniques and methods have been devised to<br>
protect metals from corrosion. These includes coating, passivation, alloying, and<br>
cathodic protection.<br>
(a)<br>
<b>Coating</b><br>
<b>The electrochemical nature of the rusting of iron explains the importance of</b><br>
moisture in the corrosion process. Moisture must be present to act an electrolyte<br>
between anodic and cathodic regions. When there is no moisture, the circuit is<br>
incomplete and therefore, no reaction occurs and hence corrosion is impaired.<br>
<b>Prevention of corrosion by coating is based on this principle where the metal</b><br>
surface is coated with a layer of paint or another metal. For example, iron is<br>
commonly coated with tin, zinc, paints like red-oxide, and organic polymers.<br>
<b>However, coating is not permanent, it can become weak or worn out after</b><br>
sometime and can fail to prevent the metal from being attacked by corrosion.<br>
(b)<br>
<b>Passivation</b><br>
<b>The surface of iron or any other metal can be made inactive by a process</b><br>
called passivation. When iron is treated with a strong oxidising agent such as<br>
concentrated HNO3, a thin layer of oxide is formed on the surface of the metal.<br>
The metal surface becomes passive. The metal is therefore, said to be passivated.<br>
However, the developed resistance against corrosion is temporary.<br>
(c)<br>
<b>Alloying</b><br>
<b>Mixing of one metal with one or more other metals in a specified proportion</b><br>
<b>Fe(s)           Fe2+(aq) + 2e-</b><br>
<b>O2(g) + 4H+(aq)  + 4e-                 2H2O(l)</b><br>

<b>Fe2O3.xH2O</b><br>
<b>Anodic site</b><br>

is called alloying. Brass, steel and stanless steel are examples of alloys. The<br>
tendency of metals to oxidise is greatly reduced when they are alloyed with<br>
certain other metals. For example, stainless steel is resistant to corrosion because<br>
nickel and chromium form oxide coating that changes steel’s reduction potential<br>
to one characteristic of the noble metals. However, alloying is expensive.<br>
(d)<br>
<b>Cathodic protection</b><br>
<b>Cathodic protection is a method most often used to protect steel in buried fuel</b><br>
tanks and pipelines. In this process the tanks or pipelines are connected to an<br>
active metal such as magnesium by a wire. If the pipeline or tank is made of<br>
iron, electrons will be furnished by magnesium because it is a better reducing<br>
agent than iron. This will keep iron away from being oxidised. As the oxidation<br>
goes on, magnesium anode wears and so must be replaced periodically. Similarly,<br>
iron coated with zinc is protected by coating but at the same time is protected<br>
by cathodic protection. From Table 7.1 of the standard electrode potentials it<br>
is seen that zinc is readily oxidised than iron. Therefore, iron coated with zinc<br>
(galvanised) acts as the cathode and zinc acts as the anode. In normal atmospheric<br>
exposure, zinc will lose electrons in preference to iron and therefore, iron will<br>
remain unattacked.<br>
7.8<br>
<b>Electrode potential</b><br>
When a metal is dipped into the solution containing its ions, a potential difference<br>
is developed between the surface of the metal and the solution. This potential is<br>
known as electrode potential. It is the measure of the tendency of the metal to<br>
provide electrons. Copper and zinc metal dipped in copper(II)) sulphate and zinc<br>
sulphate solutions, respectively, represent electrodes of the two metals each with<br>
its own electrode potential (Figure 7.4).<br>
The electrode immersed in a solution containing its ions as in Figure 7.4 constitutes<br>
a half-cell. Each half-cell has its own electrode potential or half-cell potential. To<br>
measure electrode potential or half-cell potential in each case, a second electrode<br>
is needed. A voltmeter has to be connected to the external circuit across the two<br>
electrodes. The reading on the voltmeter will be the sum of the electrode potentials<br>
of the two electrodes. Therefore, in order to obtain the electrode potential of a<br>
particular half-cell, a reference electrode with known potential is used.<br>

<b>The hydrogen electrode has been chosen as the standard electrode against which</b><br>
electrode potentials of other elements can be measured. An electrode potential<br>
for the hydrogen electrode is arbitrarily assigned a value of 0.00 V. Hence, when<br>
a standard hydrogen electrode is connected to copper electrode (Figure 7.5), the<br>
reading on the voltmeter is assumed to be the potential of the copper electrode.<br>
<b>The standard hydrogen electrode consists of platnum foil immersed in a 1.0 M</b><br>
solution of H+, a hydrogen gas at 1 atmosphere pressure bubled over Pt foil, and<br>
a temperature of the electrolyte of 25°C.<br>

<b>The electrode potential of an element measured against standard hydrogen</b><br>
electrode as a reference electrode and ions of the element equals to 1 mol dm−3<br>
at standard conditions is called standard electrode potential, E°. During this<br>
measurement the hydrogen electrode is the anode and the metal is the cathode.<br>
<b>The redox reactions occuring in the electrochemical cell during the measurement</b><br>
of electrode potential of copper (Figure 7.5) are:<br>
<b>At the anode: H2(g)</b><br>
2H+(aq) + 2e−<br>
(Oxidation)<br>
<b>E° = 0.00 V</b><br>
<b>At the cathode: Cu2+(aq) + 2e−</b><br>
<b>Cu(s)  (Reduction)</b><br>
<b>E° = 0.34 V</b><br>
<b>The overall redox reaction equation is:</b><br>
<b>H2(g) + Cu2+(aq)</b><br>
2H+(aq) + Cu(s)<br>
<b>E° = 0.34 V</b><br>
<b>The hydrogen gas is oxidised and the Cu2+ is reduced. Therefore, the potential</b><br>
difference which is measured by the voltmeter is the driving force for the<br>
reduction process of Cu2+. This is called a reduction potential of the element and<br>
if measured at the standard conditions, is called standard reduction potential.<br>
<b>Each element has its own value of standard reduction potential. When the values</b><br>
of the standard reduction potentials of elements are arranged from negative to<br>
positive values, the obtained series is called an electrochemical series. The most<br>
negative values are placed at the top of the electrochemical series and the most<br>
positive at the bottom (Table 7.1).<br>

7.8.1 	 Applications of electrochemical series<br>
The relative position of a metal in the electrochemical series is a useful technique<br>
in predicting its reducing or oxidising ability. Metals with positive electrode<br>
potentials have higher tendency to accept electrons and therefore, are better<br>
oxidising agents, M+ + e−→ M. However, metals with negative electrode potentials<br>
have greater tendency to lose electrons and therefore, are better reducing agents,<br>
<b>M→ M+ + e−.  This entails that the metals with higher electrode potential (more</b><br>
positive) will be reduced by the metal with lower electrode potential (less positive)<br>
in the electrochemical series. The following points are important regarding<br>
electrochemical series of elements:<br>
(a)<br>
<b>Predicting the oxidising or reducing ability of a metal</b><br>
<b>The electrode potentials arranged in order of increasing</b><br>
o<br>
electrode<br>
<b>E</b><br>
values, place<br>
the oxidising agent in increasing order of their ability to attract electrons. Thus:<br>
(i)<br>
<b>The more positive the value of</b><br>
,<br>
o<br>
electrode<br>
<b>E</b><br>
the better the oxidising ability of<br>
the ion, element or compound.<br>
(ii)<br>
<b>The more negative the value of</b><br>
,<br>
o<br>
electrode<br>
<b>E</b><br>
the better the reducing ability of<br>
the ion, element or compound.<br>
(iii)	 Any substance in the series will spontaneously reduce any other substance<br>
lower than it in the series.<br>
(b)<br>

(d)<br>
<b>Predicting the preferential displacement of metal from its salt solution</b><br>
<b>As stated previously, metals lower in the electrochemical series are stronger</b><br>
oxidising agents. Contrarily, metals at the top of the series are strong reducing<br>
agents and they are oxidised to their ions. For instance, zinc found higher in the<br>
series is oxidised to Zn2+ ions while copper which is located lower in the series is<br>
produced by reduction of Cu2+ ions to copper metal. In this process, the following<br>
half reactions occur:<br>
<b>Anode reaction: Zn(s)</b><br>
<b>Zn2+(aq) + 2e</b><br>
<b>Cathode reaction: Cu2+(aq) + 2e</b><br>
<b>Cu(s)</b><br>
Therefore, when zinc is placed in CuSO4 solution, copper metal is precipitated.<br>
<b>Generally, it can be said that a metal higher in the series can precipitate the</b><br>
metal lower in the series. So, silver metal cannot precipitate nickel from aqueous<br>
solution, since silver metal is lower in the series and is a strong oxidising agent.<br>
(e)<br>
Predicting the preferential displacement of hydrogen from a dilute acid<br>
solution<br>
<b>Metals found above hydrogen in the electrochemical series are stronger reducing</b><br>
agents than hydrogen itself and can reduce H+ to H2. However, metals found<br>
below hydrogen in the series are weaker reducing agents than hydrogen and<br>
therefore cannot convert H+ to H2. This justifies, for example,  why Zn reacts<br>
with dilute sulphuric acid to liberate hydrogen gas, while copper does not react<br>
with dilute sulphuric acid.<br>
7.8.2 	 The salt-bridge<br>
<b>Redox reactions involve the transfer of electrons from the reducing agent to an</b><br>
oxidising agent. Redox reactions can either take place in the same solution or<br>
between two solutions kept separately. The separated half-cells are connected<br>
with an external wire that allows the flow of electrons from the reducing agent to<br>
the oxidising agent. In this arrangement, conventionally the oxidation half-cell<br>
<b>Electrochemistry</b><br>

(anode) is placed to the left-hand side and the reduction half-cell (cathode) is<br>
placed to the right-hand side (Figure 7.6).<br>
<b>Figure 7.6 Separated oxidising and reducing agent of the two compartments of</b><br>
electrochemical cell<br>
<b>From the standard reduction potential values, zinc has a greater tendency of</b><br>
losing electrons than copper. Therefore, copper electrode will withdraw electrons<br>
from zinc electrode and the following half-reactions take place at the electrodes.<br>
<b>However, when the two half-cells are connected as in Figure 7.6, the current</b><br>
flows for a short time and then ceases. This is because the flow of electrons<br>
from zinc electrode to copper electrode results into the build-up of charge in<br>
the two compartments. That is to say there is accumulation of Zn2+ ion on the<br>
anode compartment. To sustain the flow of electrons under these conditions, it<br>
requires greater amount of energy which is not feasible. This problem can be<br>
solved by connecting the two solutions with a salt-bridge as shown in Figure<br>
7.7. The salt-bridge is a U-shaped tube filled with a concentrated solution of an<br>
inert electrolyte sealed by cotton plugs. The contents of the U-tube (normally<br>
solutions of KCl, KNO3 or NH4NO3) are called inert electrolytes because they do<br>
not undergo any chemical change with the solutions in the two half-cells. The salt<br>
bridge therefore, completes the circuit and allows ions to flow without extensive<br>
mixing of the solutions.<br>

<b>Figure 7.7 represents one of the two types of electrochemical cells known as</b><br>
<b>Galvanic or voltaic cell, and the redox reaction taking place in the cell is:</b><br>
Note: Figure 7.7 is a special type of galvanic cell called the Daniell cell named in<br>
favour of the British chemist, John Frederick Daniell (1770-1845).<br>
In the galvanic cell there is a constant flow of electrons and hence, generation of<br>
electricity. An electric current flows from the anode to the cathode because of the<br>
existing difference in electrode potentials between the electrodes which act as the<br>
driving force of the electric current. This difference in potential is measured by<br>
a voltmeter. The reading on the voltmeter indicates the cell voltage which is also<br>
known as cell electromotive force (emf) or cell potential. When a galvanic cell<br>
operates spontaneously, its cell potential is positive, Ecell &gt; 0.<br>
7.8.3	 Standard cell potential<br>
<b>The measured potential of a voltaic cell is affected by factors like changes in</b><br>
concentration, temperature, pressure, and energy loss. Therefore, in order to<br>
compare the output of different cells, a standard cell potential has to be considered.<br>
<b>The standard cell potential is the potential of the cell measured at standard</b><br>
conditions (temperature of 298 K, concentrations of the reactants and products<br>
are 1 mol dm–3 and 1 atm pressure for gases). Just as each part of a half-reaction<br>
contributes to the overall reaction, the potential of each half- cell also makes up<br>
a part of the overall standard cell potential.<br>

7.8.4	 Cell diagram or cell notation<br>
The conventional notation for representing galvanic cells is called cell diagram<br>
or cell notation.<br>
For the cell shown in Figure 7.7, if the concentrations of Zn2+ and Cu2+ ions are<br>
1 M, the cell diagram is:<br>
aq<br>
aq<br>
s<br>
s<br>
<b>In the cell diagram, the single vertical line represents phase boundary between</b><br>
the solution and the electrode and double vertical lines represent salt bridge.<br>
<b>Conventionally, cell diagrams are written by using the following rules:</b><br>
(a)<br>
<b>The anode is written on the left hand side of the salt bridge and the cathode</b><br>
on the right hand side.<br>
(b)<br>
<b>The anodic half-cell is represented by writing the metal first followed by</b><br>
single vertical line and then metal ions present,  for example, Zn(s)/Zn2+(aq)<br>
(c)<br>
<b>The magnitude of the concentration in mol dm−3 of the solution in which</b><br>
the electrode is dipped is written in brackets after the oxidation or reduction<br>
half reactions in a cell diagram, for example, Zn(s)/Zn2+aq (1 mol dm–3).<br>
(d)<br>
<b>The cathodic half-cell is represented by first writing the metal ion of the</b><br>
electrolyte in solution, and then the metal. Both of them are separated by a<br>
single vertical line, for example, Cu2+(aq)/ Cu(s).<br>
(e)<br>
<b>The molar concentration or activity of the solution is written in brackets</b><br>
after the formula of the ion just like in the anodic cell, Cu2+(aq)(1 M)/Cu.<br>
<b>Note that when the reactants or products of the same phase are more than</b><br>
one they are separated by semicolon, for example, the cathodic reaction of<br>
the following cell diagram:<br>
<b>Fe/Fe2+(1 M)//MnO4</b><br>
–(1 M); H (1 M); Mn2+(1 M)/Pt.<br>

7.9<br>
<b>The Dependence of cell potential on concentration</b><br>
For any galvanic cell, when temperature and pressure of gases are not in standard<br>
conditions, the emf of the galvanic cell differs from its standard emf value. It can<br>
be less or greater than its standard cell potential. To understand this phenomenon,<br>
consider the following reversible redox reaction:<br>
<b>Electrochemistry</b><br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   271</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:20<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five and Six</b><br>
<b>Physical Chemistry</b><br>
for Advanced Level Secondary Schools<br>
272<br>
Under standard conditions this cell has standard cell potential of 2.24 V. Applying<br>
the Le Cháteliers Principle, an increase in the concentration of Pb2+ ions will<br>
favour the forward reaction that results into an increase in the driving force of<br>
the electrons and a corresponding increase in cell potential. At the same time,<br>
an increase in the concentration of a product, Mg2+ will favour the backward<br>
reaction, thus, decreasing the cell potential. The maximum cell potential is<br>
obtained when there is no current flow. When the current flows the cell discharges<br>
and the concentrations change, as a result the Ecell also changes. The following<br>
equations are examples which distinguish the cell potential at standard and non-<br>
standard conditions:<br>
<b>Zn(s) + Cu2+(1.0 M) →Zn2+(1.0 M) + Cu(s)</b><br>
<b>E°cell = 1.103 V</b><br>
<b>Zn(s) + Cu2+(0.075 M) →Zn2+(1.5 M)  + Cu(s)</b><br>
<b>Ecell = 1.064 V</b><br>
<b>Any galvanic cell will spontaneously discharge until it reaches equilibrium, a</b><br>
point at which the reaction quotient becomes equal to the equilibrium constant.<br>
That is, Q = K and Ecell = 0. At this point, a cell or battery is said to be dead. A<br>
dead battery is the one in which its cell reaction has reached equilibrium. At this<br>
state, there is no more driving force to push electrons through the external wire.<br>
7.9.1	 Relationship between free energy and cell potential<br>
In galvanic cells, chemical energy is converted to electrical energy. The electrical<br>
energy that is generated as the result of the chemical reaction is equal to the product<br>
of the emf of the cell and the total charge that passes through the electrochemical<br>
cell. Thus,<br>
Electrical energy (J) = maximum cell potential (V) × total electrical charge (C)<br>
<b>However, the total charge is obtained by multiplying the number of moles, n, of</b><br>
electrons that pass through the circuit by the electrical charge contained in 1 mol<br>
of electrons, thus;<br>
<b>Total electrical charge = nF</b><br>
Substituting the value of total electrical charge into the electrical energy equation<br>
under standard states leads to:<br>
<b>Electrical energy</b><br>
cell<br>
<b>E</b><br>

= -<br>
´<br>
!<br>
The negative sign on the right-hand side of the equation indicates that the system<br>
does work on the surroundings. In accordance with Gibbs, the energy released<br>

<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>

between the final and initial stages of the reaction. The change in the Gibbs free<br>

7.9.2 	 Equilibrium constants for redox reactions in electrochemical<br>
cells<br>
<b>In the preceding sections, a discusion was made on the dependence of the cell</b><br>
potential on the electrode potentials, concentrations of ions and on the free energy<br>
of the solution. It was also mentioned that apart from these factors, the potential<br>
of the cell depends also on the equilibrium constant of the cell. When the cell has<br>
established equilibrium, the cell potential, ∆E = 0 and the the reaction quotient, Q<br>

<b>7.10 	 Ionisation of electrolytes in solutions</b><br>
When electrolytes are dissolved in water, they dissociate into their constituent<br>
ions. The ions released in the solution are responsible for the flow of charges as<br>
they carry them from one place to another. Different substances have different<br>
degree of dissociation in water, some electrolytes ionise completely while others<br>
ionise partially. Based on the degree of ionisation, electrolytes can be classified<br>
into strong and weak electrolytes. Strong electrolytes ionise completely when<br>
dissolved in water to form free ions. Examples of strong electrolytes are soluble<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   280</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:24<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
281<br>
salts like BaCl2, strong acids like HCl, HNO3, H2SO4 and strong bases like NaOH<br>
and KOH. On the other hand, weak electrolytes dissociate partially to produce<br>
relatively few ions when dissolved in water. An example of a weak electrolyte is<br>
<b>CH3COOH.</b><br>
<b>The extent to which an electrolyte has dissociated in aqueous solution is known</b><br>
as the degree of dissociation. Weak electrolytes form an interesting field of<br>
chemistry called the dissociation of weak electrolyes. It is of interest to know<br>
the percentage of ions of a weak electrolyte that has dissociated, since the free<br>
ions participate in chemical reactions. Generally, the degree of dissociation or<br>
ionisation is the fraction of the electrolyte which has dissociated into ions in<br>
aqueous solution. For example, if an electrolyte is 50% dissociated, its degree<br>
of dissociation is 0.5. The degree of dissociation is represented by a symbol α.<br>
<b>Dissociation of electrolyte results into an increase in the number of dissolved</b><br>
particles in the solution. The ratio of the number of particles produced as the<br>
result of dissociation to the number of undissociated particles gives what is<br>
known as the van’t Hoff’s Factor, i, which is denoted by:<br>
<b>Number of particles after dissociation</b><br>
i =<br>
<b>Number of particles before dissociation</b><br>
The number of particles after dissociation refers to the actual number of particles<br>
produced by dissociation/ionisation.<br>
<b>Actual number of particles produced by ionisation</b><br>
i =<br>
<b>Number of particles without ionisation</b><br>
Assuming that 1 mol of an electrolyte is capable of producing ν ions on complete<br>
ionisation and if its degree of ionisation is α, then the total number of particles in<br>
the solution is equal to the number of unionised particles (1-α) plus the number<br>
of ions produced by the process of ionisation (αν) i.e total number of particles<br>

7.10.1 	Conductivity of electrolyte in solutions<br>
The important characteristic of electrolytes is their ability to conduct electricity.<br>
They conduct electricity because they possess mobile charge carriers (ions). The<br>
property of electrolytic solution which indicates the extent to which it conducts<br>
electricity is known as conductance. Its numerical value is equal to the reciprocal<br>
of resistance, R, to the flow of electricity through the solution and it is denoted by<br>
the symbol, G. The mathematical expression of conductance is given by Equation<br>
7.20. The unit for conductance is siemens (symbol S), i.e., S = Ω−1.<br>

electrodes being 1 m apart and electrodes being of unit surface area.<br>

It follows from Equation 7.27 that, if the concentration of electrolytes decreases,<br>

Dilution Law which states that, at constant temperature, the degree of ionisation<br>
or dissociation is inversely proportional to the square root of the concentration<br>
and directly proportional to the square root of dilution. The Ostwald Dilution<br>
Law is only applicable on solutions of weak electrolytes.<br>

293<br>
<b>Acids, base</b><br>
<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Eight</b><br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
<b>Acids and bases are found in various things or items ranging from natural food</b><br>
substances to commercial products. For instance, acids are found in fruits such as<br>
oranges, lemons, pineapples, grapes, passion, and tomatoes. The commercially<br>
produced basic products include baking soda, washing soda, oven cleaners, and<br>
antacid tablets such as magnesium hydroxide. The salts include table salt, different<br>
types of fertilisers, and a wide range of medicine sources. In this chapter, you will<br>
learn about the concept of acids and bases, ionic equilibria of acids and bases,<br>
buffer solutions, and salt hydrolysis.<br>
8.1<br>
<b>Concept of acids and bases</b><br>
<b>Chemical substances were classified as acids and bases based on the properties</b><br>
they show when dissolved in aqueous solutions. However, as time passed<br>
scientists discovered that some other chemical substances which were initially<br>
excluded in the acids and bases family were found to show similar properties<br>
when are dissolved in water or other solvents. Therefore, the concept of acids<br>
and bases had been broadening from time to time in order to accommodate all<br>
substances which were found to show properties similar to those which were<br>
shown by the already classified acids and bases. Arrhenius, Brønsted-Lowry and<br>
<b>Lewis made a significant contribution to the today’s understanding of acids and</b><br>
bases.<br>
8.1.1 	 Arrhenius concept of acids and bases<br>
<b>In 1884, a Swedish chemist Svante August Arrhenius proposed two specific</b><br>
classifications of compounds. He termed them as acids and bases depending on<br>
the type of ions they produce when dissolved in aqueous solutions. According<br>
to Arrhenius, acids were defined as substances which produce H+ ions when<br>
dissolved in water. For example, HCl and HNO3 are Arrhenius acids because<br>
they give H+ ions as the only positive ions when dissolved in water.<br>
The H+ ions give acids a sour taste, they change colour of indicators such as blue<br>
litmus paper to red and cause corrosion of some metals.<br>
<b>Acids, Bases and Salts</b><br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   293</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:27<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five and Six</b><br>
<b>Physical Chemistry</b><br>
for Advanced Level Secondary Schools<br>
294<br>
Arrhenius defined bases as ionic compounds that ionise into cations and hydroxide<br>
ions, OH− when dissolved in water. Sodium hydroxide (NaOH) and potassium<br>
hydroxide (KOH) are examples of  Arrhenius bases because they ionise in water<br>
to release OH− ions as the only negatively charged species.<br>
The OH– ions are responsible for the bitter taste, slippery feeling and turning the<br>
colour of red litmus paper to blue or phenolphthalein to pink. From the Arrhenius<br>
concept, a combination of an acid and a base results into the formation of water<br>
molecule from the H+ ion of the acid and the OH− ion of the base. This leads to the<br>
concept of neutralisation which is the reaction of an acid and a base to form salt<br>
and water only. The Arrhenius Theory explains many properties and reactions of<br>
acids and bases. For example, the reaction between HCl and NaOH results into a<br>
neutral solution containing table salt (NaCl). However, the Arrhenius Theory has<br>
the following weaknesses:<br>
(a)<br>
<b>It is applicable only for acids and bases in aqueous medium implying that</b><br>
the concepts are not valid for acid-base behaviour in the absence of water.<br>
(b)<br>
<b>The concept defines acids and bases as substances that contain hydrogen</b><br>
ions (H+) and hydroxyl ions (OH–), respectively. However, many compounds<br>
act as acids or bases even if they do not contain H+ or OH– groups as part<br>
of the molecule.<br>
<b>In order to accommodate other acid-base behaviours which the Arrhenius theory</b><br>
failed to account for, another theory developed by Brønsted J. N and Lowry T. M<br>
was established.<br>
8.1.2 	 Brønsted-Lowry concept of acids and bases<br>
<b>In 1923, Brønsted and Lowry independently developed a theory to expand the</b><br>
definition of acids and bases. This theory was known as the Brønsted-Lowry<br>
<b>Proton Transfer Theory. They suggested that any hydrogen containing species</b><br>
(molecules, cations or anions) which is capable of donating one or more proton(s)<br>
to any other substance is called an acid. On the other hand any chemical species<br>
which is capable of accepting one or more proton(s) from an acid is called a base.<br>
<b>Therefore, according to Brønsted-Lowry Theory, an acid is a proton donor and a</b><br>
base is a proton-acceptor.  To accept a proton, a base needs to have a lone pair of<br>
electrons to bind the H+ ions.<br>
<b>Brønsted-Lowry definition of acid and base can be explained by the following</b><br>
example:<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   294</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:27<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
295<br>
<b>In aqueous solution, HNO3 donates a proton to H2O to form hydroxonium ion</b><br>
(H3O+) and nitrate ion (NO3<br>
–). Thus, H2O is a base because it accepts the donated<br>
proton. Similarly, in the next example ammonia (NH3) acts as a base by accepting<br>
a proton when it reacts with water.<br>
<b>From the two examples, it can be noted that water shows both behaviour of acids</b><br>
and bases by donating and accepting a proton depending on the strength of the<br>
second substance. The substances which show both acidic and basic properties<br>
are called amphoteric substances.<br>
<b>An important feature of Brønsted-Lowry concept is that the acids and bases are</b><br>
symbiotic. Every acid must form a base on donating its proton, and every base<br>
must form an acid on accepting a proton. The symbiotic nature exhibited by acids<br>
and bases can be exemplified by the reaction:<br>
<b>A base that is produced when an acid donates its proton is called the conjugate</b><br>
base of the acid. Similarly, the acid that is produced when a base accepts a proton<br>
is called the conjugate acid of the base. In the above example, F– is the conjugate<br>
base of the acid HF and H3O+ is the conjugate acid of the base H2O. Thus, a<br>
conjugate acid differs from its conjugate base by one proton. A pair of an acid<br>
and a base which differs from one another by a proton is said to be a conjugate<br>
acid-base pair as shown in Figure 8.1.<br>
<b>Figure 8.1 Reaction between HF and H2O to demonstrate acid-base conjugate pairs</b><br>

<b>Acid	          Base</b><br>
<b>Conjugate base   Conjugated acid</b><br>
<b>F  (aq)      +    H3O  (aq)</b><br>

<b>Another example of conjugate acid-base pairs is given by the reaction between</b><br>
ammonia and water:<br>
<b>Conjugate bases differ in strength depending on the strength of their conjugate</b><br>
acids.  A conjugate base of a strong acid is a weak base whereas a conjugate<br>
base of a weak acid is a strong base. The stronger the acid, the weaker will be its<br>
conjugate base and vice versa.<br>
<b>The Brønsted-Lowry concept proved to be better than the Arrhenius concept</b><br>
because it is applicable to aqueous and non-aqueous protic solvent equally<br>
well. However, it cannot account for the acidic character of the compounds not<br>
containing hydrogen such as oxides of non-metals like CO2, SO2, SO3 as well as<br>
AlCl3 and BF3, and the basic character of compounds such as CaO and Na2O.<br>
<b>Therefore, the need ascended to establish another theory to give explanation on</b><br>
areas where Brønsted-Lowry Theory failed. Lewis concept of acids and bases<br>
was then established.<br>
8.1.3 	 Lewis concept of acids and bases<br>
In 1938, Lewis G. N. proposed an alternative theory to describe acids and bases.<br>
<b>His theory gave a generalised explanation of acids and bases based on structure</b><br>
and bonding. The theory used electrons instead of proton transfer and specifically<br>
stated that an acid is a species that accepts an electron pair while a base is a<br>
species which donates an electron pair. This implies that bases are electron rich<br>
while acids are electron deficient. The following example can be used to explain<br>
Lewis acid and base reaction.<br>
In this case, the OH− ion with a pair of electrons is a Lewis base because it has<br>
donated its lone pair of electrons to H+ ion to form a dative covalent bond in H2O<br>
molecule. On the other hand, H+ ion is a Lewis acid because it has accepted a pair<br>
of electrons from the base into its vacant orbital.<br>

<b>Based on the Lewis concept of acids and bases, their identification can be done</b><br>
following their features.<br>
(a)<br>
<b>Lewis acids includes:</b><br>
(i)<br>
all molecules possessing an atom with incomplete octet of electrons<br>
such as BF3 and AlCl3 or doublet such as H+. For example, when<br>
<b>BF3 reacts with water, B accepts a lone pair of electrons from water</b><br>
molecule followed by release of HF. This reaction proceeds in the<br>
same way until all the three fluorine atoms of BF3 are replaced,<br>
forming B(OH)3.<br>
(ii)<br>
all cations such as Ag+, Cu2+, Fe2+ and Fe3+. For example, in the<br>
reaction between Ag+ and NH3, the Ag+ is accepting electron. It is<br>
therefore, a Lewis acid.<br>
Another example of Lewis acid-base reaction is given by the<br>
dissolution of CuSO4 crystals in water to form Cu2+ and SO4<br>
2− ions.<br>

light blue complex, [Cu(H2O)6]2+.<br>
In this case, Cu2+ ions and H2O molecules act as the Lewis acid and<br>
Lewis base, respectively.<br>
(ii)<br>
all molecules in which the central atom can acquire more than eight<br>
valence electrons such as SiCl4 and SiF4. For example, in the reaction<br>

<b>Limitations of Lewis concept</b><br>
(a)<br>
According to Lewis, acid-base reactions involve electron transfer.<br>
<b>Therefore, they are expected to proceed fast. However, many Lewis acid-</b><br>
base reactions are very slow.<br>
(b)<br>
<b>The Lewis acid-base concept failed to account for the relative strength of</b><br>
acids and bases because it is not based on ionisation.<br>

<b>Example 8.1</b><br>
<b>Explain how sulphur dioxide (SO2) behaves as a Lewis acid when it reacts with</b><br>
water.<br>
<b>Solution</b><br>
<b>Oxygen atom being more electronegative than sulphur, it withdraws electron</b><br>
density from the central S atom, and thus, makes it partially positive. The oxygen<br>
atom of water molecule donates a lone pair of electrons to the S atom, breaking<br>
one of the π–bonds and forming an S–O bond, and a proton is transferred to the<br>
oxygen atom. The process can be represented by the following equation:<br>

8.2<br>
<b>Ionic equilibria of acids and bases</b><br>
<b>In the process of ionisation in water, an acid or base is broken into ions. The</b><br>
strength of an acid is determined by the number of moles of H3O+ produced for<br>
<b>Acids, Bases and Salts</b><br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   299</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:28<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>

each mole of acid that is ionised. Likewise, the strength of a base is determined by<br>
the number of moles of OH− ions produced for each mole of a base that is ionised.<br>
Acids and bases can therefore, be categorised as strong or weak depending on the<br>
extent to which they are ionised.<br>
Strong acids and bases ionise completely when dissolved in water. For example,<br>
when HF, a strong acid ionises, H+ ion is transferred to water resulting into a<br>
solution that contains essentially H3O+ and F− ions only. The ionisation of HF in<br>
water is considered to be 100% complete. Similarly, KOH, a strong base when<br>
dissolved in water, it ionises to K+ and OH– ions resulting to a solution containing<br>
only K+ and OH–. It is also considered to be 100% ionised.<br>
Unlike strong acids and bases, weak acids and bases ionise partially to establish<br>
equilibrium where most of the original acid or base remain unionised. Table 8.1a<br>
presents a list of some strong and weak acids whereas Table 8.1b presents a list<br>
of some strong and weak bases.<br>
<b>Table 8.1a A List of some strong and weak acids</b><br>

leaving behind OH– ion. This process can be expressed by the following equation:<br>

8.2.3 	 Ionic product of water and pH<br>
<b>In the previous discussion, water was shown to exhibit amphoteric property when</b><br>
reacted with a base and acid. In pure water, there is a forward reaction between<br>
two water molecules that transfers H+ from one water molecule to another. One<br>
molecule acts as an acid by losing H+ (proton) and the other water molecule<br>
that gains H+ acts as a base. The products of the forward reaction are H3O+ and<br>
<b>OH–. Since the ionisation of water is a reversible process, the products of the</b><br>
forward reaction react in the reverse reaction to form two water molecules. Thus,<br>
the equilibrium is established between the conjugate acid-base pairs of water, a<br>
process called self ionisation of water.<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   304</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:29<br>

<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>

At equilibrium, the equilibrium constant expression for the above ionisation is:<br>

<b>K</b><br>
Considering the concentration of pure water to be constant, Equation 8.3 for the<br>
ionisation constant of water (Kw) is obtained.<br>
<b>Kw = [H3O+][OH–]</b><br>
(8.3)<br>
<b>Experiments have determined that in pure water, the concentrations of H3O+ and</b><br>
<b>OH– at 298 K are 1.0 × 10–7 mol dm–3 each. When the [H3O+] and [OH–] are</b><br>
substituted into Equation 8.3 the numerical value of Kw is obtained.<br>
<b>Kw = 1.0 × 10–7 mol dm–3 × 1.0 × 10–7 mol dm–3 = 1.0 × 10–14 mol2 dm–6</b><br>
Therefore, at 298 K the ionisation constant of water, Kw is 1.0 × 10–14 mol2 dm–6.<br>
<b>The obtained value of Kw is known as the ionic product of water. The value</b><br>
10–14 mol2 dm–6 is a large negative exponent such that its handling is difficult. In<br>
aqueous solutions, [H3O+] can vary over an enormous range from about 10 to<br>
10–14. Thus, for simplicity, the values of [H3O+] are expressed in terms of positive<br>
numerical system called pH-scale which is a negative of the common logarithm<br>
of the concentration of such ions. Applying this numerical system to the [H3O+]<br>
gives pH, the negative logarithm of [H3O+] or [ H+]. Thus,<br>
pH = –log [H3O+] or pH = –log [H+]<br>
(8.4)<br>
<b>A pH-scale is also used to express other quantities as well. Hydroxide ion</b><br>
concentration and ionic product of water can also be expressed as pOH and pKw,<br>
respectively as in Equations 8.5 and 8.6.<br>
<b>Acids, Bases and Salts</b><br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   305</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:29<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five and Six</b><br>
<b>Physical Chemistry</b><br>
for Advanced Level Secondary Schools<br>
306<br>
pOH = –log [OH–]<br>
(8.5)<br>
pKw = –log Kw<br>
(8.6)<br>
It is possible then to take the negative log of both sides of Equation 8.3 and obtain<br>
a very useful relationship of Equation 8.7.<br>
–log Kw = –log [H3O+] + –log [OH–]<br>
or<br>
pKw = pH + pOH<br>
(8.7)<br>
<b>Thus, at 298 K the values of pH and pOH are equal to 7 and that of pKw is equal</b><br>
to 14. The scale can be used to determine acidic or basic strength of an aqueous<br>
solution. Acidic strength increases with decrease in pH value where as the basic<br>
strength increases with an increase in pH value.<br>

<b>Given that the values of Kw at 291 K and 298 K are 0.64 × 10–14 mol2 dm–6 and</b><br>

(b)<br>
The pH of water is greater at 298 K than at 291 K.<br>

(d)<br>
Water is only neutral at 298 K.<br>
<b>Solution</b><br>
(a)<br>
Kw value shows the extent to which a solute has ionised into its ions. The<br>
greater the value of Kw the higher the extent of its ionisation. Comparing<br>
the two values of the Kw given in the question, it can be concluded that<br>
with an increase in temperature from 291 K to 298 K, the Kw value of water<br>
increases from 0.64 × 10–14 mol2 dm–6 to 1.0 × 10–14 mol2 dm–3 showing that<br>
ionisation of water increases with an increase in temperature which is an<br>
endothermic process.<br>
(b)<br>
From Kw = [OH−][H+] and assuming that water has ionised to produce equal<br>
concentrations of H+ and OH− = x, then Kw = x2.<br>

8.3<br>
<b>Buffer solutions</b><br>
<b>The pH of water and most solutions changes drastically when a small amount of</b><br>
acid or base is added. However, some solutions show resistance to pH change<br>
when a small amount of either acid or base is added. Aqueous solution of sodium<br>
chloride and ammonium acetate have a pH of 7 at 298 K. An addition of 1.0<br>
cm3 of 0.1 mol dm−3 HCl to 1.0 dm3 of sodium chloride solution alters the pH<br>
to 4. Similarly, the addition of 1.0 cm3 of 0.1 mol dm−3 NaOH solution to 1.0<br>
dm3 solution of NaCl changes the pH of sodium chloride solution from 7 to 10.<br>
<b>However, the addition of the same amount of HCl and NaOH to a neutral solution</b><br>
of ammonium acetate causes no significant change in pH of the solution. From<br>
these observations, it can be concluded that certain solutions have a tendency<br>
to resist any change in its hydrogen (hydroxonium) ion concentration or pH<br>
whenever a small amount of either a strong acid or a strong base is added to it.<br>
This property of resisting any change in the pH of a solution is known as buffer<br>
action. A solution which resists any change of pH when a small amount of either<br>
strong acid or a strong base is added to it is called a buffer solution.<br>
8.3.1  Types of buffer solutions<br>
Buffer solutions can be categorised as acidic and basic buffers.<br>
<b>Acidic buffers</b><br>
Generally, acidic buffers can be made by dissolving relatively large amounts of a<br>
weak acid and the salt of its conjugate base. Examples, include buffers containing<br>
acetic acid and sodium acetate, formic acid and sodium formate as well as benzoic<br>
acid and sodium benzoate. At 298 K, the pH of acidic buffers is less than 7.<br>
<b>Basic buffers</b><br>
<b>Basic buffers can be made by dissolving relatively large amounts of a weak base</b><br>
and the salt of its conjugate acid. This is exemplified by buffer containing weak<br>
base such as ammonia and the salt of its conjugate acid, ammonium chloride. At<br>
298 K, the pH of the basic buffers is greater than 7.<br>
8.3.2  Preparation of buffer solutions<br>
<b>To prepare either of the two types of the buffer solutions, the following steps</b><br>
must be followed:<br>
(a)<br>
<b>Decide on the chemical composition of the buffer by choosing the conjugate</b><br>
acid-base pair.<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   310</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:30<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
311<br>
(b)<br>
<b>Calculate the ratio of concentrations of buffer components. This is important</b><br>
because the ability of the buffer to resist the pH change (buffer capacity)<br>
is maximum when acid to salt or base to salt ratio is equal to 1. This ratio<br>
occurs when there is equal number of moles of acid (or base) and the salt.<br>
(c)<br>
<b>Determine the buffer concentration by keeping in mind that the higher</b><br>
the concentration of the components, the greater the buffer capacity.<br>
Concentrations of about 0.5 mol dm−3 of each component is suitable.<br>
(d)<br>
<b>Mix the components to make a solution and adjust the pH by adding a</b><br>
strong acid or strong base while monitoring the pH of the solution with a<br>
pH meter.<br>
