8.3.4 	 Mechanism of buffer action<br>
<b>The action of buffer solutions can be explained in terms of Le Châtelier’s</b><br>
Principle. Consider a buffer solution that is made up of acetic acid and its strong<br>
salt, sodium acetate. Being a weak acid, acetic acid ionises partially as per the<br>
equation:<br>
<b>Sodium acetate ionises almost completely to give Na+ and CH3COO− ions. Assume</b><br>
that all the CH3COO− ions present in the solution at equilibrium are from the salt<br>
(CH3COONa). When small amounts of acid such as HCl is added to this solution,<br>
the added H+ ions from HCl will react with CH3COO− ions as represented by the<br>
chemical equation:<br>
This reaction proceeds until all the added H+ ions are removed, and therefore,<br>
the pH of the solution remains unchanged. Similarly, if a small amount of strong<br>
base such as NaOH is added to the buffer solution, the following reaction occurs:<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   314</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:32<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
315<br>
The H+ ions present in the equilibrium mixture react with the added OH−  ions and<br>
thus, shift the equilibrium to the right causing further ionisation of CH3COOH.<br>
<b>The reaction proceeds until all added OH– ions are removed. Consequently, the</b><br>
pH of the solution remains unchanged. In a similar way, the basic buffers operate<br>
to resist the change in the pH. In summary, the presence of significant amounts<br>
of a weak acid and its conjugate base makes buffer solutions resist the pH as<br>
small amounts of either a strong acid or a strong base is added. When an acid is<br>
added to a buffer solution, the conjugate base present in the solution neutralises<br>
the added acid eliminating its effect in the buffer system. In the same way, when<br>
a strong base is added to a buffer solution, the weak acid present in the solution<br>
neutralises the added base thereby eliminating its effect to the buffer system.<br>
Although the mechanism of absorbing OH– and H+ ions is the same for all buffers,<br>
different buffer solutions have different capabilities of resisting pH changes. The<br>
capability of a buffer solution to resist pH change is measured as buffer capacity.<br>
<b>Buffer capacity is defined as the amount of H+ or OH– ions a buffer solution</b><br>
can absorb without significant change in pH. The buffer capacity depends on the<br>
concentration and ratios of the components of the buffer solution. For example,<br>
a buffer that is made up of equal volumes of 1 mol dm–3 NH4OH and 1 mol dm–3<br>
<b>NaOH has the same pH as a buffer solution that is made up of equal volumes of</b><br>
<b>0.1 mol dm−3 NH4OH and 0.1 mol dm–3 NaOH but the former buffer solution has a</b><br>
much larger capacity. This is because, the former buffer is made up of components<br>
of high concentration. When a buffer solution performs its role, the concentration<br>
of one component increases while that of the other decreases. This causes the ratio<br>
of the components to change. Since the ratio of these concentrations determines<br>
the pH, the greater the changes in the ratio the lower the pH changes. The buffer<br>
components of similar concentrations will have less pH change when a small<br>
amount of an acid or a base is added.<br>

8.3.5  Properties of buffer solutions<br>
<b>Buffer solutions have the following properties:</b><br>
(a)<br>
<b>Buffer solutions contain relatively large concentrations of a weak acid or</b><br>
weak base and a conjugate base or conjugate acid, respectively.<br>
(b)<br>
<b>When a small amount of H+ ion is added to a buffer solution, it is neutralised</b><br>
by the weak base present in the solution as shown below:<br>
e.g.,<br>
or<br>

The pH of the buffer solution is determined by the buffering components,<br>

or OH- ions added.<br>
8.3.6  Application of buffer solutions<br>
<b>Buffer solutions play important roles in many industrial processes such as</b><br>
electroplating, manufacturing of dyes, and photographic materials. Many<br>
biological and other systems such as saliva, stomach and blood depend on buffer<br>
actions to preserve a constant pH. Many household and cosmetic products need<br>
to control their pH values. Some instrumental methods like electrophoresis also<br>
depend on buffers for effective operations. Not only that but also plants absorb<br>
nutrients from the soil at certain pH values which must be maintained.<br>

8.4<br>
<b>Salt hydrolysis</b><br>
Salts are ionic compounds.  Some salts are soluble while others are insoluble in<br>
water. Water soluble salts can be completely soluble in a sense that they ionise<br>
completely, or sparingly soluble as they give fractions of the ions in solution.<br>
Such water soluble salts are assumed to break up into their constituent ions, which<br>
move about independently in solution. Salts can be grouped into four categories<br>
depending on the source from which they are made. Some salts are made up of<br>
weak acids and weak bases, others from strong acids and strong bases. Also,<br>
there are salts which are made up of strong acids and weak bases and those which<br>
are made up of weak acid and strong bases. Salts that consist of the cations of<br>
strong bases and the anions of strong acids have no effect on H+ ion produced<br>
by ionisation of water when dissolved in it. This means that aqueous solutions<br>
of salts such as KCl, NaCl, NaNO3, and KNO3 are neutral. However, when some<br>
salts are dissolved in water, they produce acidic solution and others produce basic<br>
solutions. This implies that such salts react with water to produce H+ ions or<br>
OH–ions. Thus, they cause change in pH of the solution. The reaction of salt with<br>
water to form acidic or basic solution is called salt hydrolysis.<br>
8.4.1 	 Behaviour of salts in water<br>
<b>When salt AB dissolves in water, it ionises according to the equation:</b><br>
where, n is the number of charge of the ions.<br>
<b>Therefore, the solution contains only An+, Bn–, and H2O. In order for the pH of</b><br>
the solution to change, An+ or Bn– should react with water by combining with H+<br>
or OH− ions. The reaction will produce either acidic or basic solution depending<br>
on which of the two ions (H+ or OH−) combines with the anion or cation of the<br>
salt. If the ions of the salt have no effect on [H+] or [OH−], the solution produced<br>
is neutral.<br>
<b>Salts that produce basic solutions</b><br>
<b>Aqueous solution of sodium acetate (CH3COONa) is basic (pH > 7). To account</b><br>
for this observation, consider the composition of the solution when the salt<br>
dissolves in water.<br>
Assuming that CH3COONa dissolves completely, the solution consists of Na+,<br>
<b>CH3COO−, and H2O only. The Na+ ion has neither acidic nor basic properties, and</b><br>
therefore it cannot combine with H+ or OH−. The CH3COO− ion is the conjugate<br>
base of CH3COOH, a weak acid. Therefore, it has a significant affinity for a<br>

<b>Salts that produce acidic solution</b><br>
<b>When solid NH4Cl is dissolved in water, NH4</b><br>
+ and Cl− ions are released. The<br>
solution produced contains only NH4<br>
+ ions, Cl− ions, and H2O molecules. As Cl−<br>
ions has no affinity for H+ ions in water, it does not affect the pH of the solution.<br>
<b>Therefore, the observed acidic property of the solution is produced by NH4</b><br>
+ ions.<br>
<b>In water, NH4</b><br>
+ ion behaves as a weak acid by donating a proton to water molecule.<br>
<b>This reaction causes an increase in the concentration of H3O+ or H+ ions, and</b><br>
hence the pH of the solution. In general, a salt whose cation is the conjugate acid<br>
of a weak base reacts with water to produce an acidic solution. Salts that contain<br>
highly charged metal ions (highly charged cations) such as Al3+ and Fe3+ also<br>
produce acidic solution when are dissolved in water. Although these cations are<br>
not Brønsted-Lowry acids, the high charge on the metal ion polarises the O–H<br>
bonds in the coordinated water molecules. This makes the hydrogen atoms in<br>
these water molecules more acidic and can readily be attracted and taken away<br>
by water molecules present in the solution.<br>
This causes an increase in [H3O+] of the solution making it acidic.<br>
<b>Salts from weak bases and weak acids</b><br>
<b>Ammonium cyanide (NH4CN) is an example of a salt that is made from a weak</b><br>
base NH3 and weak acid HCN. In water, it ionises to produce NH4<br>
+ and CN− ions.<br>
<b>The produced NH4</b><br>
+ and CN− ions react with water as:<br>
The ions H3O+ and OH− react immediately to produce H2O.<br>
The resulting solution can be either acidic, basic or neutral depending on the extent<br>
to which NH4+ and CN− ions have been hydrolysed. If NH4+ ion hydrolyses<br>
more than CN− ion, the solution will be acidic, and if CN− ion hydrolyses more<br>
than NH4+ ion the solution will be basic. If both ions hydrolyse at equal extent<br>
the solution will be neutral.<br>

8.4.2	 Hydrolysis constant, degree of hydrolysis, and pH of salt<br>
solutions<br>
From the preceding sub-section, it is possible to qualitatively predict the nature<br>
of the solution which is obtained when a salt of a strong acid and weak base or a<br>
salt of a weak acid and strong base is dissolved in water. However, to determine<br>
how much acidic or basic the solution has become as a result of hydrolysis of<br>
the salt is difficult, especially when the solution is obtained by dissolving a salt<br>
of weak acid and weak base. This is because the cations and anions of the salt<br>
hydrolyses to furnish H3O+ and OH–  ions in the solution, but to judge which of<br>
the two is predominant it becomes difficult. To solve this problem, you need to<br>
consider quantitative aspect of salts hydrolysis by introducing the concepts of<br>
hydrolysis constant, degree of hydrolysis, and pH of the salt solution.<br>

corresponding weak acid. Consider the hydrolysis of salt of weak acid and strong<br>

<b>Example 8.12</b><br>
<b>Common salt (NaCl), sulphate of ammonia ((NH4)2SO4) and baking powder</b><br>
(NaHCO3) are frequently used in our life. Predict the nature of their aqueous<br>
solutions as acidic, basic, or neutral. Give reasons to support your answer.<br>
<b>Solution</b><br>
<b>When NaCl is dissolved in water, it ionises completely to give Na+ and Cl–</b><br>
ions. The resulting solution contains only Na+, Cl− ions, and H2O molecules.<br>
Since Na+ ion has no affinity to OH− ions and Cl−  has no affinity to H+ ions, the<br>
concentration of H+ and OH− ions in the solution remain unaffected. Thus, the<br>
solution is neutral. Conversely, when (NH4)2SO4 is dissolved in water, it ionises<br>
to produce NH4<br>
+ and SO4<br>
2−. The solution therefore, contains only NH4<br>
<b>+, SO4</b><br>
2−<br>
ions , and H2O molecules. The SO4<br>

has no affinity to H+ ions. In this regard, its presence in the solution has no effect<br>
on [H+]. However, NH4<br>
+ ion, a conjugate acid of the weak base reacts with water<br>
molecules according to the chemical reaction:<br>
<b>This causes an increase in the concentration of H3O+ and thus, the solution is</b><br>
acidic.<br>
<b>Sodium hydrogen carbonate, NaHCO3, dissolves in water to produce Na+ and</b><br>
<b>HCO3</b><br>
−. The solution therefore, contains Na+, HCO3<br>
− ions, and H2O molecules.<br>
But Na+ has no affinity to OH− and H+ ions. The effect on the pH of the solution is<br>

<b>Solubility an</b><br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
<b>Based on the extent to which the chemical substances dissolve in water, they</b><br>
can be termed as soluble, sparingly soluble or insoluble. Solubility plays an<br>
important role in science and nature, ranging from water purification, making<br>
drinks, storage of vitamins, nutrients uptake and translocation by plants as well as<br>
drugs administrations to patients. In this chapter, you will learn about the concept<br>
of solubility, factors affecting solubility, and the equilibrium of sparingly soluble<br>
ionic compounds. In addition, you will learn about factors affecting solubility<br>
products, ionic product, and the prediction of precipitation of electrolytes.<br>
9.1<br>
<b>The concept of solubility</b><br>
<b>A solute-solvent interaction results into a number of chemical and physical</b><br>
processes that are of interest. When a solute is dissolved in a solvent, three<br>
processes occur that determine whether a substance is soluble, sparingly soluble<br>
or insoluble in a solvent (Figure 9.1). One of these processes (Process 1) is the<br>
solute expansion caused by interaction between the solute and solvent leading to a<br>
separation of solute into its components. Another process (Process 2) which occurs<br>
simultaneously with Process 1, is solvent expansion which leads to the creation of<br>
a room for the solute components to mix with the solvent. During these processes,<br>
the intermolecular forces of attraction between the solvent and solute are overcome.<br>
In the third process (Process 3), the expanded solute and solvent interact to form a<br>
solution. Figure 9.1 shows the three steps for the formation of solution.<br>
<b>Figure 9.1 The solution formation process</b><br>
<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Nine</b><br>
<b>Solubility and solubility</b><br>
products<br>
<b>Solvent</b><br>
<b>Solute</b><br>
<b>Expanded solute</b><br>
∆H1 + ∆H2 + ∆H3 = ∆Hsol<br>
<b>Expanded solvent</b><br>
<b>Solution</b><br>
<b>Process 1</b><br>
<b>∆H1</b><br>
<b>Process 2</b><br>
<b>∆H2</b><br>
<b>Process 3</b><br>
<b>∆H3</b><br>

<b>Steps 1 and 2 in Figure 9.1 are energy consuming processes (endothermic) as</b><br>
energy must be supplied to break the intermolecular forces existing between<br>
individual solvent and solute molecules whereas step 3 is the energy releasing<br>
process (exothermic). The overall change in energy (enthalpy or heat of solution,<br>
<b>ΔHsol) is the sum of enthalpy values:</b><br>
sol<br>
1<br>
2<br>
3<br>
<b>Δ</b><br>

ΔHsol can have a positive or negative sign depending on the difference between the<br>
sum of energies in step 1 and 2 and that of step 3. When the value of the enthalpy<br>
of solution is negative, energy is released while when it is positive, energy is<br>
absorbed. It is the value of ΔHsol which determines whether a solute is soluble,<br>
sparingly soluble or insoluble. Intramolecular and intermolecular attractions<br>
within and between a solute and a solvent are other factors for the dissolution of<br>
a solute in a solvent. For a solute to dissolve in a solvent, the bond between the<br>
solute and the solvent must be stronger than the bond between individual solute<br>
or solvent molecules.<br>
A given volume of a solvent has its expansion ability that enables the solute to<br>
interact with the solvent. Below the expansion capacity of a solvent, a solute<br>
dissolves in a solvent allowing mixing of solute and solvent molecules to form a<br>
solution. Above the expansion capacity, the solvent becomes saturated such that<br>
it dissolves no more solute.<br>
<b>Solubility of sugar in water has a limit below which one cannot dissolve any</b><br>
amount of sugar in a cup of tea. The extent to which a solute can dissolve in a<br>
given amount of solvent is known as solubility. Solubility of a solute is expressed<br>
as the maximum mass in grams of a solute which can dissolve in 100 g of the<br>
solvent to form a saturated solution. It is also expressed as the maximum amount<br>
in moles of solute which can dissolve in one kilogram of a solvent to make a<br>
saturated solution.<br>
In chemistry, solubility is a qualitative property of substances which is used to<br>
classify substances as soluble or insoluble. However, “soluble” or “insoluble” are<br>
relative terms as they depend on the conditions to which a chemical substance<br>
is subjected. No chemical substance is unconditionally soluble or insoluble.<br>
<b>For example, when 50 g of magnesium carbonate (MgCO3) are added to 100</b><br>
g of water at room temperature, only 0.06 g of the salt dissolves. Since most of<br>
<b>MgCO3 remain unchanged, it is said to be insoluble in water. Another example</b><br>
is dissolution of 50 g of hydrated magnesium sulphate (MgSO4•7H2O) in 100<br>
g of water at room temperature. Under these conditions, only 13.6 g of the salt<br>
remain unchanged. Since 36.4 g dissolve, MgSO4•7H2O is regarded as soluble in<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   336</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:39<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
337<br>
water. Salts which are considered to be completely insoluble in water are actually<br>
not. For example, lead(II) carbonate is regarded as highly insoluble in water but<br>
experiments show that 0.000017 g of the salt dissolves in 100 g of water at room<br>
temperature. Therefore, the solubility of ionic solid may be considered to be<br>
an equilibrium process between solids and ions in solution and is affected by a<br>
number of factors. Thus, a correct definition of solubility of a chemical substance<br>
is a maximum mass in grams or number of moles of that substance which can<br>
dissolve in a known mass of a solvent at a given temperature and pressure.<br>
9.2<br>
<b>Factors affecting solubility</b><br>
Solubility of a solute in any given solvent is affected by several factors such as<br>
pressure, type of solvent, temperature, purity of solvent (presence of common<br>
ion), pH of the solution, and complex ion formation.<br>
(a) 	 The effects of pressure<br>
<b>Pressure affects the solubility of gases in liquid solvent but not of dissolved</b><br>
solids. When a pressure is applied to a gas that is above the surface of a solvent,<br>
the gas will dissolve in the solvent and occupy some of the spaces between the<br>
particles of the solvent. When the pressure is decreased, the solubility of the<br>
gas is also decreased. This can be demonstrated by opening a bottle of coca-<br>
cola or any carbonated beverage. The pressure of the gas is lowered when the<br>
stopper is removed and the dissolved gas immediately starts leaving the solution<br>
as effervescence (fizzing). The quantitative relationship between gas pressure<br>
and solubility is expressed by the Henry’s Law which states that the solubility<br>
of a gas in aqueous solution is directly proportional to the partial pressure of the<br>
gas above the solution, i.e. C = kPgas where, C is the gas solubility (mol dm–3),<br>
k is Henry’s gas constant, and P is the partial pressure of the gas above the gas<br>
soulution.<br>
(b) 	 The effect of solvent<br>
<b>The amount of the solute in solid form which dissolves in a given amount of a</b><br>
solvent depends on the type or nature of solute and solvent. Solubility is favoured<br>
if the solute and solvent have similar properties, i.e., “like dissolves like”. Thus,<br>
polar solvents dissolve polar solutes and non-polar solvents dissolve non-polar<br>
solutes. For example, fat-soluble vitamins (A, D, E, and K) cannot dissolve in<br>
water and water-soluble vitamin B and C cannot dissolve in fat. This explains<br>
why people are advised to frequently take fruits containing vitamin B and C<br>
because the body can not store them.<br>
<b>Solubility and solubility products</b><br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   337</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:39<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five and Six</b><br>
<b>Physical Chemistry</b><br>
for Advanced Level Secondary Schools<br>
338<br>
(c) 	 The effect of temperature<br>
<b>Solubility of solutes is dependent on temperature because when solids dissolve</b><br>
in a solvent, a change in their physical state similar to melting takes place. The<br>
process starts by breaking the bonds holding ions or molecules together. This is<br>
an endothermic process. Then, the separated particles (ions or molecules) are<br>
hydrated to form a solute-solvent bond which is an exothermic process.  If the<br>
heat given off during hydration (dissolving) is greater than the heat required to<br>
break the solid apart into its constituent particles, the net reaction for dissolving<br>
the solid is exothermic. Hence, solubility is favoured by a decrease in temperature.<br>
<b>A good example of a substance whose solubility decreases with an increase</b><br>
in temperature is Ce2SO4. Similarly, if the heat given off during dissolution<br>
(hydration) is less than the heat required to break apart solid particles, the net<br>
dissolving process is endothermic. Hence, the solubility of the solute is favoured<br>
by the increase in temperature. This is the most common situation. However, the<br>
solubility of gases decreases with the increase in temperature. The higher kinetic<br>
energy in gases as the result of increased temperatures causes more motion of<br>
molecules which breaks intermolecular bonds and sets them free to escape from<br>
the solution.<br>
9.3<br>
<b>Equilibrium of sparingly soluble ionic compounds</b><br>
<b>Sparingly soluble substances have extremely small amounts of ions present in</b><br>
the saturated solution. A solution containing a maximum amount of solute at any<br>
fixed temperature and pressure is called a saturated solution. A solution is said to<br>
be saturated if no more of the solute can dissolve in a solvent. A saturated solution<br>
therefore, cannot dissolve any more solute. Upon adding a small quantity of the<br>
solute to a saturated solution, dissolution does not take place and the solute added<br>
settles down. When a saturated solution is in contact with the solid solute, there<br>
exists a dynamic equilibrium between the solid and the solution phases.<br>
<b>Solution of the substance</b><br>
<b>Solid substance</b><br>

The term Ksp in Equation 9.2 is called the solubility product or solubility constant.<br>
<b>Generally, solubility product, Ksp can be defined as the product of the molar</b><br>

The value of Ksp indicates the solubility of an ionic compound in a solvent. The<br>

<b>Fe(OH)2. Depending on the values of x and y in the ionic compounds, Ksp values</b><br>

(9.5)<br>
The solubility product is a special form of an equilibrium constant. It varies with<br>
changes in temperature. Thus, the temperature at which a solubility product is<br>
measured must always be indicated. The Ksp is not affected by any other factor<br>
such as pressure and concentration.<br>
To assign units for the Ksp value of any sparingly soluble electrolyte, you need<br>
to know the determining factors upon which the units of Ksp depend. This can be<br>
done by considering the equilibrium which is established in a saturated solution<br>
of a substance of a general formula AxBy.<br>
It can be deduced that the units of Ksp depend on the units of the concentrations<br>
of A and B and the coefficients x and y. Since the concentration of the salt and<br>

Solubility and solubility product are interdependent quantities. Their quantitative<br>
relationship can be used to calculate the amount of solute the solvent can dissolve.<br>

the saturated solution. For example, for the dissolution of AgCl the solubility<br>
equilibrium is:<br>
The Ksp expression is formulated for ionic species appearing in the equation for<br>

soluble solute. The coefficients for the ions in solution are whatever is needed to<br>
balance the equation. The coefficients then establish the powers to which the ion<br>
concentrations are raised in the Ksp expression.<br>

Each weak electrolyte has its own value of Ksp at a given temperature.<br>

9.4<br>
<b>Factors affecting solubility products</b><br>
Solubility product is affected by the common ion, pH and complex formation.<br>
<b>The effect of common ion</b><br>
<b>If a solvent in which the solute has to be dissolved contains one of the ions</b><br>
produced by the solute upon dissolution, the amount of the solute that can be<br>
dissolved is less than the amount of the solute dissolved by a pure solvent. The<br>
common ion effect, is the decrease in solubility of a substance due to the presence<br>
of a common ion.<br>
If a salt is added to the solvent in which one of its ion is already present, the<br>
position of equilibrium shifts to the direction which tends to offset the increase in<br>
the concentration of that ion (Le Châtelier’s Principle).<br>

<b>The effect of pH</b><br>
If a compound contains anions of a weak acid, the addition of H3O+ (from a strong<br>
acid) increases its solubility, because it shifts the equilibrium to the right hand<br>
side of the equation. Consider a reaction represented by the chemical equation:<br>
When H3O+ is added to the system at equilibrium (pH is lowered), it reacts with a<br>
<b>CO3</b><br>
2– according to the following chemical equation:<br>
<b>This reaction lowers the concentration of CO3</b><br>
2− ion and thus, causes the equilibrium<br>
to shift to the right increasing the solubility of CaCO3.<br>

right causing PbCl2 to dissolve more.<br>

9.5<br>
<b>Ionic products</b><br>
<b>In section 9.3, the solubility product, Ksp, was derived and defined as the</b><br>
ionic product in their saturated solution each raised to the power equal to the<br>
stoichiomatric coefficient. In this case, the ions are in contract with the solid<br>
substance because the solution is saturated. If the solution is not saturated, the<br>
ionic product is not equal to Ksp but equal to another value, Qsp.<br>
For example, when a sparingly soluble salt AB dissolves in water, it dissociates<br>
according to the reaction:<br>
At saturation point,<br>
<b>K</b><br>
]<br>

<b>The value of Qsp is called ionic product and is defined as the product of the</b><br>
concentration of the component ions of the sparingly soluble substance each<br>
raised to its power of the stoichiometric coefficients of ions. It follows that if:<br>
Qsp &lt; Ksp, the solution is unsaturated.<br>
Qsp = Ksp, the solution is saturated.<br>
Qsp &gt; Ksp, the solution is supersaturated.<br>
When Qsp &gt; Ksp, the salt AB will precipitate until the product of [A+] and [B–] is<br>
equal to the value of Ksp at that particular temperature.<br>
Although ionic product and solubility product are similar because both are ionic<br>
products they also differ because concentrations of the ions are different. Their<br>

9.6<br>
<b>Prediction of precipitation of electrolytes</b><br>
The knowledge of whether the solution is saturated, unsaturated or supersaturated,<br>
Ksp and Qsp is used to predict the precipitation of salts by comparing the values of<br>
these physical quantities. Suppose a dilute solution of barium chloride is mixed<br>
with a very dilute solution of sodium sulphate, how will you know whether<br>
precipitation of barium sulphate will occur or not? The solubility product<br>
expression for the dissolution reaction of the salt is:<br>
<b>Its Ksp is given by:</b><br>
+<br>
sp<br>
<b>K</b><br>
[Ba ][SO<br>
]<br>
=<br>
2<br>
2<br>
4<br>
At 298 K, Ksp of BaSO4 is 1.5 × 10−9 mol2 dm−6. If the product of the concentrations<br>
of Ba2+ and SO4<br>
2− ions in the two solutions mixed together exceeds the solubility<br>
product, then precipitation occurs until the product of the concentrations has been<br>
reduced to the solubility product. If the product of the concentrations of the two<br>
species is less than the solubility product, precipitation does not occur. In this<br>
case, the equilibrium lies completely to the right. This can be expressed as: if<br>
Qsp &gt; Ksp precipitation occurs until Qsp = Ksp, but, if Qsp &lt; Ksp precipitation does<br>
not occur.<br>

<b>Example 9.6</b><br>
Will the precipitate of silver iodide form if a solution containing 5.8 × 10−10 mol<br>
dm−3 silver nitrate is mixed with another solution containing 4.6 × 10−7 mol dm−3<br>
of sodium iodide? Given that the Ksp value for AgI at 298 K is 1.5 × 10−16 mol2<br>
dm−6.<br>
<b>Solution</b><br>
<b>The reaction equation for dissolution of AgI is</b><br>
Assuming that AgNO3 and NaI dissociate completely according to the equations;<br>
then, the number of moles of silver and iodide ions are equal to the number of<br>
moles of their corresponding salts. Therefore, the solutions contain 5.8 × 10−10<br>
mol dm−3 of Ag+ ions and 4.6 × 10−7 mol dm−3 of I– ions. The expression for the<br>
ionic product of silver iodide is:<br>
]<br>
<b>] [I</b><br>
[Ag<br>
sp<br>
+<br>
=<br>
<b>Q</b><br>
<b>Substituting the values of [Ag+] and [I–] into the Qsp expression gives:</b><br>
<b>Qsp = 5.8 × 10−10 mol dm−3 × 4.6 × 10−7 mol dm−3 = 2.7 × 10−16 mol2 dm−6</b><br>
<b>To decide whether the precipitation of AgI will occur, you have to compare the</b><br>
Ksp and Qsp values. In this case, the ionic product (Qsp = 2.7 × 10−16 mol2 dm−6) is<br>
greater than the solubility product, (Ksp = 1.5 × 10−16 mol2 dm−6) and therefore, the<br>
precipitation of AgI will occur.<br>
It is important to note that the ability to predict precipitation of a salt using the<br>
knowledge of solubility product and ionic product often has a practical value.<br>
<b>For example, in industrial and laboratory preparations, the concentrations of</b><br>
ions can be adjusted until the ionic product, Qsp exceeds Ksp in order to obtain a<br>
given compound in the form of a precipitate. This technique is used in analytical<br>
chemistry to precipitate sulphides, hydroxides and carbonates.<br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   348</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:44<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
349<br>
<b>Precipitation of sulphides</b><br>
<b>Certain metals are precipitated as sulphides when hydrogen sulphide is</b><br>
bubbled through the solution. These include group II cations of the qualitative<br>
analysis such as Pb2+, Cu2+, Hg2+, Cd2+, Al3+ and Sn2+, and group IV cations of<br>
the qualitative analysis such as Co2+, Ni2+, Mn2+, and Zn2+. Group II sulphides<br>
include such sulphides as PbS, CuS and CdS which have very low solubility<br>
products. Therefore, they are precipitated even at low concentrations of sulphide<br>
ion. Group IV sulphides such as ZnS, MnS, NiS and CoS have relatively higher<br>
solubility products and hence, they are precipitated only at higher sulphide ion<br>
concentration. For a proper analysis, therefore, experimental conditions must be<br>
maintained for selective precipitation of the required metal cations present in<br>
the solution. For example, in order for a metal ion, M2+ to be precipitated as a<br>
sulphide, the following condition must be fulfilled:<br>
[M2+][S2–] &gt; Ksp<br>
Thus, if [S2–] is low, only those metals whose sulphides have low Ksp values will<br>
be precipitated. The concentration of S2– ion in solution depends on pH of the<br>
solution. This is because hydrogen sulphide is a weak acid which in aqueous<br>
solution it ionises reversibly according to the reaction:<br>
When an acid is added to the solution, this equilibrium shifts to the left. As a result,<br>
the concentration of sulphide ion is lowered. Therefore, group II cations will<br>
be selectively precipitated. When the solution is made alkaline, the equilibrium<br>
shifts to the right. As a result, the concentration of sulphide ions increases.<br>
<b>Precipitation of hydroxides</b><br>
In aqueous alkaline condition, group III cations of the qualitative analysis (Fe3+,<br>
Al3+ and Cr3+) are identified by precipitating their metal hydroxides. To prevent<br>
the precipitation of hydroxides of group IV cations (Zn2+, Mn2+, Ni2+ and Mg2+),<br>
the concentration of OH– ions should be kept low. This is achieved by first<br>
adding sufficient amount of ammonium chloride (NH4Cl) into the solution before<br>
precipitating metal hydroxides using ammonium hydroxide (NH4OH). NH4Cl<br>
furnishes NH4<br>
+ ions, which due to common ion effect suppresses the ionisation<br>
of NH4OH, thereby keeping the concentration of OH– low.<br>
<b>Solubility and solubility products</b><br>
<b>PHYSICAL CHEMISTRY FORM FIVE AND SIX.indd   349</b><br>
09/11/2019   09:44<br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>GOVERNMENT PROPERTY</b><br>
<b>FOR ONLINE USE ONLY</b><br>
<b>DO NOT DUPLICATE</b><br>
<b>Student’s Book Form Five and Six</b><br>
<b>Physical Chemistry</b><br>
for Advanced Level Secondary Schools<br>
350<br>
<b>All other metal hydroxides like Zn(OH)2, Mn(OH)2, Ni(OH)2 and Mg(OH)2 have</b><br>
higher solubility products than group III metal hydroxides and hence, they are<br>
not precipitated at low OH− concentration.<br>
<b>Precipitation of carbonates</b><br>
Group V cations of the qualitative  analysis (Ba2+, Sr2+, and Ca2+) are precipitated<br>
as carbonates by adding ammonium carbonate ((NH4)2CO3) in the presence of<br>
ammonium chloride (NH4Cl) and ammonium hydroxide (NH4OH). The presence<br>
of NH4Cl suppresses the ionisation of NH4OH due to common ion effect. This<br>
keeps the concentration of OH– low and thus, does not permit the precipitation<br>
of Mg(OH)2. The NH4<br>
+ ions from NH4Cl also suppress the ionisation of<br>
ammonium carbonate and thus, keep the concentration of CO3<br>
2− low to prevent<br>
the precipitation of MgCO3. The concentration of CO3<br>
2−, therefore, is sufficient to<br>
cause the precipitation of the carbonates of Ba2+, Sr2+, and Ca2+.<br>

<b>Glossary</b><br>
<b>Acid dissociation constant</b><br>
<b>An equilibrium constant of a weak acid which</b><br>
is expressed as the ratio of the product of the<br>
ionic concentration of the acid radical and the<br>
concentration of hydrogen ions raised to their<br>
stoichiometric coefficients to the concentration<br>
of<br>
undissociated<br>
molecule<br>
at<br>
constant<br>
temperature<br>
<b>Acid-base indicators</b><br>
<b>Substances which possess different colours at</b><br>
different hydrogen ion concentrations or pH of<br>
solution<br>
<b>Ammeter</b><br>
<b>A device for measuring the rate at which the</b><br>
charge is transferred through a circuit<br>
<b>Amphoteric substances</b><br>
are substances which show both acidic and<br>
basic properties<br>
<b>Anionic complexes</b><br>
<b>Are complexes that have a net negative charge</b><br>
outside the coordination sphere<br>
<b>Anode</b><br>
An electrode to which oxidation reaction occurs.<br>
<b>Aqueous solution</b><br>
<b>A solution in which water is a solvent</b><br>
<b>Atomisation energy</b><br>
the heat required to convert one mole of an<br>
element into gaseous atoms<br>
<b>Azeotropic mixture</b><br>
<b>A mixture of liquids that boil at constant</b><br>
temperature, behaves like a pure liquid and<br>
whose composition in the liquid phase is the<br>
same as in the vapour phase<br>
<b>Bimolecular reaction</b><br>
<b>An elementary step in which two molecules are</b><br>

<b>Boiling point</b><br>
<b>The temperature at which the vapour pressure</b><br>
of a substance  is equal to the atmospheric<br>

<b>Boiling point elevation</b><br>
<b>The difference between the boiling point of a</b><br>

<b>Bond energy</b><br>
<b>The average amount of energy which is required</b><br>
to break one mole of bonds of a particular type<br>
in gaseous covalent molecules<br>

<b>At constant temperature, the volume of a fixed</b><br>

<b>A solution which resists a change in pH when</b><br>

<b>Chemical Equivalent</b><br>
<b>The formula weight of a substance divided by</b><br>

<b>Chemical kinetics</b><br>

<b>Chromatography</b><br>

<b>Colligative properties</b><br>

<b>Column chromatography</b><br>
<b>A separation technique which uses a solid</b><br>

<b>Ideal solution</b><br>

<b>Immiscible liquids</b><br>

<b>Ionisation energy</b><br>

<b>Isotonic solutions</b><br>

<b>Kohlrausch’s Law</b><br>
<b>The molar conductivity of an electrolyte at</b><br>

<b>Lattice energy</b><br>
<b>The energy released during the formation of</b><br>

at standard conditions or the energy required to<br>
separate one mole of an ionic solid into gaseous<br>
ions at standard conditions<br>
<b>Law of mass action</b><br>
<b>The rate of chemical reaction is proportional to</b><br>
the active mass of the reacting substances.<br>
<b>Le Châtelier’s Principle</b><br>
<b>If a system is in equilibrium, any change imposed</b><br>
on the system tends to shift the equilibrium so<br>

<b>Lowering of vapour pressure	 The difference between vapour pressure of pure</b><br>

<b>Miscible liquids</b><br>
<b>Liquids which dissolve completely in one</b><br>

<b>Molality</b><br>
<b>The number of moles of a solute dissolved in 1</b><br>

<b>Molar conductivity</b><br>

<b>Molar gas volume</b><br>
<b>The volume occupied by one mole of any gas at</b><br>

<b>Molecularity of the reaction</b><br>
<b>The number of molecules (species) reacting in</b><br>
an elementary step<br>
<b>Mole fraction</b><br>
<b>The ratio of the number of moles of a given</b><br>

<b>Non-ideal solution</b><br>
<b>A solution that is made from completely</b><br>

attractions are different from the intermolecular<br>
forces of the liquid components that form the<br>
solution<br>

<b>Unimolecular reaction</b><br>

van’t Hoff – Avogadro’s Law	 Two solutions with equal concentrations of<br>
different solutes exert the same osmotic pressure<br>
at the same temperature<br>
van’t Hoff – Boyle’s Law<br>
<b>The osmotic pressure (π) of a dilute solution is</b><br>

(M) or inversely proportional to the volume of<br>
the solution, provided that temperatures remain<br>
constant<br>
van’t Hoff-Charles’  Law<br>
<b>The osmotic pressure of a dilute solution is</b><br>
directly proportional to the absolute temperature<br>
provided that the concentration remains constant<br>
van’t Hoff’s factor<br>
<b>The ratio of number of particles produced as</b><br>
the result of dissociation to the number of non-<br>
dissociated particles<br>
van’t Hoff’s Law of osmotic pressure<br>
<b>The magnitude of osmotic pressure</b><br>
is proportional to the number of solute particles<br>
present in a given volume of solution<br>
<b>Vapour density</b><br>
<b>The ratio between the mass of a certain volume</b><br>
of the gas to the mass of the same volume of<br>
hydrogen gas at the same temperature and<br>
pressure<br>
